Circular Reasoning in Third and Fourth Figures
Analysis of circular reasoning restrictions in third and fourth figures.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine when circular reasoning is possible in the third and fourth figures of categorical syllogisms.

Core Idea
Circular reasoning fails in the third figure and the fourth when the major is negative, but succeeds when the minor is negative.

In the previous lesson, we saw how circular reasoning works in the first and second figures. Now we turn to the third and fourth figures, where the conditions are more restrictive. The author explains why circularity is generally impossible here, except in a specific case when the minor premise is negative. Understanding these restrictions is crucial for verifying the soundness of logical proofs in Islamic sciences, where circular arguments are invalid.

Guided Path
1
Identify the figure
Determine whether the syllogism is in the third or fourth figure based on the position of the middle term. In the third figure, the middle term is the subject of both premises; in the fourth, it is the predicate of the major and subject of the minor.
Refer to standard definitions of figures.
2
Check the major premise's quality
If the syllogism is in the fourth figure and the major premise is negative, circular reasoning is impossible. If the minor is negative, a negative conclusion can be derived from an affirmative, but not vice versa.
3
Analyze the examples
Study the two examples given. The first shows how converting the major and using the conclusion as a new premise yields the original minor. The second uses a modified conversion to derive the original major. Notice the role of the second figure in these derivations.
The author uses the first mood of the second figure.
4
Generalize the condition
Formulate a rule: in the third figure, circularity is never possible for affirmative conclusions; in the fourth, only when the minor premise is negative can a negative be derived from an affirmative.
This mirrors the earlier discussion on affirmatives.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفن الرابع: في قياس الدور وعكس القياس. وأما إن كان من الشكل الثالث فليس يتهيأ منه دور أيضاً؛ لما أسلفناه في الموجبات، وكذا إن كان من الرابع والكبرى فيه هي السالبة، بل إن كانت الصغرى هي السالبة أمكن استنتاج السالبة من الموجبة، وأما استنتاج الموجبة من السالبة فليس إلا على نوع من التخيل كما أسلفنا. منه المقدمة مثال الأول ما لو قيل: لا شيء من الإنسان فرس، وكل ضاحك إنسان، فلا شيء من الفرس ضاحك، فإنك إذا جعلت النتيجة كبرى لعكس الكبرى من غير تغيير كمية فقلت: كل إنسان ضاحك، ولا شيء من الفرس ضاحك، أنتج من الاقتران الأول من الشكل الثاني: لا شيء من الإنسان فرس، وهو عين الصغرى. ومثال الثاني ما لو قيل: لا شيء من الحادث قديم، وكل معلول حادث، فلا شيء من القديم معلول، فإنك إذا أخذت عكس النتيجة من غير تغيير كمية وجعلها معدولة صغرى للازم المقدمة السالبة فقلت: كل معلول فهو غير قديم، وكل ما هو غير قديم حادث، أنتج: كل معلول حادث، وهو عين المقدمة الكبرى. وأما استنتاج الجزئية من الكلية فممكن والاقتران إذ ذاك إما موجبتان، أو إن إحداهما سالبة، فإن كانتا من موجبتين فإما من الشكل الأول، أو الثاني أو الثالث أو الرابع، فإن كان من الأول فاقتران عين النتيجة بعكس الكبرى من غير تغيير كمية ينتج عين المقدمة الصغرى، وهو كما قيل: بعض الحيوان ضاحك، وكل ضاحك كاتب، فبعض الحيوان كاتب، فقلت: بعض الحيوان كاتب، وكل كاتب ضاحك، فإنه ينتج بعض الحيوان ضاحك، وهو عين المقدمة الصغرى. وإن كان من الشكل الثاني من المختلطات فليس يتهيأ منه دور لما سلف في الكليات، وإن كانت من الشكل الثالث فاقتران النتيجة بعكس المقدمة الكلية ينتج عكس الجزئية، وبالعكس.
The fourth art: On circular syllogism and conversion of syllogism. As for if it is from the third figure, a circular (syllogism) is not possible from it either, due to what we previously mentioned regarding affirmatives. Similarly if from the fourth figure and the major premise in it is negative. Rather, if the minor premise is negative, it is possible to derive the negative from the affirmative. As for deriving the affirmative from the negative, it is only in a kind of imagination as we previously mentioned. From it the premise: Example of the first: as if it is said: 'Nothing from human is horse' and 'Every laughing is human', so 'Nothing from horse is laughing'. For if you make the conclusion the major premise for the conversion of the major premise without changing quantity, saying: 'Every human is laughing' and 'Nothing from horse is laughing', it produces from the first mood of the second figure: 'Nothing from human is horse', which is the very same minor premise. And example of the second: as if it is said: 'Nothing from the originated is eternal' and 'Everything caused is originated', so 'Nothing from the eternal is caused'. For if you take the conversion of the conclusion without changing quantity and make it modified as the minor premise for the necessary concomitant of the negative premise, saying: 'Everything caused is not eternal' and 'Everything that is not eternal is originated', it produces: 'Everything caused is originated', which is the very same major premise. As for deriving the particular from the universal, it is possible, and the conjunction then is either two affirmatives or one of them is negative. If they are two affirmatives, then either from the first figure, or the second, or the third, or the fourth. If from the first, conjoining the very same conclusion with the conversion of the major premise without changing quantity produces the very same minor premise, as is said: 'Some animal is laughing' and 'Every laughing is writer', so 'Some animal is writer'. Then you said: 'Some animal is writer' and 'Every writer is laughing', it produces 'Some animal is laughing', which is the very same minor premise. If from the second figure of the mixed (syllogisms), then a circular (syllogism) is not possible due to what preceded regarding universals. If from the third figure, conjoining the conclusion with the conversion of the universal premise produces the conversion of the particular, and vice versa.

Commentary
This passage is a continuation of the discussion on circular reasoning in syllogisms, now focusing on the third and fourth figures. The author states that circular syllogisms are not possible in the third figure for the same reason as with affirmative conclusions, and similarly for the fourth figure when the major premise is negative. However, if the minor premise is negative, a negative can be derived from an affirmative, but deriving an affirmative from a negative is only imaginary. Two examples are given for the second figure (note: the text says 'first mood of the second figure', not third or fourth, but it illustrates circularity from a different perspective). The first example shows deriving the minor premise by making the conclusion the major for the converted major. The second example uses a modified conversion to derive the major premise. The author then turns to deriving particular conclusions from universal ones. He explains that this is possible in various figures with two affirmative premises. In the first figure, conjoining the conclusion with the converted major yields the minor premise. In the second figure (mixed), circularity is impossible. In the third figure, combining the conclusion with the conversion of the universal premise yields the conversion of the particular, and vice versa.

Key Takeaway
Circular reasoning in the third and fourth figures is highly restricted; it fails in the third figure and in the fourth when the major is negative, succeeding only when the minor is negative to derive a negative from an affirmative.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a categorical syllogism in the third figure on an Islamic topic (e.g., all prophets are truthful, all prophets are human). Then attempt to derive the minor premise circularly and explain why it fails according to the author's rule.
?
Given the following syllogism: 'No created thing is eternal, and every soul is created, therefore no soul is eternal.' Identify whether it is in the fourth figure, check the major premise quality, and determine if circular reasoning is possible.

Deriving Particular Premises from Universal Ones
Method for deriving particular premises from universal ones in various figures.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to derive a particular premise from a universal premise in categorical syllogisms of the first, second, third, and fourth figures.

Core Idea
Deriving a particular from a universal is possible in all four figures when both premises are affirmative.

After discussing circular reasoning, the author turns to another important operation: deriving a particular premise from a universal one. This technique is useful for expanding a deductive argument or testing its validity. The author explains that it is possible in all four figures when both premises are affirmative, though the second figure (mixed) is an exception. Understanding this helps in constructing valid arguments in Islamic legal reasoning, where moving from general principles to specific cases is common.

Guided Path
1
Identify the type of premises
Check whether the premises are both affirmative (universal and particular) or one is negative. The author focuses on the case of two affirmative premises.
2
Determine the figure
The procedure varies by figure. For the first figure, conjoin the conclusion with the conversion of the major premise (without changing quantity) to obtain the minor premise. For the second figure (mixed), circularity is impossible. For the third figure, combining the conclusion with the conversion of the universal premise yields the conversion of the particular, and vice versa.
The fourth figure is not explicitly detailed but is implied to follow similar rules.
3
Work through the first figure example
Example: 'Some animal is laughing' and 'Every laughing is writer' gives 'Some animal is writer'. Conjoining the conclusion with the converted major ('Every writer is laughing') yields the original minor 'Some animal is laughing'.
The conversion here is simple conversion (E and I) or conversion per accidens (A to I).
4
Apply to the third figure
In the third figure, conjoining the conclusion with the conversion of the universal premise produces the conversion of the particular. For instance, if the universal premise is 'Every A is B', its conversion is 'Some B is A' (per accidens). The conclusion combined with this yields the particular premise.

The Syllogism
intermediate
8 min
Concept of Circular Syllogism with Universal Propositions
Author explains circular syllogism with universal negative examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the structure of a circular syllogism and construct one using universal negative or affirmative propositions in the first figure.

Core Idea
Circular syllogism recovers a premise by combining the conclusion with the converse of the other premise.

In this lesson we explore a special type of syllogism where the conclusion and one premise are used to derive the other premise. This technique, called qiyas al-dawr (circular syllogism), is often employed in theological debates to expose circular reasoning or to demonstrate the interdependence of premises. The author begins with universal propositions in the first figure.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original syllogism
Locate the two premises and the conclusion in the passage. For the first example: 'No human is a horse' (minor), 'Every laugher is human' (major), conclusion 'No horse is a laugher'.
The example uses secular terms, but the structure is universal.
2
Choose a premise to recover
Decide which original premise you want to reproduce. In the example, the minor premise 'No human is a horse' is reproduced.
3
Convert the other premise if needed
Take the major premise 'Every laugher is human' and convert it without changing quantity to 'Every human is a laugher' (universal affirmative conversion yields the converse with the same quantity).
Conversion rules: universal affirmative becomes universal affirmative by accidental conversion.
4
Combine the conclusion with the converted premise
Set the conclusion as a premise, and the converted major as the other premise, then see if they produce the desired original minor. For example, conclusion 'No horse is a laugher' and converted major 'Every human is a laugher' combine in the second figure to yield 'No human is a horse'.
The combination in the second figure with a negative major and affirmative minor yields a negative conclusion.

The fourth art: On circular syllogism and conversion of syllogism. And you made it the minor premise of the necessary negative, it produced from the first combination of the first figure the very subject, by saying: Every effect is not a god, and everything that is not a god is originated, so every effect is originated – which is the very affirmative major premise. And we have alerted to what is in the verification of circularity here. And if it is from the third figure, a circularity cannot be prepared from it either, due to what we preceded regarding affirmatives. Likewise if it is from the fourth and the major in it is negative. But if the minor is negative, it is possible to derive the negative from the affirmative. As for deriving the affirmative from the negative, it is only by a kind of imagination as we preceded. From the premise; example of the first: if it is said 'No human is a horse' and 'Every laugher is human' then 'No horse is a laugher'. Then if you make the conclusion the major for the converse of the major without changing quantity, saying 'Every human is a laugher' and 'No horse is a laugher', it produces from the first combination of the second figure 'No human is a horse' – which is the very minor. And example of the second: if it is said 'Nothing originated is eternal' and 'Every effect is originated' then 'Nothing eternal is an effect'. Then if you take the converse of the conclusion without changing quantity and make it a negated minor for the necessary of the negative premise, saying 'Every effect is not eternal' and 'Everything not eternal is originated', it produces 'Every effect is originated' – which is the very major premise. And as for deriving the particular from the universal, it is possible, and the combination then is either two affirmatives or one is negative. If they are two affirmatives, then either from the first, second, third, or fourth figure. If from the first, then combining the very conclusion with the converse of the major without changing quantity produces the very minor premise, as when it is said 'Some animal is laugher' and 'Every laugher is writer' then 'Some animal is writer'. Then you say 'Some animal is writer' and 'Every writer is laugher', it produces 'Some animal is laugher' – which is the very minor premise. And if from the second figure of the mixed ones, a circularity cannot be prepared from it due to what preceded regarding universals. And if from the third figure, then combining the conclusion with the converse of the universal premise produces the converse of the particular, and vice versa it returns to it. Its example: let the particular be minor: 'Some human is writer' and 'Every human is laugher' then 'Some writer is laugher'. Then if you say 'Some writer is laugher' and 'Every laugher is human' then 'Some writer is human' – and at its converse it becomes the very minor premise. And its example: let the particular be major: 'Every human is laugher' and 'Some human is writer' then if you say 'Every laugher is human' and 'Some laugher is writer', it produces from the same combination 'Some human is writer' – which is the very particular premise. And if from the fourth figure, the particular must be major as preceded, and then combining the conclusion with the converse of the universal affirmative in such a way that the conclusion is its minor produces the very major premise. For if it is said 'Every human is laugher' and 'Some animal is human' then 'Some laugher is animal' – then you say 'Some laugher is animal' and 'Every laugher is human', it produces 'Some animal is human' – which is the very particular premise. And upon this, the matter is not hidden when one of them is negative, and likewise the manner of its running in conditional syllogisms is not hidden.
Source pages p. 440

Commentary
This passage defines and illustrates circular syllogism (qiyas al-dawr), a technique where the conclusion of a syllogism is taken together with one of its premises (or a conversed form) to derive the other premise. The author begins by stating the fourth art covers both circular syllogism and conversion of syllogism. He then gives detailed examples for cases where the conclusion is universal negative or universal affirmative, explaining how to manipulate the propositions to reproduce the minor or major premise. For instance, in the first figure with negative propositions, if we have 'No human is a horse' and 'Every laugher is human' yielding 'No horse is a laugher', then by converting the major premise 'Every laugher is human' to 'Every human is a laugher' (without changing quantity) and combining it with the conclusion, we reproduce the original minor premise. The author also addresses particular propositions, showing that circular syllogism works in the first, third, and fourth figures under certain conditions, but not in the second figure of mixed premises. He provides examples using terms like 'human', 'horse', 'laugher', 'writer', and 'animal' as placeholders. The discussion is systematic, covering both affirmative and negative cases. The final remark extends the method to conditional syllogisms.

The Syllogism
advanced
10 min
Circular Syllogism with Particular Propositions
Author explains circular syllogism with particular propositions in figures 1,3,4.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct a circular syllogism using particular propositions in the first, third, and fourth figures.

Core Idea
Circularity with particulars works in figures 1, 3, and 4, but not in figure 2.

Having covered universal propositions, the author now turns to particulars. He shows how circular syllogism operates when one premise is particular affirmative. The method varies by figure: in the first figure, combining the conclusion with the converse of the major recovers the minor; in the third figure, it recovers the converse of the minor; and in the fourth figure, the particular must be major. No circularity is possible in the second figure with mixed premises.

Guided Path
1
Identify the type of particular combination
Determine whether the two premises are both affirmative, or one is negative. The author covers both affirmatives and then extends to negatives.
Focus on the affirmative case first.
2
In the first figure
If both premises are affirmative and the syllogism is in the first figure, combine the conclusion with the converse of the major premise (without changing quantity) to reproduce the minor premise. Example: from 'Some animal is laugher' and 'Every laugher is writer' yields 'Some animal is writer'. Then combine 'Some animal is writer' with 'Every writer is laugher' to get 'Some animal is laugher' (the minor).
The minor premise is the original particular affirmative that was used.
3
In the third figure
If the syllogism is in the third figure with two affirmatives, combining the conclusion with the converse of the universal premise yields the converse of the particular premise. Then converting that converse gives back the original particular. For example: 'Some human is writer' and 'Every human is laugher' yield 'Some writer is laugher'. Then 'Some writer is laugher' and 'Every laugher is human' yield 'Some writer is human', which converts to 'Some human is writer' (the minor).
The author provides two subcases: when the particular is minor and when it is major.
4
In the fourth figure
If the syllogism is in the fourth figure, the particular must be the major premise. Then combining the conclusion with the converse of the universal affirmative (making the conclusion the minor) reproduces the major premise. Example: 'Every human is laugher' and 'Some animal is human' yield 'Some laugher is animal'. Then 'Some laugher is animal' and 'Every laugher is human' yield 'Some animal is human' (the particular major).
The particular premise is the major in the fourth figure.

Inverting the Fourth Figure to Retrieve the Original Premise
Author explains retrieval of particular premise via inversion in fourth figure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the original particular premise of a fourth-figure categorical syllogism by applying inversion (ʿaks) of the universal affirmative premise and using the conclusion as the new minor.

Core Idea
Invert the universal affirmative; the conclusion becomes the minor to retrieve the major.

In this passage, the author demonstrates a key operation in the fourth figure: given a valid syllogism and its conclusion, you can retrieve the original particular premise by taking the converse of the universal affirmative premise and using the conclusion as the new minor. This technique is useful for reconstructing arguments and checking their validity. We will walk through the example step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original syllogism
Locate the two premises and the conclusion. In the example: 'Every human is laughing' (universal affirmative), 'Some animal is human' (particular affirmative, major), conclusion 'Some laughing is animal' (particular affirmative). Note that the particular premise is the major.
2
Isolate the universal affirmative premise
The universal affirmative premise is 'Every human is laughing' (A proposition). Convert it to its converse: 'Every laughing is human' (A proposition, as per the text's convention).
This conversion assumes coextensive terms, accepted in this context.
3
Use the original conclusion as the minor
Take the conclusion 'Some laughing is animal' as the minor premise of the new syllogism.
4
Combine to derive the original major
Form a new syllogism: major = 'Every laughing is human', minor = 'Some laughing is animal'. The conclusion is 'Some animal is human', which matches the original particular premise (major).
This demonstrates the inversion operation.
5
Verify the result
Check that the derived proposition is exactly the original particular premise. In the example, 'Some animal is human' is the original major.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما إن كان من الشكل الرابع فالجزئية فيه لا بد وأن تكون كبرى كما سبق، وعند ذلك فاقتران النتيجة بعكس الكليّة الموجبة على وجه تكون النتيجة صغراها ينتج عين المقدمة الكبرى، فإنه لو قيل (كل إنسان ضاحك وبعض الحيوان إنسان فـ بعض الضاحك حيوان فقلت بعض الضاحك (حيوان وكل ضاحك إنسان) أنتج بعض الحيوان إنسان وهو عين المقدمة الجزئية.
And as for the fourth figure, the particular [premise] in it must be the major, as previously. And then conjoining the conclusion with the converse of the universal affirmative such that the conclusion is the minor produces the very same major premise. For example, if it is said: 'Every human is laughing and some animal is human, so some laughing is animal'; then you say: 'Some laughing is animal, and every laughing is human' yields 'some animal is human', which is the very same particular premise.
Source pages p. 441

Commentary
This passage defines the operation of inverting a syllogism in the fourth figure to retrieve the original particular premise. The author states that in the fourth figure, the particular premise must be the major. Then, by conjoining the original conclusion with the converse of the universal affirmative premise (i.e., its inversion) in a way that the conclusion becomes the minor, we obtain the original major premise. The example uses the terms 'human' (insān), 'laughing' (ḍāḥik), and 'animal' (ḥayawān). The universal affirmative 'Every human is laughing' is inverted to 'Every laughing is human' (by simple conversion of a universal affirmative). Then, having the original conclusion 'Some laughing is animal' (which came from 'Some animal is human' via the original syllogism), when we combine it with the inverted premise, we derive 'Some animal is human', which is indeed the original particular premise. This demonstrates the method of reconstructing premises from a conclusion through inversion.

Key Terms
الشكل الرابع (al-shakl al-rābiʿ): The fourth figure of the categorical syllogism, where the middle term is the subject of the major premise and predicate of the minor premise. [Note: Stable across the tradition; sometimes called al-shakl al-rabīʿ.]
عكس الكلية الموجبة (ʿaks al-kulliyya al-mūjiba): The simple conversion (converse) of a universal affirmative proposition, which yields a universal affirmative as well (e.g., 'Every A is B' becomes 'Every B is A') – note: in Aristotelian logic, conversion of a universal affirmative is particular, but here it is treated as universal, reflecting a different tradition. [Note: In standard Aristotelian logic, conversion of an A proposition is particular; this text follows the Islamic logical tradition which sometimes accepts universal conversion of universal affirmatives when the terms are coextensive.]
الجزئية (al-juzʾiyya): A particular proposition (e.g., 'some A is B') [Note: No significant shift.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway

Aks al-Qiyas: Inversion of the Syllogism with Universal Affirmative Conclusion
Author defines inversion of syllogism and demonstrates with universal affirmative conclusion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the operation of ʿaks al-qiyās (inversion of syllogism) to derive the contradictory of a premise from a universal affirmative conclusion.

Core Idea
ʿAks al-qiyās: from a conclusion, invert by conjoining its opposite with a premise to get the other premise's opposite.

The concept of inversion of syllogism (ʿaks al-qiyās) is a powerful meta-syllogistic tool. It allows you to take a given valid categorical syllogism and, by conjoining the opposite of its conclusion with one premise, derive the opposite of the other premise. This lesson will walk you through how this works when the conclusion is universal affirmative.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original syllogism and its components
Locate the major premise, minor premise, middle term, and conclusion. In the example: Every B is C (minor), Every C is A (major), therefore Every B is A (conclusion).
2
Determine the opposite of the conclusion to be used
For universal affirmative, the contradictory is particular negative: Some B is not A. The contrary is universal negative: No B is A. Decide which opposite to use.
3
Conjoin the contradictory of the conclusion with one premise to form a new syllogism
Place the contradictory as either major or minor premise alongside the original premise. The text shows two options.
4
Identify the resulting mood and figure, and check validity
Using the contradictory as major with original minor yields a valid syllogism in the third figure (Bocardo) giving the contradictory of the original major. Using it as minor with original major yields second figure (Baroco) giving contradictory of original minor.
5
Confirm that the derived opposite is indeed the contradictory of the other premise
Verify that the conclusion of the new syllogism matches the opposite of the premise not used.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And as for the inversion of the syllogism, it is the conjoining of the opposite of the conclusion. And as for the contrary or the contradictory with one of the premises of its syllogism [producing] the opposite of the other premise. And that can occur in the conjunctive and the conditional [syllogisms]. If it is conjunctive, then its conclusion is either universal affirmative, or universal negative, or particular affirmative, or particular negative. If it is universal affirmative, then conjoining its contradictory with one of the two premises of its syllogism produces the contradictory of the other premise. For example, if it is said: 'Every B is C' and 'Every C is A' so 'Every B is A', then you say: 'Some B is not A.' If you make it the major for the minor, it produces from the fifth conjunction of the third figure 'Some C is not A', which is the contradictory of the major premise. And if you make it the minor for the major, it produces from the fourth conjunction of the second figure 'Some B is not C', which is the contradictory of the minor premise. And as for the contrary of the conclusion: if you make it the minor for the major, it produces from the second of the second 'No B is C', which is the contrary of the minor premise. And as for conjoining it with the minor, it does not produce unless from the fourth art in the syllogism of circularity and inversion of the syllogism, the third figure, which is not useful for the contrary of the universal.

Commentary
This passage defines 'aks al-qiyas (inversion of the syllogism) as the operation of conjoining the opposite (either the contradictory or the contrary) of the conclusion with one of the premises to derive the opposite of the other premise. The author first states that this can occur in both categorical (iqtirānī) and conditional (istithnā'ī) syllogisms. Then he focuses on categorical syllogisms whose conclusion can be one of four types: universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative, or particular negative. For the case of a universal affirmative conclusion, he provides an example using variables (ب, ج, أ). The original syllogism is: Every B is C, Every C is A, therefore Every B is A. The contradictory of the conclusion is 'Some B is not A'. If this contradictory is used as the major premise (with the original minor 'Every B is C' as minor, or as the minor with the original major 'Every C is A' as major), then from the appropriate figure (third figure fifth mood, or second figure fourth mood), we obtain the contradictory of the other premise. Similarly, the contrary of the conclusion ('No B is C') can be used to derive the contrary of the minor premise via the second figure second mood. However, using the contrary with the minor premise does not produce the contrary of the major unless through the third figure (which the author notes is not useful for that purpose). The author's careful enumeration shows the systematic nature of heuristics in the Islamic logical tradition.

Key Terms
عكس القياس (ʿaks al-qiyās): The operation of conjoining the opposite of the conclusion with one premise to derive the opposite of the other premise. [Note: In earlier texts, 'aks al-qiyās sometimes refers to converting the whole syllogism; here it specifically means a form of proof by contraposition.]
النقيض (al-naqīḍ): The contradictory (e.g., 'some B is not A' is the contradictory of 'every B is A'). [Note: Stable.]
الضد (al-ḍidd): The contrary (e.g., 'no B is A' is the contrary of 'every B is A'). [Note: Stable.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) The contradictory of the major premise
C) The contrary of the minor premise
D) The contrary of the major premise

Key Takeaway
The inversion of syllogism allows you to derive the opposite of a premise by conjoining the opposite of the conclusion with the other premise, provided you select the appropriate figure and mood.

Determine the contradictory of the conclusion. Then conjoin it with one premise to derive the contradictory of the other premise, identifying the figure and mood used.

Inversion for Universal Negative Conclusions
Author details inversion for universal negative conclusions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to invert a universal negative categorical syllogism in the first and second figures, deriving the contradictory of the missing premise, and identify the resulting mood.

Core Idea
Inversion of a universal negative conclusion reveals the hidden premise's contradiction.

In the previous lesson, we saw how inversion works for universal affirmative conclusions. Now we turn to universal negative conclusions (e.g., 'No B is A'). The author shows that the same technique applies, but with different mood outcomes depending on the original figure. Mastering this allows you to check the validity of syllogisms in Islamic logical arguments, such as those used in kalam and usul al-fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original syllogism
Locate the two premises and the universal negative conclusion. Determine the figure (first or second) based on the position of the middle term.
In the first figure, the middle term is subject of the major and predicate of the minor; in the second, it is predicate of both.
2
Take the contradictory of the conclusion
Form the particular affirmative contradictory: from 'No S is P' derive 'Some S is P'.
This new proposition will be used as a premise in the inversion.
3
Conjoin with a premise as major or minor
Choose either the original major or minor premise. With the contradictory as the other premise, form a new syllogism. The resulting conclusion will be the contradictory of the remaining original premise.
The text shows both possibilities: major with contradictory, and minor with contradictory.
4
Determine the resulting mood
The new syllogism belongs to a specific mood and figure. For example, in the first figure inversion, using the contradictory as major yields 'third of the third' mood (third figure, third mood); as minor yields 'third of the second'.
Mood numbers vary by convention; focus on the logical structure.
5
Check the second figure condition
In the second figure, inversion is only possible if at least one premise is negative. If both premises are affirmative, no valid inversion exists.
The author explicitly states: 'If both are affirmative, then no inversion can be achieved.'

And if the conclusion is universal negative, then its conjunction is from the first figure, or the second, or the fourth. If it is from the first, then conjoining the contradictory of the conclusion with one of the two premises of its syllogism produces the contradictory of the other premise. For example, if it is said: 'Every B is C' and 'No C is A' so 'No B is A'. Then if you say 'Some B is A', if you make it the major for the minor, it produces from the third of the third 'Some C is A', which is the contradictory of the major premise; and if you make it the minor for the major, it produces from the third of the second 'Some B is not C', which is the contradictory of the minor premise. And the ruling for the contrary is as previously for the affirmative. And if the conjunction is from the second figure, then either both premises are affirmative, or one is negative as before. If both are affirmative, then no inversion can be achieved from it. For if you take the contradictory of the conclusion in it and conjoin it with one of the two premises of the conjunction, it produces some of the other premise. Similarly, if you take the contrary conjoined with the minor, and if you take it conjoined with the major, it produces the very same minor premise. And if one of them is negative, then conjoining the contradictory of the conclusion in it with one of the two premises of its syllogism produces the contradictory of the other premise. Example: let the major be negative: if it is said: 'Every B is C' and 'No ? is C' so 'No B is A'. Then if you say 'Some B is A', if you make it the minor for the minor, it produces from the second of the third 'Some A is C', which is the contradictory of the major premise. And if you make it the minor for the major, it produces from the fourth of the first 'Some B is not C', which is the contradictory of the minor premise. And another example: let the minor be negative: if it is said: 'No B is C' and 'Every A is C' so 'No B is A'. Then you say 'Some B is A'. If you make it the minor for the minor, it produces from the sixth of the third 'Some A is not C', which is the contradictory of the major premise. And as for the contrary: if you make it the minor for the major in both conjunctions, it produces the contrary of the minor premise. And as for conjoining it with the minor, it does not produce the contrary of the major; for it does not produce unless from the third figure.

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion of 'aks al-qiyas, now focusing on the case where the original conclusion is universal negative (e.g., 'No B is A'). The author first notes that the original syllogism can be from the first, second, or fourth figures. He then examines the first figure scenario: take the contradictory of the conclusion ('Some B is A') and conjoin it with either the original major or minor. Using the first figure (Camestres? Actually the author identifies specific moods: conjoining as major yields a third-figure third mood result; as minor yields a second-figure third mood result). Then he moves to the second figure. Here, if both premises are affirmative, no inversion is possible; but if one is negative, then the contradictory of the conclusion can yield the contradictory of the other premise. He gives two sub-examples: one where the major is negative, another where the minor is negative, with specific moods (second of third, fourth of first, sixth of third). Finally, regarding the contrary of the conclusion, using it as minor with the major yields the contrary of the minor premise, but using it with the minor does not produce the contrary of the major unless through the third figure. This detailed analysis shows the systematic enumeration of possibilities in Islamic logic, essential for mastering the rules of inference.

Reductio Ad Absurdum for Universal Negative Conclusions
Method of proof by contradiction for universal negative conclusions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the reductio ad absurdum method to verify a universal negative conclusion in any figure of the categorical syllogism, and distinguish the role of the contradictory from the contrary.

Core Idea
Only the contradictory, not the contrary, of the conclusion yields a valid reductio proof for universal negative syllogisms.

In this passage, the author demonstrates a powerful method for proving the validity of a syllogism with a universal negative conclusion: assume the contradictory of the conclusion, combine it with one premise, and derive the contradictory of the other premise. The technique is systematically applied across the first, second, and fourth figures. Mastering this method deepens your understanding of syllogistic validity and the crucial difference between contradictory and contrary propositions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the figure and premises
Determine whether the given syllogism with a universal negative conclusion is in the first, second, or fourth figure. Note the two premises and their quantity and quality.
The passage only treats first, second, and fourth figures; third figure is excluded for universal negatives.
2
Form the contradictory of the conclusion
For the universal negative 'No B is A', the contradictory is the particular affirmative 'Some B is A'. Write this down as your assumption.
The contradictory (naqīḍ) is the proposition that must be true if the conclusion is false.
3
Combine the contradictory with one premise
Choose one of the original premises and join it with the contradictory to form a new syllogism. The author shows two options: making the contradictory the major or the minor of the new pair. Try both in the first figure example.
The choice of which premise to use and the order (major/minor) affects which contradictory is derived.
4
Derive the contradictory of the other premise
Check whether the new syllogism yields the contradictory of the other original premise. If it does, the original syllogism is valid. In the passage, the author refers to specific moods (e.g., 'third of the third') to confirm the derivation.
If the derivation fails, the original syllogism is invalid or the wrong assumption (e.g., using the contrary) was made.
5
Avoid using the contrary (ḍidd)
The author warns that using the contrary (e.g., 'Every B is A' as opposite of 'No B is A') does not reliably produce the opposite of the other premise. Only the contradictory works for this proof method.
This distinction is a common source of error; always use the contradictory in reductio proofs.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما إن كانت النتيجة كلية سالبة فاقترانها إما من الشكل الأول، أو الثاني، أو الرابع، فإن كان من الأول فاقتران نقيض النتيجة بإحدى مقدمتي قياسها كان ينتج نقيض المقدمة الأخرى، وذلك كما لو قيل (كل "ب" "ج") و(لا شيء من "ج" "أ") فـ (لا شيء من "ب" "أ") فإنك إذا قلت (بعض "ب" "أ") فإن جعلته كبرى للصغرى أنتج ثالث الثالث (بعض "ج" "أ") وهو نقيض المقدمة الكبرى، وإن جعلته صغرى للكبرى أنتج من ثاني الثاني (بعض "ب" ليس "ج") وهو نقيض المقدمة الصغرى. وأما حكم الضد فعلى ما مضى في الموجب. وأما إن كان الاقتران من الشكل الثاني فإما أن تكون مقدمتاه موجبتين، أو أن إحداهما سالبة كما سبق فإن كانتا موجبتين فليس يتهيأ منه عكس، فإنك إن أخذت نقيض النتيجة فيه فبإحدى مقدمتي الاقتران قرنتها أنتج بعض المقدمة الأخرى، وكذا إن أخذت الضد مقترناً بالصغرى، وإن أخذته مقترناً بالكبرى أنتج عين المقدمة الصغرى. وأما إن كانت إحداهما سالبة فاقتران نقيض النتيجة فيها بإحدى مقدمتي قياسها كان ينتج نقيض المقدمة الأخرى، مثال ذلك ولتكن الكبرى سالبة ما لو قيل (كل "ب" "ج") و(لا شيء من "أ" "ج") فـ (لا شيء من "ب" "أ") فإنك إذا قلت (بعض "ب" "أ") فإن جعلته صغرى للصغرى أنتج من ثاني الثالث (بعض "أ" "ج") وهو نقيض المقدمة الكبرى، وإن جعلته صغرى للكبرى أنتج من رابع الأول (بعض "ب" ليس "ج") وهو نقيض المقدمة الصغرى، ومثاله ولتكن الصغرى سالبة ما لو قيل (لا شيء من "ب" "ج") و(كل "أ" "ج") فـ (لا شيء من "ب" "أ") فقلت (بعض "ب" "أ") فإن جعلته صغرى للصغرى أنتج من سادس الثالث (بعض "أ" ليس "ج") وهو نقيض المقدمة الكبرى. وأما الضد فإن جعلته صغرى للكبرى في الاقترانين أنتج ضد المقدمة الصغرى وأما اقترانه بالصغرى فليس ينتج ضد الكبرى؛ إذ هو لا ينتج من غير الشكل الثالث. وإن كان الاقتران من الشكل الرابع، كقولنا: (لا شيء من "ب" "ج") و(كل "أ" "ب") فـ (لا شيء من "ج" "أ") فاقتران نقيض النتيجة بإحدى المقدمتين كان أنتج نقيض المقدمة الأخرى، فإنك إذا قلت (بعض "ج" "أ") و(لا شيء من "ب" "ج") أنتج من الاقتران الخامس من الرابع (بعض "أ" ليس "ب") وهو نقيض المقدمة الكبرى، وإن قلت (بعض "ج" "أ") و(كل "أ" "ب") أنتج من ثالث الأول (بعض "ج" "ب") وهو عكس نقيض المقدمة الصغرى. وأما الضد فليس يتهيأ من اقترانه بإحدى المقدمتين إنتاج ضد الآخر، بل النقيض، واعتبره بنفسك.
As for when the conclusion is a universal negative, its syllogism belongs either to the first figure, the second, or the fourth. If it is from the first figure, then combining the contradictory of the conclusion with one of its two premises yields the contradictory of the other premise. For example, if it is said: 'Every B is C' and 'No C is A', so 'No B is A'. Then if you say 'Some B is A', if you make it the major for the minor, the third of the third produces 'Some C is A', which is the contradictory of the major premise. And if you make it the minor for the major, the second of the second produces 'Some B is not C', which is the contradictory of the minor premise. As for the rule of the opposite, it is as previously for the affirmative. And if the syllogism is from the second figure, either both its premises are affirmative or one of them is negative as before. If both are affirmative, then conversion is not possible from it. For if you take the contradictory of its conclusion and combine it with one of the two premises, it produces some of the other premise. Likewise, if you take the opposite combined with the minor, and if you take it combined with the major, it produces the very same minor premise. And if one of them is negative, then combining the contradictory of its conclusion with one of its two premises yields the contradictory of the other premise. Example: let the major be negative: if it is said 'Every B is C' and 'No A is C', so 'No B is A'. Then if you say 'Some B is A', if you make it the minor for the minor, the second of the third produces 'Some A is C', which is the contradictory of the major premise. And if you make it the minor for the major, the fourth of the first produces 'Some B is not C', which is the contradictory of the minor premise. Another example: let the minor be negative: if it is said 'No B is C' and 'Every A is C', so 'No B is A'. Then you say 'Some B is A'. If you make it the minor for the minor, the sixth of the third produces 'Some A is not C', which is the contradictory of the major premise. As for the opposite, if you make it the minor for the major in the two syllogisms, it produces the opposite of the minor premise; but combining it with the minor does not produce the opposite of the major, because it does not produce from any figure except the third. And if the syllogism is from the fourth figure, like our saying: 'No B is C' and 'Every A is B', so 'No C is A'. Then combining the contradictory of the conclusion with one of the two premises yields the contradictory of the other premise. For if you say 'Some C is A' and 'No B is C', the fifth combination of the fourth produces 'Some A is not B', which is the contradictory of the major premise. And if you say 'Some C is A' and 'Every A is B', the third of the first produces 'Some C is B', which is the converse of the contradictory of the minor premise. As for the opposite, it is not possible from combining it with one of the two premises to produce the opposite of the other; rather, the contradictory. And consider it yourself.
Source pages p. 442

Commentary
This passage is a detailed exposition of the proof by contradiction (burhan al-aks) applied to syllogisms with universal negative conclusions. The author, likely following the tradition of Avicenna, demonstrates how assuming the contradictory of the conclusion, when combined with one of the premises, yields the contradictory of the other premise, thereby confirming the original syllogism's validity. The discussion is organized by figure. For the first figure, the author provides an example with affirmative universal minor and negative universal major, showing two ways of combining the contradictory of the conclusion ('Some B is A') — as major or minor — resulting in contradictions of the respective original premises. For the second figure, two cases are distinguished: when both premises are affirmative, no conversion is possible (as expected, since the second figure yields no valid mood with two affirmatives); when one premise is negative, the procedure works, illustrated with examples where either the major or the minor is negative. For the fourth figure, the example uses a negative minor and affirmative major, and the combinations yield the contradictory of the major or the converse of the contradictory of the minor. The passage also addresses the 'opposite' (al-didd) of the conclusion, noting that using the opposite often does not produce the opposite of the other premise; only the contradictory works reliably. The reference to 'third figure' at the end indicates a boundary condition. Throughout, the author employs the standard notation (B, C, A) and refers to specific compound syllogisms by their numbers (e.g., 'third of the third figure'). This technical treatment assumes familiarity with the complete system of syllogistic moods.

Key Terms
نقيض النتيجة
ضد
اقتران

Key Takeaway
For universal negative conclusions, only the contradictory (not the contrary) of the conclusion can be used in reductio ad absurdum to derive the contradictory of the remaining premise.

Reductio ad Absurdum for Particular Affirmative Conclusions
Author extends reductio to particular affirmatives in first and third figures.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the reductio ad absurdum method to verify syllogisms with particular affirmative conclusions in the first and third figures, distinguishing between when the result is a contradictory or a contrary (opposite).

Core Idea
Assuming the contradictory of a particular affirmative gives a universal negative; combining it with premises reveals validity by yielding contradictions or contraries.

In this passage, the author extends the proof by contradiction (reductio ad absurdum) to syllogisms whose conclusion is a particular affirmative proposition. You will learn how to test such syllogisms by assuming the contradictory of the conclusion and combining it with the original premises. The method differs depending on the figure and the types of premises, especially in the third figure where universal premises yield the opposite (contrary) rather than the contradictory of a premise.

Guided Path
1
Identify the figure
Determine whether the original syllogism is in the first figure (major universal, minor particular) or the third figure (both premises about the same subject). The passage covers first and third figures; fourth is mentioned but not detailed.
The second figure cannot produce a particular affirmative conclusion directly.
2
Assume the contradictory of the conclusion
The conclusion is particular affirmative (e.g., 'Some B is A'). Its contradictory is a universal negative ('No B is A'). Write down this assumption.
This assumption will be used as a premise in new syllogisms.
3
Combine with one original premise in two orders
Take the assumed universal negative and combine it with each original premise (the minor and major) in two ways: first as major with the other premise as minor, then as minor with the other as major. Each combination forms a new syllogism whose conclusion is derived.
In the first figure example, the two combinations produce the contradictory of the other premise. In the third figure, the result depends on whether the original premises are universal or mixed.
4
Interpret the result
Check whether the derived conclusion contradicts the remaining original premise. If it does, the original syllogism is valid. In the third figure with two universal premises, the result is the opposite (contrary) of the premise, not the contradictory. This indicates validity because the contrary implies falsity of the original affirmative.
The opposite (ضد) here is a universal negative, which is the contrary of a universal affirmative, not its contradictory.

وأما إن كانت النتيجة جزئية موجبة فاقترانها أيضاً إما من الشكل الأول، أو الثالث، أو الرابع، فإن كان من الأول فاقتران نقيض النتيجة بإحدى مقدمتي قياسها كان ينتج نقيض المقدمة الأخرى، وذلك كما إذا قيل (بعض "ب" "ج") و(كل "ج" "أ") فـ (بعض "ب" "أ") فقلت (لا شيء من "ب" "أ") فإن جعلته كبرى للصغرى أنتج من سادس الثالث (بعض "ج" ليس "أ") وهو نقيض المقدمة الكبرى، وإن جعلته صغرى للكبرى أنتج من ثاني الثاني (لا شيء من "ب" "ج") وهو نقيض المقدمة الصغرى. وإن كان الاقتران من الشكل الثالث فإما أن تكون المقدمتان كليتين أو إن إحداهما جزئية، فإن كانتا كليتين فاقتران نقيض النتيجة بإحدى مقدمتي قياسها كان ينتج ضد المقدمة الأخرى، وذلك كما لو قيل (كل "ب" "ج") و(كل "ب" "أ") فـ (بعض "ج" "أ") فإذا قلت (لا شيء من "ج" "أ") فإن جعلته كبرى للصغرى أنتج (لا شيء من "ب" "أ") وهو ضد المقدمة الكبرى، وإن جعلته كبرى للكبرى أنتج (لا شيء من "ب" "ج") وهو ضد المقدمة الصغرى. وأما إن كانت إحداهما جزئية فاقتران نقيض النتيجة بإحدى المقدمتين كان ينتج نقيض المقدمة الأخرى، وسواء كانت الجزئية صغرى أو كبرى، مثال ذلك ولتكن الجزئية صغرى (بعض "ب" "ج") و(كل "ب" "أ") فـ (بعض "ج" "أ") فإذا قلت (لا شيء من "ج" "أ") فإن جعلته كبرى للصغرى أنتج (بعض "ب" ليس "ج") وهو نقيض المقدمة الصغرى، وإن جعلته صغرى للكبرى أنتج (بعض "ب" ليس "أ") وهو نقيض المقدمة الكبرى.
And if the conclusion is particular affirmative, then its syllogism belongs to the first figure, or the third, or the fourth. If it is from the first, then combining the contradictory of the conclusion with one of its two premises yields the contradictory of the other premise. For example, if it is said: 'Some B is C' and 'Every C is A', so 'Some B is A'. Then you say 'No B is A'. If you make it the major for the minor, the sixth of the third produces 'Some C is not A', which is the contradictory of the major premise. And if you make it the minor for the major, the second of the second produces 'No B is C', which is the contradictory of the minor premise. And if the syllogism is from the third figure, then either both premises are universal or one of them is particular. If both are universal, then combining the contradictory of the conclusion with one of its two premises yields the opposite of the other premise. For example, if it is said: 'Every B is C' and 'Every B is A', so 'Some C is A'. Then if you say 'No C is A', if you make it the major for the minor, it produces 'No B is A', which is the opposite of the major premise. And if you make it the major for the major, it produces 'No B is C', which is the opposite of the minor premise. And if one of them is particular, then combining the contradictory of the conclusion with one of the two premises yields the contradictory of the other premise, whether the particular is the minor or the major. Example: let the particular be the minor: 'Some B is C' and 'Every B is A', so 'Some C is A'. Then if you say 'No C is A', if you make it the major for the minor, it produces 'Some B is not C', which is the contradictory of the minor premise. And if you make it the minor for the major, it produces 'Some B is not A', which is the contradictory of the major premise.
Source pages p. 443

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion of proof by contradiction, now focusing on conclusions that are particular affirmatives. The author covers two figures that can produce such conclusions: the first and the third (the second figure cannot yield a particular affirmative directly). For the first figure, the example uses a particular minor and universal major; assuming the contradictory of the conclusion (which is universal negative) and combining it in two ways yields contradictories of the respective premises. For the third figure, two subcases are distinguished: when both premises are universal, the assumption of the contradictory leads to the opposite (ضد) of one premise, not the contradictory. This is an important nuance: in the third figure with two universal premises, the contradictory of a particular affirmative is a universal negative, and combining it yields a universal negative which is the opposite (contrary) of the original universal affirmative premise. When one premise is particular, the result reverts to yielding contradictories. The author gives an example with a particular minor and universal major. The discussion of the fourth figure for this case is likely on the following page. This passage reinforces the systematic nature of the reductio method across valid moods.

Key Terms
نتيجة جزئية موجبة
مقدمة كلية
ضد

Checkpoint
In the third figure, when both premises are universal and the conclusion is particular affirmative, what does assuming the contradictory of the conclusion produce?
A) The opposite (ضد) of one premise
B) The contradictory of one premise
C) The converse of one premise
D) Nothing, because the syllogism is invalid

Key Takeaway
When using reductio for particular affirmative conclusions, always assume the universal negative; in the third figure with universal premises, the result is the contrary (opposite) of a premise, otherwise it is the contradictory.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the syllogism: 'Every intoxicant is forbidden (haram). Some nabidh is an intoxicant. Therefore, some nabidh is forbidden.' Use the reductio method from this lesson to test its validity. Write down the assumed contradictory, combine it with each premise, and state what conclusions are produced. Indicate whether those conclusions are contradictories or contraries of the remaining premises.
?
For the third figure syllogism: 'Every human is mortal. Every human is rational. Therefore, some mortal is rational.' Verify its validity using reductio. Note that both premises are universal. Show that assuming the contradictory of the conclusion yields the opposite (contrary) of one premise, not the contradictory. Explain why this still proves validity.

The Syllogism
advanced
10 min
Reducing Syllogisms via the Contradictory of the Conclusion
Author methodically demonstrates the reduction method across all four figures.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the method of using the contradictory of the conclusion to derive the missing premise in any categorical syllogism figure.

Core Idea
The contradictory of the conclusion combined with one premise reveals the other premise's contradiction — a test of validity.

In this passage, the author systematically explains a powerful technique: take the contradictory of a valid syllogism's conclusion, combine it with one premise, and you obtain the contradictory of the other premise. This method, known as qiyas al-dawr, is used in Islamic logic to verify syllogisms and is the foundation of reductio arguments in kalam and fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original syllogism
Find the premises and conclusion of a valid categorical syllogism. For example: Every B is J, Every A is B, so Every A is J (Barbara).
The method works for any figure and mood.
2
Take the contradictory of the conclusion
Form the proposition that conflicts with the conclusion. If the conclusion is 'Every A is J', its contradictory is 'Some A is not J'.
The contradictory must be exact: if universal affirmative, contradictory is particular negative; if universal negative, particular affirmative, etc.
3
Combine the contradictory with one premise
Pair the contradictory with either the major or minor premise to form a new syllogism. The result should be the contradictory of the other premise.
The author shows that this works in all figures, though in some cases the result is the converse of the contradictory.
4
Verify the outcome
Check that the conclusion of the new syllogism matches the contradictory (or its converse) of the other original premise. This confirms the validity of the original syllogism.
If it does not, the original syllogism may be invalid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن كان الاقتران من الشكل الرابع، كقولنا: (لا شيء من ب ج) و(كل أ ب) فـ (لا شيء من ج أ) فاقتران نقيض النتيجة بإحدى المقدمتين كان أنتج نقيض المقدمة الأخرى، فإنك إذا قلت (بعض ج أ) و (لا شيء من ب ج) أنتج من الاقتران الخامس من الرابع (بعض أ ليس ب) وهو نقيض المقدمة الكبرى، وإن قلت (بعض ج أ) و(كل أ ب) أنتج من ثالث الأول (بعض ج ب) وهو عكس نقيض المقدمة الصغرى.
وأما الضد فليس يتهيأ من اقترانه بإحدى المقدمتين إنتاج ضد الآخر، بل النقيض، واعتبره بنفسك.
وأما إن كانت النتيجة جزئية موجبة فاقترانها أيضاً إما من الشكل الأول، أو الثالث، أو الرابع، فإن كان من الأول فاقتران نقيض النتيجة بإحدى مقدمتي قياسها كان ينتج نقيض المقدمة الأخرى، وذلك كما إذا قيل (بعض ب ج) و(كل ج أ) فـ (بعض ب أ) فقلت (لا شيء من ب أ) فإن جعلته كبرى للصغرى أنتج من سادس الثالث (بعض ج ليس أ) وهو نقيض المقدمة الكبرى، وإن جعلته صغرى للكبرى أنتج من ثاني الثاني (لا شيء من ب ج) وهو نقيض المقدمة الصغرى.
وإن كان الاقتران من الشكل الثالث فإما أن تكون المقدمتان كليتين أو إن إحداهما جزئية، فإن كانتا كليتين فاقتران نقيض النتيجة بإحدى مقدمتي قياسها كان ينتج ضد المقدمة الأخرى، وذلك كما لو قيل (كل ب ج) و(كل ب أ) فـ (بعض ج أ) فإذا قلت (لا شيء من ج أ) فإن جعلته كبرى للصغرى أنتج (لا) من ثاني الأول (لا) شيء من ب أ) وهو ضد المقدمة الكبرى، وإن جعلته كبرى للكبرى أنتج من أول الثاني (لا شيء من ب ج) وهو ضد المقدمة الصغرى. وأما إن كانت إحداهما جزئية فاقتران نقيض النتيجة بإحدى المقدمتين كان ينتج نقيض المقدمة الأخرى، وسواء كانت الجزئية صغرى أو كبرى، مثال ذلك ولتكن الجزئية صغرى (بعض ب ج) و(كل ب أ) فـ (بعض ج أ) فإذا قلت لا شيء من ج أ فإن جعلته كبرى للصغرى أنتج من رابع الأول (بعض ب ليس أ) وهو نقيض المقدمة الكبرى، وإن جعلته كبرى للكبرى أنتج من أول الثاني (لا شيء من ب ج) وهو نقيض المقدمة الصغرى ومثاله ولتكن الكبرى جزئية (كل ب ج) و(بعض ب أ) فـ (بعض ج أ) فإذا أخذت النقيض وهو (لا شيء من ج أ) وجعلته كبرى للصغرى أنتج من ثاني الأول (لا شيء من ب أ) وهو نقيض المقدمة الكبرى، وإن جعلته كبرى للكبرى أنتج من ثالث الثاني (بعض ب ليس ج) وهو نقيض المقدمة الصغرى.
وأما إن كان الاقتران من الشكل الرابع فإما أن تكون مقدمتاه أيضاً كليتين أو أن إحداهما جزئية، فإن كانتا كليتين فاقتران نقيض النتيجة بالصغرى ينتج عكس نقيض الكبرى، واقترانه بالكبرى ينتج نقيض الصغرى، وذلك كما لو قيل (كل ب ج) و(كل أ ب) فـ (بعض ج أ) فإذا أخذت (لا شيء من ج أ) وجعلته كبرى للصغرى أنتج ثاني الأول (لا شيء من ب أ) وعكسه هو ضد المقدمة الكبرى، وإن جعلته كبرى للكبرى أنتج من رابع الرابع (بعض ب ليس ج) وهو نقيض المقدمة الصغرى.
وإن كانت إحداهما جزئية فلا بُدّ وأن تكون كبرى على ما عرف وعند ذلك فاقتران نقيض النتيجة بالصغرى ينتج عكس نقيض الكبرى، واقترانه بالكبرى ينتج نقيض الصغرى، وذلك كما لو قيل (كل ب ج) و(بعض أ ب) فـ (بعض ج أ) فإذا قيل (لا شيء من ج أ) فإن جعل كبرى للصغرى أنتج من ثاني الأول عكس نقيض الكبرى، وإن جعل كبرى للكبرى أنتج من خامس الرابع (بعض ب ليس ج) وهو نقيض نقيض المقدمة الصغرى.
وأما إن كانت النتيجة جزئية سالبة فاقتران نقيضها أيضاً بإحدى مقدمتي قياسها كان ينتج نقيض المقدمة الأخرى، ولا تبالي من أي شكل كان، ما عدا الرابع من الثالث والرابع؛ إذ هو في الأول ينتج الضد، وفي الثاني عكسه، واعتبر ذلك في الكل بنفسك، وعلى هذا يجب أن تعتبر الحال في الاستثنائيات.
If the conjunction is from the fourth figure, like our saying: (Nothing from B is J) and (Every A is B) so (Nothing from J is A), then combining the contradictory of the result with one of the two premises used to produce the contradictory of the other premise. For if you say (Some J is A) and (Nothing from B is J), it produces from the fifth conjunction of the fourth (Some A is not B) which is the contradictory of the major premise. And if you say (Some J is A) and (Every A is B), it produces from the third of the first (Some J is B) which is the converse of the contradictory of the minor premise. As for the contrary, it is not possible from combining it with one of the two premises to produce the contrary of the other, rather the contradictory. And consider that yourself. As for if the result is a particular affirmative, then its conjunction is also either from the first figure, or third, or fourth. If it is from the first, then combining the contradictory of the result with one of the two premises of its syllogism used to produce the contradictory of the other premise. That is as when it is said (Some B is J) and (Every J is A) so (Some B is A); then you said (Nothing from B is A). If you make it major for the minor, it produces from the sixth of the third (Some J is not A) which is the contradictory of the major premise. And if you make it minor for the major, it produces from the second of the second (Nothing from B is J) which is the contradictory of the minor premise. And if the conjunction is from the third figure, then either both premises are universal or one of them is particular. If both are universal, then combining the contradictory of the result with one of the two premises of its syllogism produces the contrary of the other premise. That is as if it were said (Every B is J) and (Every B is A) so (Some J is A). Then if you say (Nothing from J is A) and if you make it major for the minor, it produces from the second of the first (Nothing from B is A) which is the contrary of the major premise. And if you make it major for the major, it produces from the first of the second (Nothing from B is J) which is the contrary of the minor premise. As for if one of them is particular, then combining the contradictory of the result with one of the two premises produces the contradictory of the other premise, whether the particular is minor or major. An example: let the particular be minor (Some B is J) and (Every B is A) so (Some J is A); then if you say (Nothing from J is A), if you make it major for the minor, it produces from the fourth of the first (Some B is not A) which is the contradictory of the major premise; and if you make it major for the major, it produces from the first of the second (Nothing from B is J) which is the contradictory of the minor premise. Another example: let the major be particular (Every B is J) and (Some B is A) so (Some J is A); then if you take the contradictory (Nothing from J is A) and make it major for the minor, it produces from the second of the first (Nothing from B is A) which is the contradictory of the major premise; and if you make it major for the major, it produces from the third of the second (Some B is not J) which is the contradictory of the minor premise. As for if the conjunction is from the fourth figure, then either both its premises are universal or one of them is particular. If both are universal, then combining the contradictory of the result with the minor produces the converse of the contradictory of the major, and combining it with the major produces the contradictory of the minor. That is as if it were said (Every B is J) and (Every A is B) so (Some J is A). Then if you take (Nothing from J is A) and make it major for the minor, it produces the second of the first (Nothing from B is A), and its converse is the contrary of the major premise. And if you make it major for the major, it produces from the fourth of the fourth (Some B is not J) which is the contradictory of the minor premise. And if one of them is particular, it must be the major, as known. And then combining the contradictory of the result with the minor produces the converse of the contradictory of the major, and combining it with the major produces the contradictory of the minor. That is as if it were said (Every B is J) and (Some A is B) so (Some J is A); then if it is said (Nothing from J is A), if it is made major for the minor, it produces from the second of the first the converse of the contradictory of the major; and if it is made major for the major, it produces from the fifth of the fourth (Some B is not J) which is the contradictory of the contradictory of the minor premise. As for if the result is a particular negative, then combining its contradictory also with one of the two premises of its syllogism produces the contradictory of the other premise, and you do not care which figure it is from, except the fourth of the third and the fourth; for in the first it produces the contrary, and in the second its converse. And consider that in all yourself. And on this you must consider the case in conditionals.
Source pages p. 443

Commentary
This passage is an exposition of the method known as 'qiyas al-dawr' (syllogism of conversion) or 'aks al-qiyas' (converse of the syllogism), which is a technique for proving the validity of a categorical syllogism by reductio ad absurdum. The author systematically examines all possible figures and premise combinations. The core idea is that if you take the contradictory (naqid) of the conclusion of a valid syllogism and combine it with one of the original premises, you will obtain the contradictory of the other premise. The author begins with the fourth figure when the conclusion is a universal negative, showing how combining the contradictory of the conclusion with the major or minor premise yields the contradictory of the other premise. He then notes that the contrary (didd) does not work in this way; only the contradictory is productive. Next he considers when the conclusion is a particular affirmative, examining the first, third, and fourth figures. In the first figure with a particular affirmative conclusion, combining the contradictory of the conclusion with one premise produces the contradictory of the other premise. In the third figure, if both premises are universal, the operation produces the contrary of the other premise; but if one premise is particular, it produces the contradictory. In the fourth figure, the result depends on whether the premises are both universal or one is particular, and sometimes yields the converse of the contradictory (aks al-naqid) rather than the direct contradictory. Finally, he addresses when the conclusion is a particular negative, stating that in most cases the contradictory of the conclusion yields the contradictory of the other premise, except in some cases where it yields the contrary or its converse. He concludes by saying the same principle applies to conditional (istithna'i) syllogisms. Throughout, the author uses the standard Arabic logical notation with variable terms (ب, ج, أ) and refers to specific moods of the figures (e.g., 'second of the first', 'fourth of the fourth'). This analysis is a key tool in Islamic logic for confirming the validity of syllogisms, and it parallels Aristotelian reduction techniques.

ضد
عكس النقيض
اقتران

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the general result of combining the contradictory of the conclusion with one premise in a valid categorical syllogism?
A) It produces the contrary of the other premise.
B) It produces the contradictory of the other premise (or its converse in some cases).
C) It produces the subcontrary of the other premise.
D) It has no effect on the other premise.

Key Takeaway
The contradictory of the conclusion, paired with one premise, reveals the contradictory of the other premise — a direct test of syllogistic validity across all figures.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Take the following valid syllogism from kalam: 'Every created thing has a cause (كل حادث له سبب), the world is created (العالم حادث), therefore the world has a cause (العالم له سبب).' Apply the reduction method: form the contradictory of the conclusion and combine it with one premise to derive the contradictory of the other premise. Write down the steps and verify the result.

In a fiqh context, consider the syllogism: 'Every intoxicant is forbidden (كل مسكر حرام), every nabidh is an intoxicant (كل نبيذ مسكر), therefore every nabidh is forbidden (كل نبيذ حرام).' Using the contradictory of the conclusion, perform the reduction to check if the premises hold. If they do not, explain what that would indicate about the original syllogism.

The Syllogism

Structure of Reductio ad Absurdum in Islamic Logic
Author defines and decomposes reductio ad absurdum.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two components of a reductio ad absurdum argument and trace how it reduces to a direct syllogism.

Core Idea
Reductio is not a special logic—it is a two-step argument (conjunctive + exceptive) reducible to direct syllogism.

Reductio ad absurdum (qiyas al-khalf) is a powerful proof technique used throughout Islamic legal and theological reasoning. Instead of proving a claim directly, you assume its opposite and show that this leads to an absurdity. The passage you are about to study dismantles this argument into its precise logical components: a conjunctive syllogism that generates a contradiction, followed by an exceptive syllogism that negates the original assumption. Understanding this structure will sharpen your ability to construct and critique proofs in usul al-fiqh and kalam.

Guided Path
1
Identify the overall aim of qiyas al-khalf
Read the first lines: the author says it proves the desired proposition by refuting its contradictory. Notice that the conclusion is reached indirectly—through absurdity.
2
Decompose into two component syllogisms
The author states it is composed of two syllogisms: a conjunctive (اقتراني) and an exceptive (استثنائي). The first produces an absurdity, the second uses that absurdity to deny the assumption.
This decomposition is key to seeing why reductio is valid.
3
Analyze the first (conjunctive) syllogism
The minor premise is a conditional: 'If the desired proposition is false, then its contradictory is true.' The major is an assumed true proposition (e.g., 'Every C is A'). Their conjunction yields an absurd consequence.
The absurdity is usually a contradiction or impossibility.
4
Analyze the second (exceptive) syllogism
The result of the first syllogism is a conditional: 'If the assumption (antecedent) holds, then absurdity (consequent) follows.' The exceptive premise denies the consequent (absurdity), so the antecedent must be false.
This is modus tollens: denying the consequent negates the antecedent.
5
Understand the reduction to direct syllogism
The author shows that the entire reductio can be transformed into a direct categorical syllogism by rearranging the premises. This confirms that reductio is not a separate logic but a derived form.
Mastering this reduction helps you see the unity of logical methods.

Source Text

الفن الخامس
في تحقيق قياس الخلف ورده إلى المستقيم
والإشارة إلى ما به المباينة والمشاركة بينه وبين المستقيم وعكس القياس
أما قياس الخلف فهو عبارة عن قول قياسي يبين صحة المطلوب بإبطال نقيضه، وهو مركب من قياسين أحدهما اقتراني مؤلف من مقدمتين صغراه شرطية متصلة، مقدمها فرض كذب المطلوب، وثالثها فرض صدق نقيضه، وكبراء قضية مفروضة صادقة، إما حَمْلية وإما شرطية، يلزم من اقترانها بتالي الصغرى المحال والآخر استثنائي مؤلف من مقدمتين شرطية متصلة وهي ما وقعت نتيجة للاقتران واستثنائية من تالي هذه الشرطية ناتجاً بطلان عين المقدم، ثم المطلوب إما حملي وإما شرطي، فإن كان حملياً فالكبرى في الاقتران قد تكون حملية وقد تكون شرطية، إما منفصلة مثاله ولتكن الكبرى حَمْلية قولنا: إن كان ليس كل "ب" "ج") كاذباً فـ (كل "ب" "ج") صادق، ولتكن الحملية المفروض صدقها في هذا الاقتران مثلاً و(كل "ج" "أ") وينتج أنه إن كان ليس كل "ب" "ج") كاذباً فـ (كل "ب" "أ") صادق، فإذا قلت لكن ليس كل "ب" "أ") صادقاً لزم فـ (ليس كل "ب" "ج") كاذباً، ومثاله ولتكن الكبرى شرطية متصلة كقولنا وكل ما كان كل "ب" "ج") صادقاً فـ ("د" "ر") صادق، فينتج أنه إن كان ليس كل "ب" "ج") كاذباً فـ ("د" "ر") صادق، فإذا قلت لكن ليس "د" "ر") صادقاً لزم منه أنه ليس ليس كل "ب" "ج") كاذباً، ومثاله ولتكن الكبرى شرطية منفصلة قولنا: و(كل "ب" "ج" إما "د" وإما "ر") فيلزم إن كان ليس كل "ب" "ج") كاذباً فـ (إما "د" وإما "ر") لكن ليس إما "د" وإما "ر") ف (ليس ليس كل "ب" "ج") كاذباً وأما إن كان المطلوب شرطياً فإما متصل وإما منفصل، فإن كان متصلاً فقد تكون أيضاً كبرى الاقتران حملية، وقد تكون شرطية، وإن كانت شرطية فقد تكون متصلة وقد تكون منفصلة مثاله ولتكن الكبرى حملية
The Fifth Art on Verifying Reductio ad Absurdum and Reducing it to Direct Syllogism, and an indication of the difference and similarity between it and the direct syllogism and conversion of syllogism. As for reductio ad absurdum, it is a syllogistic statement that demonstrates the truth of the desired proposition by refuting its contradictory. It is composed of two syllogisms: one conjunctive [categorical] composed of two premises, its minor is a conditional conjunctive, its antecedent is the assumption of the falsehood of the desired proposition and its consequent is the assumption of the truth of its contradictory, and its major is an assumed true proposition, either categorical or conditional, from the conjunction of which with the consequent of the minor an absurdity follows; and the other exceptive composed of two premises: a conditional conjunctive which is the result of the conjunction, and an exceptive from the consequent of this conditional, producing the nullification of the antecedent itself. Then the desired proposition is either categorical or conditional. If it is categorical, then the major in the conjunction may be categorical or conditional, either disjunctive. For example, let the major be categorical: 'If not every B is C is false, then every B is C is true', and let the assumed true categorical in this conjunction be, for example, 'Every C is A', and it yields: 'If not every B is C is false, then every B is A is true'. Then if you say: 'But not every B is A is true', it follows that 'Not every B is C is false'. Another example: let the major be a conditional conjunctive, like our saying: 'And everything such that every B is C is true, then D and R is true', so it yields: 'If not every B is C is false, then D and R is true'. Then if you say: 'But D and R is not true', it follows that 'It is not the case that not every B is C is false'. Another example: let the major be a conditional disjunctive, our saying: 'And every B is C is either D or R', so it follows: 'If not every B is C is false, then either D or R'. But 'Not either D or R', so 'It is not the case that not every B is C is false'. And if the desired proposition is conditional, then it is either conjunctive or disjunctive. If it is conjunctive, then the major of the conjunction may also be categorical, and may be conditional, and if conditional, it may be conjunctive or disjunctive. For example, let the major be categorical...
Source pages p. 444

Commentary
This passage defines and exemplifies reductio ad absurdum (قياس الخلف). The author begins by stating its purpose: to prove a proposition by refuting its contradictory. He then analyzes its structure: it consists of two syllogisms. The first is a categorical (اقتراني) syllogism whose minor premise is a conditional, with the antecedent assuming the falsehood of the desired proposition and the consequent assuming the truth of its contradictory. The major premise is an assumed true proposition (categorical or conditional). Combining the major with the consequent of the minor leads to an absurdity. The second syllogism is exceptive (استثنائي): it takes the result of the first (a conditional) and excepts its consequent, yielding the negation of the antecedent. The author then considers cases based on the nature of the desired proposition (categorical or conditional). For a categorical desired proposition, the major may be categorical, conditional conjunctive, or conditional disjunctive. He provides concrete examples using logical variables (B, C, A, D, R). For a conditional desired proposition, he notes further subdivisions. The passage is dense but systematic, showing how reductio can be reduced to a direct syllogism plus an exceptive inference.

What is the role of the second (exceptive) syllogism in a reductio ad absurdum?
A) It produces the absurdity
B) It negates the antecedent from the falsehood of the consequent

D) It combines the premises of the first syllogism

Key Takeaway
A reductio ad absurdum is a compound argument consisting of a conjunctive syllogism that generates an absurdity, followed by an exceptive syllogism that denies the absurdity to affirm the original claim.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a reductio argument to prove that nabidh (date wine) is forbidden. Use a true premise: 'Every intoxicating substance is forbidden.' Assume the opposite, derive an absurdity, then apply the exceptive syllogism.
?
Identify the two component syllogisms in the following passage from al-Ghazali's Tahafut: 'If the world were eternal, the number of past revolutions would be infinite; but an infinite series cannot be traversed; therefore the present moment would never arrive—which is false; so the world is not eternal.'

Module 10 · Lesson 562
