The Syllogism
advanced
15 min
Definition and Structure of Qiyas al-Khalf (Reductio ad Absurdum)
Defines qiyas al-khalf, its composition, and examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two syllogisms that compose a qiyas al-khalf and reconstruct its reduction to a direct syllogism.

Core Idea
Qiyas al-khalf is a compound of conjunctive and exceptive syllogisms proving by contradiction.

The logicians of the Islamic tradition recognized that not all arguments follow the direct pattern of a single conjunctive or exceptive syllogism. One important compound form is qiyas al-khalf, the reductio ad absurdum. This lesson unpacks its structure: how it uses two syllogisms to prove a conclusion by showing that its negation leads to an absurdity. Understanding this will sharpen your ability to analyze proofs in kalam and usul al-fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the desired conclusion (al-matlub)
Determine what the argument aims to prove. This is the proposition whose truth is established by reductio.
2
Recognize the two parts: conjunctive and exceptive
The first part is a conjunctive syllogism (iqtirani) that, from assuming the falsity of the conclusion and a true major premise, derives an absurdity. The second part is an exceptive syllogism (istithna'i) that denies the absurd consequent to affirm the truth of the original conclusion.
The conjunctive syllogism's minor premise is a conditional: its antecedent assumes the negation of the conclusion, its consequent assumes the truth of the contradictory of the conclusion.
3
Trace the flow of absurdity
Follow how the assumption of the contradictory, combined with a true premise, leads to a logical impossibility (muhal). For example, in the first categorical example: assuming 'not every B is C' false leads to 'every B is C' true, then with 'every C is A' yields 'every B is A', but assuming also 'not every B is A' true gives a direct contradiction.
The absurdity is the conjunction of two contradictory propositions.
4
Apply modus tollens in the exceptive syllogism
The exceptive syllogism has the form: If P then Q, not Q, therefore not P. Here P is the assumption 'not-(conclusion)' and Q is the absurdity. Denying the absurdity (like 'it is false that both every B is A and not every B is A') yields the falsity of the assumption, thus proving the original conclusion.
This step reduces the reductio to a direct argument.
5
Practice with variations
The passage shows that the desired conclusion may be categorical or conditional, and the major premise of the conjunctive syllogism may be categorical, conditional conjunctive, or conditional disjunctive. Work through one example for each type to solidify the pattern.
For conditional conclusions, the structure adapts similarly: the absurdity follows from the assumption of the negation.

The fifth art: on the investigation of the syllogism of reductio ad absurdum (qiyas al-khalf) and reducing it to the direct, and indication of what distinguishes and shares between it and the direct and the converse of the syllogism. As for the syllogism of reductio (qiyas al-khalf), it is a syllogistic statement that demonstrates the correctness of the desired conclusion by invalidating its contradictory. It is composed of two syllogisms: one conjunctive (iqtirani) composed of two premises, its minor premise being a conditional conjunctive, its antecedent being the assumption of the falsity of the desired conclusion, and its consequent being the assumption of the truth of its contradictory; and its major premise being an assumed true proposition, either categorical or conditional, from whose conjunction with the consequent of the minor premise an absurdity follows. The other syllogism is exceptive (istithna'i) composed of two premises: a conditional conjunctive which is the result of the conjunctive syllogism, and an exceptive from the consequent of this conditional, resulting in the falsity of the antecedent itself. Then the desired conclusion is either categorical or conditional. If it is categorical, then the major premise in the conjunctive syllogism can be categorical or conditional, either disjunctive. Example: let the major be categorical, our saying: 'If not every B is C is false, then every B is C is true'; and let the assumed true categorical in this conjunctive be, for example, 'Every C is A'; and it results that if not every B is C is false, then every B is A is true. So if you say 'but not every B is A is true', then it follows that not every B is C is false. Another example: let the major be a conditional conjunctive, like our saying: 'And everything such that every B is C is true, then D is R is true'; it results that if not every B is C is false, then D is R is true. So if you say 'but D is R is not true', then it follows that it is not the case that not every B is C is false. Another example: let the major be a conditional disjunctive, our saying: 'And every B is C is either D or R'; then it follows that if not every B is C is false, then either D or R, but not either D or R, so it is not the case that not every B is C is false. And if the desired conclusion is conditional, then either conjunctive or disjunctive. If it is conjunctive, then the major of the conjunctive syllogism can also be categorical or conditional. If it is conditional, it can be conjunctive or disjunctive. Example: let the major be categorical, our saying: 'If not everything such that B is C then D is R is false, then everything such that B is C is R is true, and every D is R is K is L'; it results that if not everything such that B is C then D is R is false, then K is L; but K is L is not true, so it is not the case that not everything such that B is C then D is R is false. Another example: let the major be a conditional conjunctive, our saying: 'And everything such that if B is C then D is R is K is L'; it results that if not everything such that B is C then D is R is false, then K is L is true; but K is L is not true, so it is not the case that not everything such that B is C then D is R is false. Another example: let the major be a conditional disjunctive, our saying: 'And every D is R is either K or L'; it results that if not everything such that B is C then D is R is false, then either K or L; but not either K or L; so it is not the case that not everything such that B is C then D is R is false. This is the analysis of the reductio syllogism, and its modality is the elucidation of the desired conclusion from it. And sometimes convention omitted mentioning the result of the conjunctive, and only its consequent and the judgment that it is absurd are expressed, as when it is said: 'If not every B is C is false, then every B is C is true, and every C is A, so every B is A; but every B is A is absurd, so the antecedent is absurd.' And sometimes some people attempted to derive a reductio from the first conditional premise by positing it and judging its consequent to be absurd, then after that they contrived a syllogistic composition from it and a true premise, from which absurdity follows; then it is said: this consequent, together with something true, composes an absurdity, so it is absurd. And sometimes it is said: if this absurdity follows, it is either from the composition or from the material; but it is not from the composition because it is true; and if from the material, then from the major or minor; but it is not from the major because it is true, and a true premise does not imply an absurdity; so then if its following is only from the minor, then it is absurd, and there is no harm in that. In any case, the demonstration of the desired conclusion by the reductio syllogism is only complete if the absurdity follows from assuming its contradictory true. Hence it is called 'qiyas al-khalf' with fath al-kha' (the 'a' vowel), not with damm (the 'u' vowel), because it returns the discourse to the absurd; and 'khalf' (with fath) is a name for the absurd. As for 'khulf' with damm, it only occurs in promises (i.e., breaking promises). And it is not impossible that the reason for its naming is that it explains...
Source pages p. 445

Commentary
This passage defines and analyzes the syllogism known as qiyas al-khalf or reductio ad absurdum. The author begins by stating that this is the fifth art (al-fann al-khamis) concerning the investigation of qiyas al-khalf and its reduction to the direct syllogism (al-mustaqim), as well as the similarities and differences between them. The definition is then given: qiyas al-khalf is a syllogistic statement that proves the truth of a desired conclusion (al-matlub) by invalidating its contradictory (naqid). This is achieved by showing that assuming the opposite leads to an absurdity (muhal).

The structure is composed of two syllogisms. The first is a conjunctive syllogism (iqtirani) with two premises: a minor premise that is a conditional conjunctive (shartiyya muttasila), whose antecedent (muqaddam) assumes the falsity of the desired conclusion and whose consequent (tali) assumes the truth of its contradictory. The major premise (kubra) is a true proposition, either categorical (hamliyya) or conditional (shartiyya). From the conjunction of this major with the consequent of the minor, an absurdity follows. The second syllogism is an exceptive syllogism (istithna'i) composed of a conditional conjunctive (the result of the first) and an exceptive premise that denies the consequent, leading to the falsity of the antecedent (i.e., the original assumption of the falsity of the desired conclusion is false, so the desired conclusion is true).

The passage then considers cases where the desired conclusion is either categorical or conditional. For a categorical desired conclusion, the major premise in the conjunctive syllogism can be categorical, conditional conjunctive, or conditional disjunctive. Three examples are given using logical variables (Arabic letters ب، ج، أ, etc.): one with a categorical major, one with a conjunctive conditional major, and one with a disjunctive conditional major. Each example shows how the reductio proceeds: from assuming the falsity of the desired conclusion (e.g., 'not every B is C'), and using a true premise (e.g., 'every C is A'), an absurdity is derived (e.g., 'not every B is A' is both true and false), thus the assumption is false.

For a conditional desired conclusion (either conjunctive or disjunctive), similar variations are presented. The author then notes that sometimes in practice the result of the conjunctive syllogism is omitted, and only the conditional premise and the judgment that its consequent is absurd are stated directly. An example is given: 'If not every B is C is false, then every B is C is true, and every C is A, so every B is A; but every B is A is absurd, so the antecedent is absurd.'

The passage also discusses alternative formulations attempted by some scholars, where they take the first conditional premise and judge its consequent to be absurd, then construct a syllogism from that consequent and a true premise to derive an absurdity. This leads to an analysis of whether the absurdity arises from the composition or the material (the premises). Since the composition is valid and the major premise is true, the absurdity must be due to the minor premise (the assumption), which is therefore false.

Finally, the author explains the etymology of the term 'khalf' (with fath al-kha'). It means absurdity (muhal) because the argument returns (yariddu) to the absurd. The word with damm (khulf) means breaking promises and is not used in logic.

This passage is a detailed technical exposition of reductio ad absurdum in Aristotelian logic as received in the Islamic tradition, showing its composition and variations.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what are the two types of syllogisms that compose a qiyas al-khalf?
A) Direct and indirect syllogisms.
B) Conjunctive (iqtirani) and exceptive (istithna'i).
C) Categorical and conditional syllogisms.
D) Conjunctive and disjunctive syllogisms.

Key Takeaway
Qiyas al-khalf is a compound argument that proves a conclusion by showing its negation leads to an absurdity, using a conjunctive syllogism to derive the absurdity and an exceptive syllogism to reject the negation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Take the following categorical syllogism and rewrite it as a qiyas al-khalf: 'Every intoxicant is forbidden; every wine is an intoxicant; therefore, wine is forbidden.' Show the two syllogisms and the absurdity derived from assuming the contradictory.
?
Identify the qiyas al-khalf in this passage from a kalam text: 'If the world were not created, it would be eternal. But an eternal being cannot have a beginning. Yet we observe that the world has a beginning (change). Therefore, the world has a beginning, so it must be created.' Break it into the conjunctive and exceptive syllogisms.

Critique of Using Khalf to Prove Converses: Avoiding Multiple and Circular Results
Author critiques misuse of khalf in proving converses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to detect when a khalf argument for propositional conversion yields multiple converses and recognize the charge of circularity (dawr) in such arguments.

Core Idea
A single proposition has only one true converse; khalf cannot produce many without error.

In this passage, the author critiques the misuse of khalf (reduction to absurdity) for proving the converse of a proposition. He shows that applying khalf carelessly can produce three different converses for the same proposition, which is impossible. He then confronts the objection that this method leads to circular reasoning. This lesson unpacks his critique, helping you understand the proper limits of khalf in conversion proofs.

Guided Path
1
Locate the original proposition and its intended converse.
The original proposition is a perpetual negative: 'No B is C as long as it is B.' The author first attempts to prove by khalf the converse 'No C is B as long as it is B.'
2
Identify the alternative converses the khalf argument could support.
The author points out that another might claim the converse is 'No C is B' in the absolute general sense, or 'No C is B' in the general possible sense. Thus three converses appear from the same khalf procedure.
3
Determine why multiple converses are problematic.
A proposition has only one correct converse. The multiplicity arises because the first assumed converse is more specific and implies the more general ones, so the khalf argument for the specific also seems to prove the general ones, which is invalid.
4
Examine the circularity objection.
The objector says: to prove the general absolute converse by khalf, you need to assume the specific true converse, but that specific converse you would have to know already by another method, making the argument circular.
5
Assess the author's rebuttal.
The author rejects the objection because the specific converse is known independently, so no circle. He also gives an example where khalf works but does not include the true specific converse, showing that the validity of khalf does not guarantee the truth of the specific converse.

And what was said that khalf may sometimes support falsehood and other times truth, so it is not reliable nor depended upon. For if someone says that the converse of 'No B is C as long as it is B' is 'No C is B as long as it is B', otherwise its contradictory is true, and it is as we indicated (some B in some times of its being B, and if some C is B), then we can assume that C is that specific something, e.g., D, so D is described by C and B. Thus what is described by B has become described by C, and some of what is described by C is described by B, which is khalf; because it contradicts the assumed true proposition which is (No B is C as long as it is B). This absurdity only follows from assuming the contradictory of what we indicated of the converse, so it is false as before. So one may say rather its converse is 'No C is B' in the absolute (general), or else (some C is B always). Then we can assume that specific something, so the true and the perpetual would have come together in it, if only in some time, and that contradicts the true proposition. Rather, such is permissible if it is said that its converse is 'No C is B' in the general possible. Then it is not hidden that a single proposition has only one converse, and khalf has produced for this proposition three converses. The truth in this sentence is only one, the rest false. So it is only due to neglect of the fact that the first assumed converse is more specific than the two latter converses. And whoever knows that from what we indicated earlier, it will not be hidden that the truth of the more general follows from the truth of the more specific, and that everything that indicates the truth of the more specific in any manner indicates by that same manner the truth of the more general; necessarily, without conversion, whether by way of khalf or otherwise. The disagreement is only concerning the khalf syllogism, that whether what leads to the truth of the converse in the general absolute or the general possible is not necessary from the truth of the assumed specific converse, but rather is distinct and incompatible, which is impossible. So if it is not established in indicating the truth of the general as it indicates the truth of the specific, then at least it refutes it. If it is said: If one judges the continuity of khalf in explaining the converse of the general absolute and the general possible because the specific converse enters into them, then the believer in that has known the specific true converse before establishing khalf by another way. Then after that he claims its entry into the divisions of the absolute and the general possible. Then he judges the continuity of khalf in them because of its entry under them. Thus the converse whose correctness is sought by khalf becomes the corrector of khalf, which is an impossible circle. Also, considering the general absolute and the general possible in a way that includes all their divisions is one thing, and considering them in a way that covers only one division is another. Khalf has occurred in them, so the desired division is not determined. Also, if the converse were on this pattern, then all propositions would have a general possible converse; for no proposition has a converse but it falls under the general possible, and the burden of seeking the converse for every proposition would be dropped. And it is not hidden that every proposition that has a converse has a specific converse about which one researches. Then he claims to present an example that does not cut off the root of disagreement, in which the khalf syllogism continues, while the true converse is not included in it. He said: If we say (No B is C as long as it is B), its converse is (No C is B at some time of its being B, not perpetually as long as it is B). This proposition does not include the true specific converse under it. The explanation of that is: If this converse is not true, its contradictory is true, which is (Some B is C) at that specific time. Then at that very time, C is B and B is C, which contradicts the original proposition (No B is C as long as it is B), and that is khalf. So it has become clear that the validity of khalf is more general than the validity of what it is used for. So whenever what khalf is used for is valid, khalf is valid; but it is not itself convertible such that whenever khalf is valid, what it is used for is valid. We say: As for the first doubt, it is far from the taste of intellects, and an arbitrary claim, because the knowledge of the specific true converse is obtained by a way other than khalf, and it does not depend on parts...
Source pages p. 447

Commentary
This passage is a critical examination of the argument by reduction to absurdity (khalf) when used to prove the converse of a categorical proposition. The author begins by noting a common objection: khalf is unreliable because it can support both truth and falsehood. He then illustrates with a specific proposition: 'No B is C as long as it is B' (a perpetual negative). The intended converse according to the khalf procedure is 'No C is B as long as it is B', but the author shows that if we assume the contradictory, we derive a contradiction with the original, so the khalf seems to work. However, he then points out that another claimant might say the correct converse is 'No C is B' in the absolute general sense (without temporal restriction), or 'No C is B' in the general possible sense. Thus the same proposition would have three different converses via khalf, which is absurd because a proposition has only one true converse. The author argues that this multiplicity arises from neglecting that the first assumed converse is more specific, and that the more general follows from the more specific. He then addresses an objection that using khalf to prove the general absolute or general possible converse presupposes knowledge of the specific converse, leading to circular reasoning (دور). The objector argues that one must already know the specific converse to justify the khalf for the general, which is circular. The author responds that this objection is invalid because the specific converse is known through another method (not khalf). He also discusses that if khalf were to prove converses in this way, every proposition would have a general possible converse, which is false. Finally, he gives an example where the khalf procedure yields a converse that does not include the true specific converse (e.g., 'No C is B at some time' instead of 'No C is B perpetually'). He concludes that the validity of khalf is broader than the validity of its use; a valid use ensures khalf is valid, but not vice versa. The author then begins to refute the first objection, declaring it baseless.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does the author reject the charge of circularity (dawr) in using khalf to prove the general absolute converse?
A) Because khalf arguments are always valid.
B) Because the specific true converse is known independently, so no circle.
C) Because the general absolute converse is always true.
D) Because the author admits the argument is circular but considers it acceptable.

Key Takeaway
When proving converses by khalf, ensure that the assumed contradictory is unique and that the argument does not inadvertently produce multiple converses or rely on circular reasoning.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a khalf argument to prove the converse of 'Every intoxicant is forbidden' and then identify two alternative converses that could mistakenly be derived if the khalf is applied without proper constraints. Explain which converse is correct and why the others are false.
?
The objector in the passage claims that using khalf for the general absolute converse leads to circularity (dawr). Reconstruct this objection in your own words and then evaluate the author's response. Is the author's rebuttal convincing? Why or why not?

The Syllogism
intermediate
10 min
Refuting Three Objections Against the Khalf Syllogism
Author refutes three objections concerning the relationship between khalf syllogism and straight syllogism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze and refute three objections against the reduction of khalf (reductio ad absurdum) to the straight syllogism.

Core Idea
The khalf syllogism is logically sound and reducible; objections to it reveal misunderstandings.

This passage from Daqa'iq al-Haqa'iq presents three objections (تشكيكات) against the khalf syllogism and their refutations. The first objection claims circularity (دور), the second alleges that khalf overgeneralizes, and the third argues that if every converse is possible, the special converse is unnecessary. The author responds systematically, demonstrating the robustness of khalf. By studying this dialectical exchange, you will learn to defend a logical method against common criticisms.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three objections
List them from the passage: first (circularity), second (undetermined division), third (unnecessary special converse).
2
Understand the logical structure of each objection
What exactly is being claimed? For example, the first objection: khalf depends on knowing the special converse, which itself requires khalf.
3
Extract the author's response for each
How does the author counter? First: special converse known by another way. Second: khalf does not cover all divisions. Third: special converse has unique aspects.
4
Evaluate the strength of each refutation
Does the author successfully neutralize the objection? Consider whether the response is cogent.
5
Apply the same method to a new objection
Take a new objection, e.g., 'Khalf is redundant because all proofs can be done via straight syllogism,' and sketch a response.

الفن الخامس: في تحقيق قياس الخلف ورده إلى المستقيم
العام بسبب دخول العكس الخاص فيهما، فالمعتقد لذلك قد عرف العكس الخاص.
الحق قبل إقامة الخلف بطريق آخر، ثم بعد ذلك زعم دخوله في أقسام المطلق والممكن العامي، ثم حكم باستمرار الخلف فيهما بسبب دخوله تحتهما، فيكون العكس المطلوب صحته بالخلف مصححاً للخلف وهو دور محال.
وأيضاً فإن اعتبار المطلق العام والممكن العام على وجه يشملان جميع أقسامهما شيء، واعتبارهما على وجه لا يتناولان إلا قسماً واحداً شيء آخر، وقد قام الخلف فيهما فلا يتعين القسم المطلوب.
وأيضاً لو كان العكس على هذا السياق لكان عكس جميع القضايا ممكناً عاميّاً؛ إذ لا قضية توجد عكساً لأخرى، إلا وهي داخلة في الممكن العام، ولسقطت مؤونة طلب العكس في كل قضية.
ولا يخفى أن كل قضية تنعكس فإن لها عكساً خاصاً عنه يبحث، ثم زعم أنه يورد مثالاً لا يقطع به دابر الخلاف يستمر فيه قياس الخلف، مع كون العكس الحق غير داخل فيه، فقال إذا قلنا (لا شيء من "ب" "ج" ما دام "ب" إن عكسها (لا شيء من "ج" "ب" في وقت من أوقات كونه "ب" لا دائماً ما دام "ب" وهذه القضية لا يدخل العكس الحق الخاص تحتها.
في
وبيان ذلك أنه إن لم يصدق هذا العكس صدق نقيضه وهو (بعض "ب" "ج") ذلك الوقت المعين، فيكون في ذلك الوقت بعينه "ج" إما "ب" و "ب" إما "ج") وهو على خلاف القضية الأصلية وهي لا شيء من "ب" "ج" ما دام "ب") وهو خلف، فقد بان من هذا أن صحة الخلف أعم من صحة ما يستعمل فيه، فكل ما كان المستعمل فيه الخلف صحيحاً كان الخلف صحيحاً، ولا تنعكس كنفسه حتى يكون كل ما كان الخلف صحيحاً كان ما استعمل فيه صحيحاً.
قلنا: أما التشكيك الأول فبعيد عن مذاق العقول، وتحكّم في الدعوى، بأن معرفة العكس الخاص الحق حاصل بطريق غير الخلف، وليس يتوقف أجزاء الخلف في المطلق العام والممكن العام على معرف العكس الخاص أولاً، حتى يكون الخلف متوقفاً على معرفة ما لا يعرف إلا بالخلف، بل جاز إجراء ذلك فيهما، وإن كان العكس الخاص الحق مجهولاً.
نعم غاية ما يقدر أنه إذا صح أجزاء الخلف في بيان معنى الأخص جاز إجراؤه في بيان الأعم، وهو غير مخفض إلى الدور ، ثم إنه وإن توقف إجراء الخلف في المطلق العام والممكن العام على معرفة العكس الخاص والعكس الخاص مما لا يتبين له إلا بالخلف فليس ذلك بدور، إلا أن تكون أجزاء الخلف في بيان العكس الخاص الحق متوقفاً على معرفة صحة العكس الخاص الذي لا تبين له إلا بالخلف، أما إذا كان إجراؤه في المطلق والممكن العام متوقفاً على معرفة صحة عكس الأخص فلا.
وأما التشكيك الثاني: فمن النمط الأول في الفساد أيضاً، وذلك أن إفضاء الخلف إلى تصحيح المطلق العام والممكن العام ليس على وجه يتناول جميع أقسامهما معاً؛ إذ يستحيل الجمع بين كون الشيء الواحد مثلاً ضرورياً مطلقاً وممكناً خاصاً، كان لا بُدّي الوجود أم لا، بل ما ثبت من المطلق أو الممكن العام بالخلف أو بغيره ليس سوى شيء سلب عنه الجهات في اللفظ، ولا خروج له عن أن يكون متصفاً بواحد منها لا بجميعها، وليس اقتضاء الخلف إلى إثبات المطلقات أو الممكنات عملاً غير هذا الاعتبار.
ولا يخفى أن ذلك مما لا منافاة بينه وبين ما ثبت بالخلف أولاً.
سلك نظام
وأما التشكيك الثالث: فهو وإن أورد على أحسن تنميق فخارج عن التحقيق، وليس يلزم من كون عكس كل قضية داخلاً تحت الإطلاق والإمكان الأعم، وإلا اكتفى به عن معرفة العكس الخاص؛ إذ قد يتعلق بالخاص ما لا يتعلق بالعام فالبحث عن الخاص واجب، وإن كان لا خلو عن أن يكون داخلاً تحت هذا الأعم، كيف التوسع والتحجر بدعوى دخول عكس كل قضية تحت الإطلاق والإمكان العام مما يُشعر بجهل قائله وقلة تدربه، وضعف نظره، فإن عكس
The fifth art: on verifying the syllogism of khalf and reducing it to the straight syllogism. The common due to the entry of the special converse into them, so the believer in that has known the special converse. The truth before establishing the khalf by another way, then after that he claimed its entry into the divisions of the absolute and the common possible, then he judged the continuation of the khalf in them due to its entry under them, so the converse whose correctness is sought by khalf becomes a corrector of the khalf, and that is an impossible circularity. Also, considering the general absolute and general possible in a manner that encompasses all their divisions is one thing, and considering them in a manner that does not take except one division is another thing, and the khalf has occurred in them so the sought division is not determined. Also, if the converse were in this manner, then the converse of all propositions would be generally possible; for there is no proposition that exists as a converse of another except it is included in the general possible, and the burden of seeking the converse in every proposition would be dropped. And it is not hidden that every proposition that is converted has a special converse that is sought, then he claimed that he gives an example that does not cut off the root of disagreement in which the khalf syllogism continues, while the true converse is not included in it, so he said: If we say 'Nothing from B is C as long as B', its converse is 'Nothing from C is B at a time of its being B, not always as long as B' and this proposition does not have the true special converse included under it. In explanation of that: if this converse is not true, its contradictory is true, namely 'Some B is C' at that specific time, so at that very time C is either B and B is C, which is contrary to the original proposition, namely 'Nothing from B is C as long as B', and that is a khalf. So it has become clear from this that the correctness of khalf is more general than the correctness of what it is used in, so whenever what khalf is used in is correct, the khalf is correct, and it does not convert like itself so that whenever khalf is correct, what it is used in is correct. We said: As for the first doubt, it is far from the taste of intellects, and an arbitrary claim, because knowing the true special converse is obtained by a way other than khalf, and the parts of khalf in the general absolute and general possible do not depend on knowing the special converse first, so that khalf depends on knowing what is not known except by khalf. Rather, it is permissible to perform that in them, even if the true special converse is unknown. Yes, the utmost that can be estimated is that if the parts of khalf are sound in explaining the more specific meaning, it is permissible to perform it in explaining the more general, and that does not reduce to circularity. Then even if performing khalf in the general absolute and general possible depends on knowing the special converse, and the special converse is only shown by khalf, that is not circular, unless the parts of khalf in explaining the true special converse depend on knowing the correctness of the special converse which is only shown by khalf. But if performing it in the absolute and general possible depends on knowing the correctness of the converse of the more specific, then no. As for the second doubt: it is also of the first type in corruption, and that is because the khalf's leading to the correction of the general absolute and general possible is not in a manner that encompasses all their divisions together; for it is impossible to combine that one thing, for example, is absolutely necessary and specifically possible, whether it is necessary of existence or not. Rather, what is established of the absolute or general possible by khalf or otherwise is nothing except something from which the modalities are stripped in wording, and it does not exit from being qualified by one of them, not by all of them. And the khalf's requiring the establishment of absolutes or possibles is nothing other than this consideration. And it is not hidden that this is not contradictory to what was established by khalf first. Sulk Nizam. As for the third doubt: although it is presented in the best ornamentation, it is outside verification. It does not necessarily follow that because the converse of every proposition is included under the absolute and the more general possible, otherwise one would suffice with it over knowing the special converse; for there may pertain to the specific what does not pertain to the general, so research into the specific is obligatory, even if it is not free from being included under this general. How presumptuous and stony is the claim that the converse of every proposition is included under the absolute and the general possible, which indicates the ignorance of its speaker, his lack of training, and the weakness of his view. For the converse...
Source pages p. 448

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical defense of the khalf (reductio ad absurdum) syllogism and its reduction to the straight syllogism. The author first presents three objections against the khalf argument, then refutes them point by point. The first objection claims circularity: the special converse sought by khalf itself depends on khalf. The author responds that the special converse can be known by another method, and that the khalf performed using general absolute and general possible does not require prior knowledge of the special converse. The second objection argues that khalf in the general absolute and possible does not pinpoint the specific division needed. The author replies that the khalf does not claim to cover all divisions simultaneously; it merely abstracts from modalities, which does not contradict the earlier khalf. The third objection suggests that if every converse is possible, then the special converse is unnecessary. The author retorts that the special converse may have unique properties, and the claim of universal inclusion reflects ignorance. Throughout, the author uses the example of propositions involving 'B' and 'C' to illustrate the logical structure.

العكس الخاص
المطلق العام
الممكن العام
الدور

Key Takeaway
The khalf syllogism is not defective; objections against it can be refuted by showing that the special converse is knowable independently and that khalf does not overreach.

The Syllogism

Reducing Qiyas al-Khalf to Direct Syllogistic Figures
Author analyzes Qiyas al-Khalf and its reduction to direct syllogistic figures.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine which categorical syllogistic figures can be used to prove each of the four proposition types via reductio ad absurdum.

Core Idea
Qiyas al-Khalf is not a separate inference but a reduction to categorical syllogisms with specific figure restrictions.

In this passage, the author investigates the logical structure of reductio (Qiyas al-Khalf) and shows how it can be reduced to a direct categorical syllogism. He examines each type of conclusion (universal affirmative, etc.) and identifies the figures in which the reductio can be validly constructed. This practical analysis equips you to check the validity of indirect proofs in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conclusion type
Determine whether the desired conclusion (the matlub) is universal affirmative (e.g., 'Every J is B'), universal negative, particular affirmative, or particular negative.
The author treats these four types separately.
2
Find its contradictory
State the contradictory (naqid) of the conclusion. For universal affirmative, it is particular negative; for universal negative, particular affirmative; for particular affirmative, universal negative; for particular negative, universal affirmative.
Be mindful of temporal modalities as the author notes.
3
Check premise suitability in each figure
For each of the four figures, determine if the contradictory can serve as a minor or major premise while respecting figure rules (e.g., no particular major in figure 2, no two negatives, no two particulars).
The author explicitly rules out certain figures for certain conclusion types.
4
Verify the reductio structure
Ensure that the direct syllogism derived from the contradictory plus other premises actually yields a contradiction (impossible), and that the original conclusion follows.
The author notes that sometimes the contradictory alone is insufficient and additional steps are needed.

Commentary
This passage is an advanced analysis of the syllogism of al-khalf (reductio ad absurdum) and how to reduce it to a direct (mustaqim) syllogism. The author begins by discussing the relationship between possibility and propositions. He argues that affirmative propositions fall under 'general possibility' but not under the 'general absolute' or 'general possible' in a technical sense, because the general possible includes necessary propositions. The text then turns to the use of Qiyas al-Khalf in establishing the true converse (al-'aks al-haqiqi). The author criticizes an erroneous example, pointing out that the contradictory of a particular negative proposition (like 'Some J is not B at some time') is not simply its converse but must be carefully constructed. He explains that the contradictory must consider the temporal modality. Then, the core of the lesson: the author enumerates four types of conclusions (universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative, and particular negative) and determines which figures of the categorical syllogism can be used to derive them via reductio. For example, a universal affirmative cannot be proved by reductio in the first or fourth figure because its contradictory is a particular negative, which cannot serve as a proper premise in those figures. However, it can be proved in other figures using specific conjunctions (اقترانات). The author concludes by stating that all other types of conclusions can be proved by reductio from the four figures, giving the specific case of the universal negative and showing how its contradictory (a particular affirmative) can be used in the first and second figures. Throughout, the commentary should note that the author is assuming a context of modal logic (with 'perpetual' etc.) and is showing the technical constraints of reducing indirect proofs to direct categorical syllogisms.

Contradiction Proof for Universal Affirmative: Figure Constraints
Author explains limitations and specific moods for proving universal affirmative by contradiction

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which syllogistic figures and moods are valid for proving a universal affirmative by contradiction, and explain why first and fourth figures are invalid.

Core Idea
Proving a universal affirmative by contradiction only works in specific moods of the second and third figures.

When we attempt to prove a universal affirmative claim (e.g., 'All A is B') using proof by contradiction (qiyas al-khalf), we assume its contradictory (a particular negative) and try to derive a contradiction. However, not every syllogistic figure permits such a proof. This passage reveals the precise conditions: the first and fourth figures are useless for this purpose, while only certain moods in the second and third figures succeed. Understanding these constraints ensures that theological and legal demonstrations using khalf are logically sound.

Guided Path
1
Identify the desired claim and its contradictory
The claim to be proved is a universal affirmative (e.g., 'Every A is B'). Its contradictory is a particular negative ('Some A is not B'). In khalf, we assume this contradictory and try to derive a false conclusion.
2
Test feasibility in first figure
In the first figure, the minor premise must be affirmative and the major universal. But if we take the contradictory (particular negative) as a premise, it violates these rules. Even if we replace it with an opposite premise (e.g., 'No A is B'), the argument only proves that opposite false, not the original true, because both can be false.
The author calls the opposite 'al-ḍidd'.
3
Test feasibility in fourth figure
The fourth figure also fails because the contradictory (particular negative) cannot serve as a useful premise. Moreover, the same issue of 'both false' arises if we use an opposite premise.
Fourth figure has different mood constraints.
4
Determine valid approach in second figure
In the second figure, the contradictory (particular negative) can only be the minor premise. The other premise must be universal affirmative (since two negatives are forbidden). Only the fourth mood of the second figure (where the major is universal affirmative and the minor particular negative? Actually the author's numbering) allows a valid khalf argument.
The author refers to 'the fourth mood' of the second figure.
5
Determine valid approach in third figure
In the third figure, the contradictory (particular negative) must be the minor premise, and the other premise must be universal affirmative (avoiding two particulars or two negatives). Only the fifth mood of the third figure works.
Mood numbering varies by author; the key is the constraint.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا عرف ما حققناه في وجه التمسك بقياس الخلف فما يقصد بيانه به من المطالب إما كلي موجب أو كلي سالب أو جزئي موجب، أو جزئي سالب، فإن كان كلياً موجباً لم يمكن بيانه بالخلف على أن يكون الاقتران منه على نمط الشكل الأول والرابع؛ إذ نقيض الكلي الموجب جزئي سالب، وليس يمكن أن يجعل صغرى ولا كبرى في الرابع ؛ لكونه جزئياً سالباً، وإن أخذ الضد بحيث يمكن جعله كبرى في الأول، أو كبرى وصغرى في الرابع، فهو وإن أنتج، فليس يلزم منه غير كذب الضد، ولا يلزم منه صدق الآخر، الذي هو المطلوب؛ إذ ليس يمتنع اجتماع الضدين في الكذب على ما سبق بل بيانه إنما يكون مما بقي من الأشكال، لكن إن كان من الشكل الثاني فالنقيض فيه لا يكون إلا صغرى؛ إذ لا قياس في الثاني مما كبراه جزئية، والمضاف إليه لا يمكن إلا كلياً، ومع كليته لا يكون إلا موجباً؛ إذ لا قياس عن سالبتين، فليس يتبين بغير الاقتران الرابع منه، وإن كان من الشكل الثالث عما صغراه سالبة، والمضاف إليه لا يكون إلا كلياً؛ إذ لا قياس عن جزئيتين، ومع كونه كلياً فلا يكون إلا موجباً؛ إذ لا قياس عن سالبتين، فليس يتبين من الثالث بغير الاقتران الخامس منه، وإن أخذ الضد فيهما بحيث يكون البيان من غير هذه الاقترانات فقد بينا أنه غير مفيد.
And when what we have verified regarding the method of using the proof by contradiction is known, then what is intended to be demonstrated by it of the requested propositions is either a universal affirmative, or a universal negative, or a particular affirmative, or a particular negative. If it is a universal affirmative, it cannot be demonstrated by contradiction such that the conjunction from it is in the pattern of the first and fourth figures, because the contradictory of a universal affirmative is a particular negative, and it cannot be made a minor or major premise in the fourth due to its being particular and negative. And if the opposite is taken such that it can be made a major in the first, or a major and minor in the fourth, even if it produces a conclusion, it does not entail anything other than the falsehood of the opposite, and does not entail the truth of the other, which is the desired conclusion, because it is not impossible for two opposites to both be false as previously mentioned. However, if it is from the second figure, then the contradictory there is only a minor premise, because there is no syllogism in the second figure whose major premise is particular, and what is added to it (the contradictory) can only be universal, and while being universal it is only affirmative, because there is no syllogism from two negatives. So it is only demonstrated by the fourth mood of it. Commentary
This passage is a technical exposition on the use of proof by contradiction (qiyas al-khalf) to establish a universal affirmative conclusion. The author begins by recalling the general principle: the desired claim (matlab) to be proved by contradiction is one of the four types of categorical propositions: universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative, or particular negative. He then takes up the universal affirmative case. The key idea is that the contradictory of a universal affirmative is a particular negative. The author argues that this contradictory cannot serve as a premise in the first and fourth figures in a way that validly yields the desired universal affirmative. For the first figure, if the contradictory is taken as a major premise, it would be a particular negative, which is not allowed in the first figure's standard moods. For the fourth, it cannot be a minor or major due to its particularity and negativity. Even if one arranges the opposite premise (the opposite of the contradictory) such that it works in the first or fourth, the conclusion only shows the falsehood of the opposite premise, not the truth of the original claim, since two opposites can both be false. Therefore, the universal affirmative can only be proved by contradiction in the second and third figures, using specific moods: in the second figure, the only valid mood is the fourth (since the contradictory must be minor and universal affirmative added to it yields a negative conclusion? Actually the author specifies the 'fourth mood' of the second figure, which typically is Cesare? But the author numbers moods differently. Similarly, in the third figure, only the fifth mood works. Any attempt outside these is futile. The author's analysis shows a deep mastery of syllogistic constraints.

Proving a Universal Negative by Contradiction Across All Four Figures
Author details how to prove a universal negative by contradiction in each figure with specific moods

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the valid moods for proving a universal negative by contradiction in each of the four figures, based on the constraints on the added premise.

Core Idea
Proof by contradiction reduces to a direct syllogism; the figure chosen determines which moods are valid.

In this passage, the author systematically describes how to prove a universal negative proposition (kullī sālib) using proof by contradiction (qiyās al-khalf). The key is that the contradictory of a universal negative is a particular affirmative, which can be placed as a premise in each of the four figures. However, each figure imposes specific conditions on the other premise (the muḍāf ilayh), restricting which moods are valid. By understanding these conditions, you can map any khalf argument onto a direct syllogism, verifying its logical soundness.

Guided Path
1
Identify the contradictory of the universal negative
The contradictory (naqiḍ) of a universal negative proposition (e.g., 'No S is P') is a particular affirmative ('Some S is P'). This contradictory becomes one premise in the khalf argument.
Remember: universal negative (E) vs particular affirmative (I).
2
Determine the role of the contradictory in the chosen figure
The contradictory can be placed as either the minor or major premise, depending on the figure. For each figure, the author specifies whether it can be minor, major, or both.
In the first and second figures, it is only minor; in the third, both; in the fourth, both.
3
Identify constraints on the added premise (muḍāf ilayh)
The other premise needed to form a syllogism must be universal, and its quality (affirmative or negative) is constrained by the figure. For example, in the second figure it must be negative, in the fourth it depends on the role of the contradictory.
Review the figure's general rules: e.g., two affirmatives invalid in second figure; negative minor invalid in third.
4
List the valid moods for each figure
Based on the constraints, the author enumerates which moods (iqtirānāt) are valid in each figure for the khalf proof. For the first figure, it is the 'other two' moods (likely Ferio and Celarent); second: only the third mood (Festino?); third: three moods (second, third, sixth); fourth: two moods (third and fifth).
The exact numbering of moods follows the author's convention. Cross-check with standard mood lists if needed.
5
Apply the method to a concrete example from Islamic sciences
Take a universal negative proposition from a discipline like fiqh or kalām. Construct its contradictory, choose a figure, add the appropriate universal premise, and verify that the resulting syllogistic mood matches one of those listed by the author.
This step consolidates the theoretical constraints into a logical construction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفن الخامس: في تحقيق قياس الخلف ورده إلى المستقيم. وأما الكلي السالب فلأن نقيضه جزئي موجب، وهو يمكن أن يكون في الشكل الأول صغرى، وما يضاف إليه يجب أن يكون كلياً إما موجباً وإما سالباً، فبيانه فيه يكون بالاقترانين الآخرين. وكذا أيضاً يمكن أن يجعل صغرى من الثاني، وما يضاف إليه يجب أيضاً أن يكون كلياً؛ إذ لا قياس عن جزئيتين، ومع كونه كلياً يجب أن يكون سالباً؛ إذ لا قياس فيه عن موجبتين في غير المختلطات، فبيانه فيه لا يكون بغير الاقتران الثالث منه، وأما في الشكل الثالث فممكن أن يكون صغرى وكبري، فإن كان صغرى فما يضاف إليه يجب أن يكون كلياً إما موجباً وإما سالباً، وإن كان كبرى فيجب أن يكون ما يضاف إليه كلياً؛ إذ لا قياس عن جزئيتين، ومع كونه كلياً موجباً؛ إذ لا قياس في الثالث عما صغراه سالبة، فإذن بيانه في الثالث ثلاثة اقترانات بالثاني والثالث والسادس وأما في الرابع فقد يكون النقيض فيه صغرى، وقد يكون كبرى، فإن كان كبرى فالمضاف إليه لا يكون إلا كلياً موجباً، وإن كان صغرى فالمضاف إليه لا يكون إلا كلياً سالباً على ما عرف فبيانه فيه لا يكون إلا باقترانين وهما الثالث والخامس.
The Fifth Art: On Verifying the Proof by Contradiction and Reducing it to Direct Syllogisms. As for the universal negative, because its contradictory is a particular affirmative, and it (the contradictory) can be a minor premise in the first figure, and what is added to it must be universal, either affirmative or negative, so its demonstration in it (the first figure) is by the other two moods. Likewise, it can also be made a minor premise in the second figure, and what is added to it must also be universal, because there is no syllogism from two particulars, and while it is universal it must be negative, because there is no syllogism in it from two affirmatives in non-mixed ones, so its demonstration in it is only by the third mood of it. As for the third figure, it can be either minor or major: if it is minor, what is added to it must be universal, either affirmative or negative; and if it is major, then what is added to it must be universal, because there is no syllogism from two particulars, and while it is universal it is affirmative, because there is no syllogism in the third from a negative minor. So its demonstration in the third is by three moods: the second, third, and sixth. As for the fourth figure, the contradictory can be either minor or major: if it is major, what is added to it is only a universal affirmative; and if it is minor, what is added to it is only a universal negative, as known. So its demonstration in it is only by two moods: the third and fifth.

Commentary
This passage is a detailed analysis of how to prove a universal negative (kulli salib) proposition using proof by contradiction (qiyas al-khalf). The author first notes that the contradictory of a universal negative is a particular affirmative. This contradictory can be used as a premise in all four figures, but each figure imposes constraints on the other premise (the 'added' premise) and the valid moods. In the first figure, the contradictory is taken as minor, and the added premise must be universal (either affirmative or negative), leading to the 'other two moods' of the first figure (presumably Ferio and Celarent? Actually the first figure has four moods: Barbara, Celarent, Darii, Ferio. The 'other two' after excluding the ones that don't apply? The author says 'the other two' which likely means Ferio and Celarent? But he says 'الاقترانين الآخرين' — the other two moods, implying that only two of the four are usable. In the second figure, the contradictory is also minor, but the added premise must be universal and negative (since two affirmatives are not allowed in pure categoricals), so only the third mood (Camestres? Actually second figure moods: Cesare, Camestres, Festino, Baroco. The third mood is Festino? Or depending on numbering). The author says 'الاقتران الثالث' — the third mood. In the third figure, the contradictory can be either minor or major, leading to three valid moods: the second, third, and sixth (third figure moods: Darapti, Disamis, Datisi, Felapton, Ferison, etc. Numbering varies). In the fourth figure, the contradictory can be major or minor, each requiring a specific type for the added premise, resulting in two moods: third and fifth (fourth figure moods: Bramantip, Camenes, Dimaris, Fesapo, Fresison. Third mood is probably Camenes? Fifth is Fresison?). This passage shows the author's intent to systematically map the proof by contradiction onto the standard syllogistic forms.

Proving a Particular Affirmative by Contradiction Across Figures
Author explains how to prove a particular affirmative by contradiction in each figure with appropriate moods

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the valid moods for proving a particular affirmative by contradiction in each of the four syllogistic figures.

Core Idea
The contradictory of a particular affirmative must be strategically placed as a premise to yield a valid syllogism in each figure.

In this lesson, we continue the study of proof by contradiction (qiyas al-khalf). The author examines how to prove a particular affirmative proposition (juz'iyy mawjib) by assuming its contradictory, a universal negative. He systematically shows which moods in each figure will produce a valid syllogism when the contradictory is used as a premise, depending on its position and the type of additional premise. Understanding this enumeration is crucial for constructing valid indirect proofs in logic and Islamic legal reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the contradictory of the particular affirmative
The contradictory of 'Some S is P' is 'No S is P' (universal negative). This is the premise we assume to prove the original by contradiction.
2
Analyze the first figure
In the first figure, the contradictory (universal negative) must be the major premise. The minor premise must be affirmative (either universal or particular). This corresponds to two moods: the second and fourth moods in the author's numbering (likely Celarent and Ferio).
3
Analyze the second figure
In the second figure, the contradictory can be either the minor or the major premise. If it is the minor, the added premise must be a universal affirmative. If it is the major, the added premise must be affirmative (universal or particular). This yields three moods: the first, second, and third moods (e.g., Cesare, Camestres, Festino).
4
Analyze the third figure
In the third figure, the contradictory serves only as the major premise. The added premise must be affirmative (universal or particular). This gives two moods: the fourth and sixth moods (e.g., Felapton and Ferison).
5
Analyze the fourth figure
In the fourth figure, the contradictory can be either minor or major. If it is minor, the major must be a universal affirmative. If it is major, the minor may be universal or particular affirmative. This yields three moods: the first, fourth, and fifth moods (e.g., Bramantip, Fesapo, Fresison).
The mood numbering follows the author's specific system; the key is the pattern of placement and added premise restrictions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الجزئي الموجب فلأن نقيضه كلي سالب وهو في الشكل الأول أن يجعل كبرى، والمضاف إليه يجب أن يكون موجباً إما كلياً وإما جزئياً، فبيانه فيه أن يكون بالاقتران الثاني والرابع. وأما في الشكل الثاني، فيمكن أن يجعل صغرى وكبرى، لكن إن كان صغرى فالمضاف إليه يجب أن يكون كلياً موجباً، وإن كان كبرى فالمضاف إليه لا يكون إلا موجباً؛ لكونه قد يكون كلياً، وقد يكون جزئياً، فبيانه فيه بثلاثة اقترانات وهي الأول والثاني والثالث. وأما في الشكل الثالث فلأنه يمكن أن يكون كبرى والمضاف إليه يجب أن يكون موجباً إما كلياً وإما جزئياً، فبيانه فيه باقترانين وهما الرابع والسادس. وأما في الشكل الرابع، فلأنه يمكن أن يكون صغرى أو كبرى، لكن إن كان صغرى فكبراه لا تكون إلا كليّة موجبة، وإن كان كبرى فصغراه قد تكون كلية موجبة، وقد تكون جزئية موجبة، فبيانه فيه بثلاثة اقترانات وهي الأول والرابع والخامس.
As for the particular affirmative, because its contradictory is a universal negative, and it (the contradictory) in the first figure is made a major premise, and what is added to it must be affirmative, either universal or particular, so its demonstration in it (the first figure) is by the second and fourth moods. As for the second figure, it can be made minor or major, but if it is minor, what is added to it must be a universal affirmative; and if it is major, what is added to it is only affirmative, because it may be universal or particular. So its demonstration in it is by three moods: the first, second, and third. As for the third figure, because it can be a major, and what is added to it must be affirmative, either universal or particular, so its demonstration in it is by two moods: the fourth and sixth. As for the fourth figure, because it can be minor or major, but if it is minor, its major is only universal affirmative; and if it is major, its minor may be universal affirmative or particular affirmative. So its demonstration in it is by three moods: the first, fourth, and fifth.

The Syllogism

15 min
Valid Placement of the Contradictory in Reductio Syllogisms
Author enumerates valid placements of the contradictory in each figure for different proposition types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine which moods (iqtiranat) are valid for placing the contradictory in a reductio syllogism for any given figure and proposition type.

Core Idea
Only specific moods allow the contradictory to produce a valid reductio; the contrary cannot guarantee the conclusion.

This passage details the precise conditions under which the contradictory (naqid) of the desired conclusion can be used in a reductio ad absurdum syllogism. Understanding these conditions is crucial for constructing valid proofs in Islamic logic, especially in Kalam and Usul al-Fiqh where reductio arguments are common. We will walk through the rules for each figure and proposition type.

Guided Path
1
Identify the proposition type
The passage considers three types: universal negative, particular affirmative, and particular negative. The contradictory of each is given (e.g., universal negative's contradictory is particular affirmative).
The universal affirmative is omitted; it is straightforward as its contradictory is particular negative.
2
Determine possible placements per figure
For each figure (first to fourth), the contradictory can be minor or major premise. The passage specifies which placements are possible.
3
Note restrictions on the other premise (al-mudaf ilayh)
The other premise must satisfy certain quantity and quality conditions (e.g., universal, affirmative) to avoid invalid syllogisms (e.g., from two particulars or two negatives).
4
Compile the valid moods (iqtiranat)
The passage lists which of the standard moods (first through sixth) are valid for each configuration. This gives a precise enumeration.
Moods are numbered in the tradition; the author assumes familiarity.
5
Distinguish contradictory from contrary
The passage warns that using the opposite (didd) instead of the contradictory, even if it leads to absurdity, does not prove the sought conclusion because the logical relation is different.

وكبري، فإن كان صغرى فما يضاف إليه يجب أن يكون كلياً إما موجباً وإما سالباً، وإن كان كبرى فيجب أن يكون ما يضاف إليه كلياً؛ إذ لا قياس عن جزئيتين، ومع كونه كلياً موجباً؛ إذ لا قياس في الثالث عما صغراه سالبة، فإذن بيانه في الثالث ثلاثة اقترانات بالثاني والثالث والسادس وأما في الرابع فقد يكون النقيض فيه صغرى، وقد يكون كبرى، فإن كان كبرى فالمضاف إليه لا يكون إلا كلياً موجباً، وإن كان صغرى فالمضاف إليه لا يكون إلا كلياً سالباً على ما عرف فبيانه فيه لا يكون إلا باقترانين وهما الثالث والخامس. وأما الجزئي الموجب فلأن نقيضه كلي سالب وهو في الشكل الأول أن يجعل كبرى، والمضاف إليه يجب أن يكون موجباً إما إلا موجباً؛ لكونه قد يكون كلياً، وقد يكون جزئياً، فبيانه فيه بثلاثة اقترانات وهي الأول والثاني والثالث. وأما في الشكل الثالث فلأنه يمكن أن يكون كبرى والمضاف إليه يجب أن يكون موجباً إما كلياً وإما جزئياً، فبيانه فيه باقترانين وهما الرابع والسادس. وأما في الشكل الرابع، فلأنه يمكن أن يكون صغرى أو كبرى، لكن إن كان صغرى فكبراه لا تكون إلا كليّة موجبة، وإن كان كبرى فصغراه قد تكون كلية موجبة، وقد تكون جزئية موجبة، فبيانه فيه بثلاثة اقترانات وهي الأول والرابع والخامس. وأما الجزئي السالب فلأن نقيضه كلي موجب وهو قد يمكن أن يكون في الأشكال الأربعة صغرى وكبرى، لكن إن كان صغرى من الأول فكبراه لا سالبة، إلا أن تكون الجهات غير متداخلة، فيجوز أن تكون موجبة على ما عرف وإن كان كبرى فصغراه أيضاً لا تكون إلا سالبة على الشرط المذكور إما كليّة وإما جزئية، فبيانه فيه بثلاثة اقترانات وهي الأول والثاني والرابع. وأما إن كان صغرى من الثالث فكبراه قد تكون موجبة إما كليّة وإما جزئية وقد تكون سالبة إما كليّة وإما جزئية، وإن كان كبرى فصغراه لا تكون إلا موجبة إما كليّة وإما جزئية، فبيانه فيه بخمس اقترانات وهي الأول والثاني والثالث والرابع والخامس، وإن كان صغرى من الرابع فكبراه قد تكون كلية موجبة، وقد تكون سالبة وقد تكون جزئية موجبة وإن كان كبرى فصغراه قد تكون كلية موجبة وقد تكون كلية سالبة، فبيانه منه بأربعة اقترانات وهي الأول والثاني والثالث والرابع. وأما إن أخذ الضد بدل النقيض في جميع هذه المطالب بحيث يمكن أن يكون من غير ما عددناه من الاقترانات فهو وإن لزم منه إحالة الضد المأخوذ فليس يلزم منه صدق المطلوب على ما عرف.
It must be universal; for there is no syllogism from two particulars, and being universal, it must be negative; for there is no syllogism from two affirmatives in non-mixed [moods], so its demonstration in it is only by the third mood. As for the third figure, it can be minor or major. If it is minor, what is added to it must be universal, either affirmative or negative. If it is major, what is added to it must be universal; for there is no syllogism from two particulars, and being universal, it must be affirmative; for there is no syllogism in the third [figure] when its minor is negative. So its demonstration in the third [figure] is by three moods: the second, third, and sixth. As for the fourth [figure], the contradictory can be minor or major. If it is major, what is added to it is only universal affirmative. If it is minor, what is added to it is only universal negative, as known, so its demonstration in it is only by two moods: the third and fifth. As for the particular affirmative, its contradictory is universal negative, and in the first figure it is made major, and what is added to it must be affirmative, either universal or particular. So its demonstration in it is by the second and fourth moods. As for the second figure, it can be made minor or major, but if it is minor, what is added to it must be universal affirmative; if it is major, what is added to it is only affirmative, because it may be universal or particular. So its demonstration in it is by three moods: the first, second, and third. As for the third figure, it can be major, and what is added to it must be affirmative, either universal or particular. So its demonstration in it is by two moods: the fourth and sixth. As for the fourth figure, it can be minor or major, but if it is minor, its major is only universal affirmative; if it is major, its minor may be universal affirmative or particular affirmative. So its demonstration in it is by three moods: the first, fourth, and fifth. As for the particular negative, its contradictory is universal affirmative, and it can be in the four figures as minor or major. But if it is minor in the first, its major is only universal, either affirmative or negative; if it is major, its minor is only affirmative, either universal or particular. So its demonstration in it is by three moods: the first, second, and third. If it is minor in the second, its major is only universal negative, unless the modalities are non-overlapping, then it may be affirmative as known; if it is major, its minor is also only negative under the stated condition, either universal or particular. So its demonstration in it is by three moods: the first, second, and fourth. If it is minor in the third, its major may be affirmative, either universal or particular, or negative, either universal or particular; if it is major, its minor is only affirmative, either universal or particular. So its demonstration in it is by five moods: the first, second, third, fourth, and fifth. If it is minor in the fourth, its major may be universal affirmative, or negative, or particular affirmative; if it is major, its minor may be universal affirmative or universal negative. So its demonstration in it is by four moods: the first, second, third, and fourth. As for taking the opposite instead of the contradictory in all these inquiries such that it can be by other than the moods we enumerated, even if it entails the absurdity of the opposite taken, it does not entail the truth of the sought conclusion, as known.
Source pages p. 452

Commentary
This passage is a detailed enumeration of the valid moods (iqtiranat) for using the contradictory (naqid) in a reductio ad absurdum (khalf) syllogism. The author considers each of the four figures of the categorical syllogism and for each type of proposition (universal negative, particular affirmative, particular negative) specifies how the contradictory should be placed (as minor or major premise) and what restrictions apply to the other premise (al-mudaf ilayh). The underlying principle is that the reductio works by assuming the contradictory of the desired conclusion and combining it with a true premise to derive an absurdity; the conditions ensure the syllogism is valid. The author also notes that using the opposite (didd) instead of the contradictory does not guarantee the truth of the sought conclusion, even if it leads to an absurdity, because the logical relation is different. This is a technical exposition meant for advanced students of logic who are familiar with the figures and moods.

Key Terms
النقيض
الخلف
الاقتران
الضد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The validity of a reductio syllogism depends on correctly placing the contradictory (not the contrary) in specific moods allowed for each figure and proposition type.

The Syllogism
intermediate
8 min
Converting Reductio to Direct Syllogism
Author explains the method to convert a reductio syllogism into a direct one.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to convert a reductio ad absurdum syllogism into a direct categorical syllogism by taking the contradictory of the absurd conclusion and conjoining it with the true premise.

Core Idea
Reductio can be transformed into a direct syllogism using the contradictory of the absurd result as a premise.

In logical demonstrations, reductio ad absurdum (khalf) is a powerful tool, but sometimes we need a direct (mustaqim) proof. This passage from al-Daqa'iq al-Haqa'iq teaches a systematic method: take the contradictory (naqid) of the absurd conclusion derived in the reductio, combine it with the true premise that was used, and you will obtain the original sought conclusion. This shows the unity of logical form beneath different proof strategies.

Guided Path
1
Identify the reductio structure
Find the assumption (the contradictory of the sought conclusion) and the true premise(s) that lead to an absurd result. In the example, the sought is 'Not every B is J'; the assumption is 'Every B is J'; the true premise is 'Every J is A'; the absurd result is 'Every B is A' which contradicts the known truth 'Not every B is A'.
2
Take the contradictory of the absurd conclusion
The absurd conclusion (al-natija al-muhala) is the one that contradicts a known true premise. Its contradictory (naqid) will be a true premise. In the example, the absurd conclusion is 'Every B is A', so its contradictory is 'Not every B is A' – which is the true premise already used.
3
Conjoin the contradictory with the other true premise
Pair the contradictory (now a true premise) with the other true premise from the reductio. In the example, we take 'Not every B is A' and conjoin it with 'Every J is A'.
4
Derive the sought conclusion
From these two premises, deduce the original sought conclusion. In the example, from 'Not every B is A' and 'Every J is A' we infer 'Not every B is J', which is the sought. This is a valid categorical syllogism (Baroco in the second figure).
5
Check the mood and figure
Identify the mood and figure of the direct syllogism. The major premise is 'Every J is A' (universal affirmative), the minor is 'Some B is not A' (particular negative), and the conclusion is 'Some B is not J' (particular negative). This is the Baroco mood (second figure).
The author notes that if the reductio conclusion is universal affirmative, the direct syllogism must be from the fourth mood (i.e., a different figure).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعند تحقق ما أشرنا إليه فاعلم أن الخلف قد يمكن رده إلى المستقيم بأخذ نقيض النتيجة المحالة واقترانها بالمقدمة الحقة الصادقة فإنه ينتج عين المطلوب وذلك كما لو كان المطلوب مثلاً ( ليس كل "ب" "ج") فقلت إن كان ليس كل "ب" "ج") فـ (كل "ب" "ج" ) و(كل "ج" "أ") فـ (كل "ب" "أ" ) لكن ليس كل "ب" "أ") "ج") و(كل "ج" "أ") فـ (ليس كل "ب" "ج") وهو عين المطلوب، لكن إن كانت نتيجة الاقتران الخلفي كليّة موجبة فقياسه المستقيم لا يكون من غير الاقتران الرابع من
And when what we have indicated is verified, know that the reductio can be reduced to the direct [syllogism] by taking the contradictory of the absurd result and conjoining it with the true correct premise, for it produces the very sought [conclusion]. This is like if the sought was, for example, 'Not every B is J.' So you say: 'If not every B is J, then (every B is J) and (every J is A), so (every B is A). But (not every B is A) and (every J is A), so (not every B is J)' — which is the very sought. But if the conclusion of the reductio conjunction is universal affirmative, then its direct syllogism is only from the fourth mood of...
Source pages p. 453

Commentary
This passage explains how to convert a reductio ad absurdum (khalf) argument into a direct (mustaqim) syllogism. The key step is to take the contradictory (naqid) of the absurd conclusion (al-natija al-muhala) that was derived in the reductio, and then conjoin it with the true premise (al-muqaddima al-haqqa al-sadiqa) that was used. This new combination yields the original sought conclusion (matlub). The author provides a concrete example using the logical variables B, J, A. The sought conclusion is 'Not every B is J.' The reductio assumes its contradictory ('Every B is J') and combines with 'Every J is A' to get 'Every B is A,' which contradicts the known true 'Not every B is A.' Then by taking the contradictory of that absurd result ('Not every B is A' is already the contradictory of 'Every B is A') and conjoining it with 'Every J is A,' we derive 'Not every B is J,' which is the original sought conclusion. The author then notes that if the conclusion of the reductio conjunction is universal affirmative, the direct syllogism must be from the fourth mood (but the text cuts off). This is a practical method for transforming a reductio proof into a direct categorical syllogism.

Key Terms
الخلف
المستقيم
النتيجة المحالة
المقدمة الحقة الصادقة

C) Every B is J (كل ب ج)
D) Not every B is J (ليس كل ب ج)

Key Takeaway
A reductio ad absurdum can be systematically converted into a direct categorical syllogism by using the contradictory of the absurd conclusion as a premise with the other original premise.

Conditions on Premises in the Four Figures
Author details the constraints on major and minor premises in each figure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the quantity and quality constraints on minor and major premises in each figure of the categorical syllogism.

Core Idea
The premises of each syllogistic figure have fixed constraints that determine valid moods.

In this passage, the author explains the necessary conditions on the minor and major premises for each of the four figures. These constraints are not arbitrary but follow from the position of the middle term and the requirement of a valid conclusion. Understanding these conditions allows you to assess any given syllogism's potential validity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the figure and premise position
Determine which figure (e.g., first) and whether the given premise is the minor (صغرى) or major (كبرى).
The figure is determined by the position of the middle term in the premises.
2
Apply the condition from the passage
For the first figure: if the minor is from the first, the major must be universal; if the major is from the first, the minor must be affirmative.
These conditions are necessary for the syllogism to yield a valid conclusion.
3
Check quantity and quality
Note the required quantity (universal or particular) and quality (affirmative or negative) for the premise.
For example, in the second figure, if the minor is from the second, the major must be universal negative (unless modalities overlap).
4
Recognize exceptions and limitations
Be aware of special cases, such as non-overlapping modalities in the second figure, and note that using the opposite (ضد) instead of the contradictory (نقيض) does not guarantee truth.
The opposite is a stronger contrary, not a direct contradiction, so the inference fails.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأشكال الأربعة صغرى وكبرى، لكن إن كان صغرى من الأول فكبراه لا تكون إلا كليّة إما موجبة وإما سالبة وإن كان كبرى فصغراه لا تكون إلا موجبة إما كليّة وإما جزئية، فبيانه فيه بالاقترانات الثلاثة الأول والثاني والثالث، وإن كان صغرى من الثاني فكبراه لا تكون إلا كلية سالبة، إلا أن تكون الجهات غير متداخلة، فيجوز أن تكون موجبة على ما عرف وإن كان كبرى فصغراه أيضاً لا تكون إلا سالبة على الشرط المذكور إما كليّة وإما جزئية، فبيانه فيه بثلاثة اقترانات وهي الأول والثاني والرابع. وأما إن كان صغرى من الثالث فكبراه قد تكون موجبة إما كليّة وإما جزئية وقد تكون سالبة إما كليّة وإما جزئية، وإن كان كبرى فصغراه لا تكون إلا موجبة إما كليّة وإما جزئية، فبيانه فيه بخمس اقترانات وهي الأول والثاني والثالث والرابع والخامس، وإن كان صغرى من الرابع فكبراه قد تكون كلية موجبة، وقد تكون سالبة وقد تكون جزئية موجبة وإن كان كبرى فصغراه قد تكون كلية موجبة وقد تكون كلية سالبة، فبيانه منه بأربعة اقترانات وهي الأول والثاني والثالث والرابع. وأما إن أخذ الضد بدل النقيض في جميع هذه المطالب بحيث يمكن أن يكون من غير ما عددناه من الاقترانات فهو وإن لزم منه إحالة الضد المأخوذ فليس يلزم منه صدق المطلوب على ما عرف.
The four figures have minor and major [premises]. However, if the minor is from the first figure, its major can only be universal, either affirmative or negative; and if the major is from the first figure, its minor can only be affirmative, either universal or particular. Its exposition is in three moods: first, second, third. If the minor is from the second figure, its major can only be universal negative, except when modalities do not overlap; then it may be affirmative, as known. And if the major is from the second figure, its minor also can only be negative under the mentioned condition, either universal or particular. Its exposition is in three moods: first, second, fourth. As for if the minor is from the third figure, its major may be affirmative (universal or particular) or negative (universal or particular); and if the major is from the third figure, its minor can only be affirmative (universal or particular). Its exposition is in five moods: first, second, third, fourth, fifth. And if the minor is from the fourth figure, its major may be universal affirmative, or negative, or particular affirmative; and if the major is from the fourth figure, its minor may be universal affirmative or universal negative. Its exposition is in four moods: first, second, third, fourth. But if one takes the opposite instead of the contradictory in all these demands such that it could be from other than the moods we enumerated, then even though it entails the absurdity of the taken opposite, it does not entail the truth of the sought conclusion, as known.
Source pages p. 453

Commentary
This passage details the constraints on the quantity and quality of the minor and major premises when each premise belongs to a specific figure. The author systematically enumerates five cases: when the minor (or major) is from the first, second, third, or fourth figure. For example, if the minor premise is from the first figure, the major must be universal (affirmative or negative). If the major is from the first figure, the minor must be affirmative. These conditions reduce the valid mood patterns. The author also notes that using the opposite (ضد) instead of the contradictory (نقيض) does not guarantee truth, even if it leads to an absurdity. This is because the opposite is a stronger contrary, not a direct contradiction, so the inference is not logically sound. Throughout, the exposition refers to specific moods (e.g., first, second, third) that follow from these constraints.
