Core Method: Reducing Khalf to a Direct Syllogism
Author explains the basic method of khalf reduction with an example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to convert a proof by contradiction into a direct categorical syllogism by taking the contradictory of the absurd conclusion and conjoining it with a true premise.

Core Idea
Every khalf can be reduced to a direct syllogism by flipping the absurd conclusion.

Proof by contradiction (khalf) is a powerful tool in Islamic logic, but it can always be transformed into a direct syllogism (mustaqim). This lesson shows you the core technique: take the contradictory of the absurd conclusion and combine it with a true premise to directly derive the desired conclusion. We'll work through a concrete example to see how it works.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original sought conclusion
Determine what you are trying to prove. This is the proposition that will eventually be derived directly.
In the example, the sought conclusion is 'Not every B is J' (a particular negative).
2
Form the indirect proof (khalf) by assuming its opposite
Assume the contradictory of the sought conclusion. Combine this assumption with a true premise to derive an absurd result (a proposition known to be false).
Assume 'Every B is J' and combine with 'Every J is A' to get 'Every B is A', which contradicts the true premise 'Not every B is A'.
3
Extract the absurd conclusion from the khalf
Identify the proposition that caused the contradiction. In the example, the absurd conclusion is 'Every B is A' (universal affirmative).
This absurd conclusion is the one that contradicts a known truth.
4
Take the contradictory of the absurd conclusion
Negate the absurd conclusion to obtain its logical opposite. This will be used as a premise in the direct syllogism.
The contradictory of 'Every B is A' is 'Not every B is A' (particular negative).
5
Conjoin the contradictory with the original true premise
Combine the proposition from step 4 with the true premise that was used in the khalf. This new syllogism will produce the sought conclusion directly.
Combine 'Not every B is A' with 'Every J is A' to deduce 'Not every B is J', which is the original sought conclusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعند تحقق ما أشرنا إليه فاعلم أن الخلف قد يمكن رده إلى المستقيم بأخذ نقيض النتيجة المحالة واقترانها بالمقدمة الحقة الصادقة فإنه ينتج عين المطلوب وذلك كما لو كان المطلوب مثلاً (ليس كل "ب" "ج") فقلت إن كان ليس كل "ب" "ج") فـ (كل "ب" "ج" ) و(كل "ج " "أ") فـ (كل "ب" "أ" ) لكن ليس كل "ب" "أ") "ج") و(كل "ج" "أ") فـ (ليس كل "ب" "ج") وهو عين المطلوب، لكن إن كانت نتيجة الاقتران الخلفي كليّة موجبة فقياسه المستقيم لا يكون من غير الاقتران الرابع من الشكل الثاني، وإن كانت نتيجته كليّة سالبة فإما أن تكون مقدماته موجبة من المختلطات من الشكل الثاني أو أن إحداهما سالبة، فإن كانت مقدماته موجبة لم يمكن رده إلى المستقيم؛ لأنك إذا أخذت نقيض المطلوب كان جزئياً موجباً، وعند اقترانه بالصادقة الموجبة ينتج من الاقتران الثالث من الأول جزئياً موجباً والمطلوب قد كان جزئياً سالباً، لضرورة أن المأخوذ في الاقتران الأول نقيضه، وقد يكون كلياً موجباً. وأما إن كانت إحداهما سالبة فإما أن تكون صغرى أو كبرى، فإن كانت صغرى فالاقتران إما من الشكل الثاني أو الرابع، فإن كان من الثاني فقياسه المستقيم لا يكون من غير الاقتران الثالث من الرابع. وأما إن كانت السالبة كبرى فالاقتران إما من الشكل الأول أو الثاني، فإن كان من الأول فقياسه المستقيم لا يكون من غير الاقتران الثالث من الثاني، وإن كان من الثاني فقياسه المستقيم لا يكون من غير الاقتران الرابع من الأول. وأما إن كانت نتيجته جزئية موجبة فإما أن يكون من الشكل الأول أو الثالث أو الرابع، فإن كان من الأول فقياسه المستقيم لا يكون من غير الاقتران السادس من الثالث. وإن كان من الشكل الثالث فإما أن تكون مقدماته كليّة أو أن إحداهما جزئية فإن كانتا كليتين فقياسه المستقيم لا يكون من غير الاقتران الأول من الشكل الثاني وإذ ذاك فالنتيجة تكون كلية سالبة عين المطلوب داخل فيها، إذ هو قد كان جزئياً سالباً؛ لضرورة أن نقيضه كلي موجب، وأما إن كانت إحداهما جزئية فإما أن تكون صغرى أو كبرى، فإن كانت صغرى فقياسه المستقيم لا يكون من غير الاقتران الثالث من الثاني. وأما إن كان من الشكل الرابع فمقدماته أيضاً إما كليّة أو أن إحداهما جزئية، فإن كانتا كليتين فقياسه المستقيم لا يكون من غير الاقتران الرابع من الرابع، وإن كانت إحداهما جزئية فهي لا تكون إلا كبرى، وقياسه المستقيم إذ ذاك لا يكون من غير الاقتران الخامس من الرابع.
And when what we have indicated is realized, know that the indirect proof (khalf) can be reduced to the direct [syllogism] by taking the contradictory of the absurd conclusion and conjoining it with the true, sound premise; then it produces exactly the sought conclusion. That is like if the sought conclusion were, for example, 'Not every B is J.' Then you said: If it is 'Not every B is J', then 'Every B is J' and 'Every J is A', so 'Every B is A' but 'Not every B is A' and 'Every J is A', so 'Not every B is J' — which is exactly the sought conclusion. However, if the conclusion of the indirect conjunction is universal affirmative, then its direct syllogism is only from the fourth mood of the second figure. If its conclusion is universal negative, then either its premises are affirmative from the mixed [moods] of the second figure, or one of them is negative. If its premises are affirmative, it cannot be reduced to the direct because when you take the contradictory of the sought [conclusion] it is particular affirmative, and when conjoined with the affirmative true [premise] it yields from the third mood of the first figure a particular affirmative, while the sought conclusion was particular negative — necessarily because what is taken in the first conjunction is its contradictory, and it may be universal affirmative. As for if one of them is negative, then either it is minor or major. If it is minor, the conjunction is either from the second or fourth figure. If from the second, its direct syllogism is only from the third mood of the fourth figure. If the negative is major, then the conjunction is either from the first or second figure. If from the first, its direct syllogism is only from the third mood of the second figure; if from the second, its direct syllogism is only from the fourth mood of the first figure. As for if its conclusion is particular affirmative, then it is either from the first, third, or fourth figure. If from the first, its direct syllogism is only from the sixth mood of the third figure. If from the third figure, then either its premises are both universal or one is particular. If both are universal, its direct syllogism is only from the first mood of the second figure, and then the conclusion is universal negative containing the exact sought [conclusion], since it was particular negative — necessarily because its contradictory is universal affirmative. As for if one of them is particular, then either it is minor or major. If it is minor, its direct syllogism is only from the third mood of the second figure. As for if [the conclusion] is from the fourth figure, its premises are also either both universal or one is particular. If both are universal, its direct syllogism is only from the fourth mood of the fourth figure; if one is particular, it can only be the major, and its direct syllogism is then only from the fifth mood of the fourth figure.
Source pages p. 453

Commentary
This passage explains the technique of reducing a proof by contradiction (khalf) to a direct categorical syllogism (mustaqim). The author begins with the core method: take the contradictory (naqid) of the absurd conclusion (al-natija al-muhala) and combine it with a true premise to produce the original desired conclusion. An example is given: to prove 'Not every B is J', assume the opposite ('Every B is J'), combine with 'Every J is A' to get 'Every B is A'. But since we have 'Not every B is A' (a true premise), we get a contradiction, so the original assumption is false. By taking the contradictory of that assumption ('Every B is J') combined with 'Every J is A', we directly derive 'Not every B is J'. The author then systematically categorizes the different cases based on the quantity and quality of the indirect conclusion (the result of the khalf conjunction) and how to map each case to a specific direct mood. For instance, if the khalf conclusion is universal affirmative, the only direct mood that yields it is the fourth mood of the second figure. If it is universal negative, the reduction depends on whether the premises are affirmative or one is negative, and if negative which premise, leading to various moods in figures 1-4. Similarly for particular affirmative conclusions. The goal is to show that every valid khalf argument can be transformed into a direct syllogism of a specific mood, maintaining logical rigor.

مستقيم
نقيض
محلة

The reduction of khalf to a direct syllogism works by negating the absurd conclusion and using it as a premise with the original true premise.

Systematic Case Analysis for Khalf Reduction
Author systematically maps khalf conclusions to direct moods.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the correct direct mood for reducing any khalf based on the quantity and quality of its indirect conclusion and premises.

Core Idea
Each type of khalf conclusion maps to a specific direct mood.

The reduction of khalf to a direct syllogism is not always the same; it depends on the quantity (universal/particular) and quality (affirmative/negative) of the conclusion of the indirect conjunction (iqtiran khalfi). The author provides a detailed classification covering all cases. This lesson systematizes those rules so you can quickly identify the correct direct mood for any khalf.

Guided Path
1
Determine the quantity and quality of the khalf conclusion
The conclusion of the indirect conjunction is the proposition that was derived as absurd. Note whether it is universal or particular, and affirmative or negative.
Example: universal affirmative (كل ب أ) or particular negative (ليس كل ب ج).
2
If the khalf conclusion is universal affirmative
According to the author, the only direct mood that yields this is the fourth mood of the second figure.
No other alternative is given; it is uniquely determined.
3
If the khalf conclusion is universal negative
Check the premises: if both premises are affirmative, it cannot be reduced; if one is negative, the reduction depends on which premise is negative and the figures involved.
The author lists subcases: minor negative leads to second or fourth figure, major negative leads to first or second figure, each with a specific direct mood.
4
If the khalf conclusion is particular affirmative
Determine the figure of the indirect conjunction: first, third, or fourth figure. Each has its own mapping to a direct mood in a specific figure.
For example, if from first figure, direct is sixth mood of third figure; if from third figure with universal premises, direct is first mood of second figure; etc.
5
If the khalf conclusion is particular negative
The passage does not explicitly cover this, but it implies that the reduction follows similar rules; the example given earlier had a particular negative sought conclusion, which was reduced via the fourth mood of the second figure.
The systematic classification is exhaustive for the other three types.

وعند تحقق ما أشرنا إليه فاعلم أن الخلف قد يمكن رده إلى المستقيم بأخذ نقيض النتيجة المحالة واقترانها بالمقدمة الحقة الصادقة فإنه ينتج عين المطلوب وذلك كما لو كان المطلوب مثلاً (ليس كل "ب" "ج") فقلت إن كان ليس كل "ب" "ج") فـ (كل "ب" "ج" ) و(كل "ج " "أ") فـ (كل "ب" "أ" ) لكن ليس كل "ب" "أ") "ج") و(كل "ج" "أ") فـ (ليس كل "ب" "ج") وهو عين المطلوب، لكن إن كانت نتيجة الاقتران الخلفي كليّة موجبة فقياسه المستقيم لا يكون من غير الاقتران الرابع من الشكل الثاني، وإن كانت نتيجته كليّة سالبة فإما أن تكون مقدماته موجبة من المختلطات من الشكل الثاني أو أن إحداهما سالبة، فإن كانت مقدماته موجبة لم يمكن رده إلى المستقيم؛ لأنك إذا أخذت نقيض المطلوب كان جزئياً موجباً، وعند اقترانه بالصادقة الموجبة ينتج من الاقتران الثالث من الأول جزئياً موجباً والمطلوب قد كان جزئياً سالباً، لضرورة أن المأخوذ في الاقتران الأول نقيضه، وقد يكون كلياً موجباً. وأما إن كانت إحداهما سالبة فإما أن تكون صغرى أو كبرى، فإن كانت صغرى فالاقتران إما من الشكل الثاني أو الرابع، فإن كان من الثاني فقياسه المستقيم لا يكون من غير الاقتران الثالث من الرابع. وأما إن كانت السالبة كبرى فالاقتران إما من الشكل الأول أو الثاني، فإن كان من الأول فقياسه المستقيم لا يكون من غير الاقتران الثالث من الثاني، وإن كان من الثاني فقياسه المستقيم لا يكون من غير الاقتران الرابع من الأول. وأما إن كانت نتيجته جزئية موجبة فإما أن يكون من الشكل الأول أو الثالث أو الرابع، فإن كان من الأول فقياسه المستقيم لا يكون من غير الاقتران السادس من الثالث. وإن كان من الشكل الثالث فإما أن تكون مقدماته كليّة أو أن إحداهما جزئية فإن كانتا كليتين فقياسه المستقيم لا يكون من غير الاقتران الأول من الشكل الثاني وإذ ذاك فالنتيجة تكون كلية سالبة عين المطلوب داخل فيها، إذ هو قد كان جزئياً سالباً؛ لضرورة أن نقيضه كلي موجب، وأما إن كانت إحداهما جزئية فإما أن تكون صغرى أو كبرى، فإن كانت صغرى فقياسه المستقيم لا يكون من غير الاقتران الثالث من الثاني. وأما إن كان من الشكل الرابع فمقدماته أيضاً إما كليّة أو أن إحداهما جزئية، فإن كانتا كليتين فقياسه المستقيم لا يكون من غير الاقتران الرابع من الرابع، وإن كانت إحداهما جزئية فهي لا تكون إلا كبرى، وقياسه المستقيم إذ ذاك لا يكون من غير الاقتران الخامس من الرابع.
And when what we have indicated is realized, know that the indirect proof (khalf) can be reduced to the direct [syllogism] by taking the contradictory of the absurd conclusion and conjoining it with the true, sound premise; then it produces exactly the sought conclusion. That is like if the sought conclusion were, for example, 'Not every B is J.' Then you said: If it is 'Not every B is J', then 'Every B is J' and 'Every J is A', so 'Every B is A' but 'Not every B is A' and 'Every J is A', so 'Not every B is J' — which is exactly the sought conclusion. However, if the conclusion of the indirect conjunction is universal affirmative, then its direct syllogism is only from the fourth mood of the second figure. If its conclusion is universal negative, then either its premises are affirmative from the mixed [moods] of the second figure, or one of them is negative. If its premises are affirmative, it cannot be reduced to the direct because when you take the contradictory of the sought [conclusion] it is particular affirmative, and when conjoined with the affirmative true [premise] it yields from the third mood of the first figure a particular affirmative, while the sought conclusion was particular negative — necessarily because what is taken in the first conjunction is its contradictory, and it may be universal affirmative. As for if one of them is negative, then either it is minor or major. If it is minor, the conjunction is either from the second or fourth figure. If from the second, its direct syllogism is only from the third mood of the fourth figure. If the negative is major, then the conjunction is either from the first or second figure. If from the first, its direct syllogism is only from the third mood of the second figure; if from the second, its direct syllogism is only from the fourth mood of the first figure. As for if its conclusion is particular affirmative, then it is either from the first, third, or fourth figure. If from the first, its direct syllogism is only from the sixth mood of the third figure. If from the third figure, then either its premises are both universal or one is particular. If both are universal, its direct syllogism is only from the first mood of the second figure, and then the conclusion is universal negative containing the exact sought [conclusion], since it was particular negative — necessarily because its contradictory is universal affirmative. As for if one of them is particular, then either it is minor or major. If it is minor, its direct syllogism is only from the third mood of the second figure. As for if [the conclusion] is from the fourth figure, its premises are also either both universal or one is particular. If both are universal, its direct syllogism is only from the fourth mood of the fourth figure; if one is particular, it can only be the major, and its direct syllogism is then only from the fifth mood of the fourth figure.
Source pages p. 453

Commentary
This passage explains the technique of reducing a proof by contradiction (khalf) to a direct categorical syllogism (mustaqim). The author begins with the core method: take the contradictory (naqid) of the absurd conclusion (al-natija al-muhala) and combine it with a true premise to produce the original desired conclusion. An example is given: to prove 'Not every B is J', assume the opposite ('Every B is J'), combine with 'Every J is A' to get 'Every B is A'. But since we have 'Not every B is A' (a true premise), we get a contradiction, so the original assumption is false. By taking the contradictory of that assumption ('Every B is J') combined with 'Every J is A', we directly derive 'Not every B is J'. The author then systematically categorizes the different cases based on the quantity and quality of the indirect conclusion (the result of the khalf conjunction) and how to map each case to a specific direct mood. For instance, if the khalf conclusion is universal affirmative, the only direct mood that yields it is the fourth mood of the second figure. If it is universal negative, the reduction depends on whether the premises are affirmative or one is negative, and if negative which premise, leading to various moods in figures 1-4. Similarly for particular affirmative conclusions. The goal is to show that every valid khalf argument can be transformed into a direct syllogism of a specific mood, maintaining logical rigor.

Key Takeaway
The reduction of khalf to a direct syllogism follows strict rules based on the type of the indirect conclusion and premises, ensuring every indirect proof has a direct counterpart.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Given a khalf argument from a fiqh source (e.g., proving that qiyas is not applicable in certain cases), identify the conclusion of the indirect conjunction (type: universal affirmative, universal negative, etc.) and the premises, then write the direct syllogism mood according to the author's rules.

Reduction of Proof by Contradiction: Second Figure Cases
Author enumerates reduction cases for second figure

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine when a proof by contradiction in the second figure with a universal or particular negative conclusion can be reduced to a direct syllogism.

Core Idea
Reducibility depends on the quality of premises, not just the figure.

This passage introduces the concept of reducing a qiyas al-khalf (proof by contradiction) to a qiyas mustaqim (direct syllogism). The author begins with the second figure, examining cases where the conclusion is universal negative or particular negative. Understanding these specific cases lays the foundation for the general reduction procedure.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conclusion type
The passage first considers a conclusion that is universal negative (كليّة سالبة) from the second figure. Later it examines a particular negative (جزئية سالبة) conclusion.
The quantity and quality of the conclusion determine which subcases are relevant.
2
Check the premises for the universal negative case
For a universal negative conclusion, the premises can be either both affirmative or one negative. If both are affirmative, the author states reduction is impossible.
The reason: taking the contradictory yields a particular affirmative, which with the true premise gives a particular affirmative, not the required particular negative.
3
Examine the universal negative case with one negative premise
If one premise is negative, the author distinguishes whether it is the minor or major, and specifies the corresponding direct syllogism mood and figure.
For example, if the negative is the minor, the direct syllogism is from the third mood of the fourth figure.
4
Handle the particular negative conclusion
For a particular negative conclusion in the second figure, the author explicitly says it cannot be reduced to the direct syllogism.
This is a key exception: not all proofs by contradiction are reducible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفن الخامس: في تحقيق قياس الخلف ورده إلى المستقيم
الشكل الثاني، وإن كانت نتيجته كليّة سالبة فإما أن تكون مقدماته موجبة من المختلطات من الشكل الثاني أو أن إحداهما سالبة، فإن كانت مقدماته موجبة لم يمكن رده إلى المستقيم؛ لأنك إذا أخذت نقيض المطلوب كان جزئياً موجباً، وعند اقترانه بالصادقة الموجبة ينتج من الاقتران الثالث من الأول جزئياً موجباً والمطلوب قد كان جزئياً سالباً، لضرورة أن المأخوذ في الاقتران الأول نقيضه، وقد يكون كلياً موجباً.
وأما إن كانت إحداهما سالبة فإما أن تكون صغرى أو كبرى، فإن كانت صغرى فالاقتران إما من الشكل الثاني أو الرابع، فإن كان من الثاني فقياسه المستقيم لا يكون من غير الاقتران الثالث من الرابع.
وأما إن كانت السالبة كبرى فالاقتران إما من الشكل الأول أو الثاني، فإن كان من الأول فقياسه المستقيم لا يكون من غير الاقتران الثالث من الثاني، وإن كان من الثاني فقياسه المستقيم لا يكون من غير الاقتران الرابع من الأول.
وأما إن كانت نتيجته جزئية موجبة فإما أن يكون من الشكل الأول أو الثالث أو الرابع، فإن كان من الأول فقياسه المستقيم لا يكون من غير الاقتران السادس من الثالث.
وإن كان من الشكل الثالث فإما أن تكون مقدماته كليّة أو أن إحداهما جزئية فإن كانتا كليتين فقياسه المستقيم لا يكون من غير الاقتران الأول من الشكل الثاني وإذ ذاك فالنتيجة تكون كلية سالبة عين المطلوب داخل فيها، إذ هو قد كان جزئياً سالباً؛ لضرورة أن نقيضه كلي موجب، وأما إن كانت إحداهما جزئية فإما أن تكون صغرى أو كبرى، فإن كانت صغرى فقياسه المستقيم لا يكون من غير الاقتران الثالث من الثاني.
وأما إن كان من الشكل الرابع فمقدماته أيضاً إما كليّة أو أن إحداهما جزئية، فإن كانتا كليتين فقياسه المستقيم لا يكون من غير الاقتران الرابع من الرابع، وإن كانت إحداهما جزئية فهي لا تكون إلا كبرى، وقياسه المستقيم إذ ذاك لا يكون من غير الاقتران الخامس من الرابع.
وأما إن كانت النتيجة جزئية سالبة فلا يكون من غير الشكل الأول، بل من الثاني أو الثالث أو الرابع، فإن كان من الشكل الثاني فلا يمكن رده إلى المستقيم واعتبره
وأما إن كان من الشكل الثالث فإما أن يكون من الاقتران الرابع أو الخامس أو السادس، فإن كان من الرابع فقياسه المستقيم من الاقتران الثاني من الثاني، ونتيجته إذ ذاك كلية سالبة، والمطلوب يكون داخلاً فيها؛ لكونه جزئياً سالباً، وإن كان من الخامس فقياسه المستقيم لا يكون من غير الاقتران الرابع من الثاني، وإن كان من السادس فقياسه المستقيم لا يكون من غير الاقتران الأول من الرابع.
وأما إن كان من الشكل الرابع فإما من الاقتران أو الخامس، فإن كان من الرابع فقياسه المستقيم لا يكون من غير الاقتران الأول من الرابع، والنتيجة إذ ذاك تكون كلية سالبة، والمطلوب يكون داخلاً فيها، وكذا إن كان من الخامس لكن نتيجته غير المطلوب.
هذا كله أن كان ما أضيف إلى نقيض النتيجة المحالة هو غير المقدمة الصادقة المأخوذة في الخلف، وإلا أن كان المضاف إليه غيرها فقد تختلف الحال في ما ذكرناه من الاقترانات واعتبره
The fifth art: On verifying the proof by contradiction and its reduction to the direct [syllogism]. The second figure: if its conclusion is universal negative, then either its premises are affirmative from the mixed [premises] of the second figure, or one of them is negative. If its premises are affirmative, it cannot be reduced to the direct [syllogism] because if you take the contradictory of the sought conclusion, it is particular affirmative, and when paired with the true affirmative premise, it yields from the third mood of the first [figure] a particular affirmative, while the sought conclusion was particular negative, necessarily because what was taken in the first mood is its contradictory, and it may be universal affirmative. As for if one of them is negative, then either it is the minor or the major. If it is the minor, the mood is either from the second figure or the fourth. If it is from the second, its direct syllogism is not other than the third mood of the fourth. As for if the negative is the major, the mood is either from the first figure or the second. If it is from the first, its direct syllogism is not other than the third mood of the second; if from the second, its direct syllogism is not other than the fourth mood of the first. As for if its conclusion is particular affirmative, then either it is from the first figure, the third, or the fourth. If it is from the first, its direct syllogism is not other than the sixth mood of the third. If it is from the third figure, then either its premises are universal or one of them is particular. If both are universal, its direct syllogism is not other than the first mood of the second figure, and then the conclusion is universal negative, containing the sought conclusion itself, since it was particular negative, necessarily because its contradictory is universal affirmative. As for if one of them is particular, then either it is the minor or the major. If it is the minor, its direct syllogism is not other than the third mood of the second. As for if it is from the fourth figure, its premises are also either universal or one of them is particular. If both are universal, its direct syllogism is not other than the fourth mood of the fourth. If one of them is particular, it can only be the major, and then its direct syllogism is not other than the fifth mood of the fourth. As for if the conclusion is particular negative, it is not from any but the first figure, but from the second, third, or fourth. If it is from the second figure, it cannot be reduced to the direct [syllogism] — consider that. As for if it is from the third figure, then either it is from the fourth, fifth, or sixth mood. If it is from the fourth, its direct syllogism is from the second mood of the second, and its conclusion then is universal negative, and the sought conclusion is included in it because it is particular negative. If it is from the fifth, its direct syllogism is not other than the fourth mood of the second. If it is from the sixth, its direct syllogism is not other than the first mood of the fourth. As for if it is from the fourth figure, then either from the [fourth] mood or the fifth. If it is from the fourth, its direct syllogism is not other than the first mood of the fourth, and the conclusion then is universal negative, and the sought conclusion is included in it. Similarly if it is from the fifth, but its conclusion is not the sought conclusion. All this is if what is added to the contradictory of the impossible conclusion is different from the true premise taken in the [proof by] contradiction. Otherwise, if what is added to it is something else, the situation may differ in the moods we mentioned — consider that.
Source pages p. 454

Commentary
This passage is a detailed technical enumeration of how to reduce a proof by contradiction (قياس الخلف) to a direct categorical syllogism (قياس مستقيم). The author systematically goes through each possible case based on the quantity and quality of the conclusion and the figure of the original syllogism. The reduction works by taking the contradictory of the original conclusion and pairing it with one of the true premises to produce a result that reveals the falsehood of the contradictory, thereby establishing the original conclusion. The author specifies exactly which mood of which figure in direct syllogism corresponds to each case. For example, when the original syllogism is in the second figure with a universal negative conclusion and both premises affirmative, no reduction is possible because taking the contradictory yields a particular affirmative, which with the true premise gives a particular affirmative, not the required particular negative. The author covers universal negative, particular affirmative, and particular negative conclusions, noting that some cases cannot be reduced (e.g., second figure with particular negative conclusion). The final remark clarifies that the reduction works only when the premise added to the contradictory is the same as the true premise used in the original proof by contradiction; otherwise, the mapping of moods changes.

القياس المستقيم

Key Takeaway
The reducibility of a proof by contradiction depends on the specific combination of figure, mood, and quality of premises; not all cases are reducible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Reduction for Particular Affirmative Conclusions
Author enumerates reduction cases for particular affirmative conclusion

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the correct direct syllogism for a proof by contradiction with a particular affirmative conclusion, based on its figure.

Core Idea
Each figure has a specific mood that serves as the direct equivalent.

The author now turns to proofs by contradiction with a particular affirmative conclusion. He covers possible figures: first, third, and fourth. For each, he specifies which mood of which figure in a direct syllogism corresponds to the reduction. This lesson breaks down those mappings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conclusion type
The conclusion is particular affirmative (جزئية موجبة). The figures considered are the first, third, and fourth. The second figure is not mentioned for this type.
This is a different category from the previous lesson.
2
First figure case
If the original syllogism is in the first figure, the direct syllogism comes from the sixth mood of the third figure.
This is one of the few mappings that cross figures.
3
Third figure cases
The author distinguishes whether the premises are both universal or one is particular. If both universal, the direct syllogism is the first mood of the second figure. If one premise is particular (and it must be the minor), the direct syllogism is the third mood of the second figure.
Note that the result is a universal negative conclusion, which contains the particular affirmative sought.
4
Fourth figure cases
Similarly, for the fourth figure: if both premises are universal, the direct syllogism is the fourth mood of the fourth. If one premise is particular (it must be the major), the direct syllogism is the fifth mood of the fourth.
The author does not mention any impossibility here; all cases are reducible.

الفن الخامس: في تحقيق قياس الخلف ورده إلى المستقيم
الشكل الثاني، وإن كانت نتيجته كليّة سالبة فإما أن تكون مقدماته موجبة من المختلطات من الشكل الثاني أو أن إحداهما سالبة، فإن كانت مقدماته موجبة لم يمكن رده إلى المستقيم؛ لأنك إذا أخذت نقيض المطلوب كان جزئياً موجباً، وعند اقترانه بالصادقة الموجبة ينتج من الاقتران الثالث من الأول جزئياً موجباً والمطلوب قد كان جزئياً سالباً، لضرورة أن المأخوذ في الاقتران الأول نقيضه، وقد يكون كلياً موجباً.
وأما إن كانت إحداهما سالبة فإما أن تكون صغرى أو كبرى، فإن كانت صغرى فالاقتران إما من الشكل الثاني أو الرابع، فإن كان من الثاني فقياسه المستقيم لا يكون من غير الاقتران الثالث من الرابع.
وأما إن كانت السالبة كبرى فالاقتران إما من الشكل الأول أو الثاني، فإن كان من الأول فقياسه المستقيم لا يكون من غير الاقتران الثالث من الثاني، وإن كان من الثاني فقياسه المستقيم لا يكون من غير الاقتران الرابع من الأول.
وأما إن كانت نتيجته جزئية موجبة فإما أن يكون من الشكل الأول أو الثالث أو الرابع، فإن كان من الأول فقياسه المستقيم لا يكون من غير الاقتران السادس من الثالث.
وإن كان من الشكل الثالث فإما أن تكون مقدماته كليّة أو أن إحداهما جزئية فإن كانتا كليتين فقياسه المستقيم لا يكون من غير الاقتران الأول من الشكل الثاني وإذ ذاك فالنتيجة تكون كلية سالبة عين المطلوب داخل فيها، إذ هو قد كان جزئياً سالباً؛ لضرورة أن نقيضه كلي موجب، وأما إن كانت إحداهما جزئية فإما أن تكون صغرى أو كبرى، فإن كانت صغرى فقياسه المستقيم لا يكون من غير الاقتران الثالث من الثاني.
وأما إن كان من الشكل الرابع فمقدماته أيضاً إما كليّة أو أن إحداهما جزئية، فإن كانتا كليتين فقياسه المستقيم لا يكون من غير الاقتران الرابع من الرابع، وإن كانت إحداهما جزئية فهي لا تكون إلا كبرى، وقياسه المستقيم إذ ذاك لا يكون من غير الاقتران الخامس من الرابع.
وأما إن كانت النتيجة جزئية سالبة فلا يكون من غير الشكل الأول، بل من الثاني أو الثالث أو الرابع، فإن كان من الشكل الثاني فلا يمكن رده إلى المستقيم واعتبره
وأما إن كان من الشكل الثالث فإما أن يكون من الاقتران الرابع أو الخامس أو السادس، فإن كان من الرابع فقياسه المستقيم من الاقتران الثاني من الثاني، ونتيجته إذ ذاك كلية سالبة، والمطلوب يكون داخلاً فيها؛ لكونه جزئياً سالباً، وإن كان من الخامس فقياسه المستقيم لا يكون من غير الاقتران الرابع من الثاني، وإن كان من السادس فقياسه المستقيم لا يكون من غير الاقتران الأول من الرابع.
وأما إن كان من الشكل الرابع فإما من الاقتران أو الخامس، فإن كان من الرابع فقياسه المستقيم لا يكون من غير الاقتران الأول من الرابع، والنتيجة إذ ذاك تكون كلية سالبة، والمطلوب يكون داخلاً فيها، وكذا إن كان من الخامس لكن نتيجته غير المطلوب.
هذا كله أن كان ما أضيف إلى نقيض النتيجة المحالة هو غير المقدمة الصادقة المأخوذة في الخلف، وإلا أن كان المضاف إليه غيرها فقد تختلف الحال في ما ذكرناه من الاقترانات واعتبره
The fifth art: On verifying the proof by contradiction and its reduction to the direct [syllogism]. The second figure: if its conclusion is universal negative, then either its premises are affirmative from the mixed [premises] of the second figure, or one of them is negative. If its premises are affirmative, it cannot be reduced to the direct [syllogism] because if you take the contradictory of the sought conclusion, it is particular affirmative, and when paired with the true affirmative premise, it yields from the third mood of the first [figure] a particular affirmative, while the sought conclusion was particular negative, necessarily because what was taken in the first mood is its contradictory, and it may be universal affirmative. As for if one of them is negative, then either it is the minor or the major. If it is the minor, the mood is either from the second figure or the fourth. If it is from the second, its direct syllogism is not other than the third mood of the fourth. As for if the negative is the major, the mood is either from the first figure or the second. If it is from the first, its direct syllogism is not other than the third mood of the second; if from the second, its direct syllogism is not other than the fourth mood of the first. As for if its conclusion is particular affirmative, then either it is from the first figure, the third, or the fourth. If it is from the first, its direct syllogism is not other than the sixth mood of the third. If it is from the third figure, then either its premises are universal or one of them is particular. If both are universal, its direct syllogism is not other than the first mood of the second figure, and then the conclusion is universal negative, containing the sought conclusion itself, since it was particular negative, necessarily because its contradictory is universal affirmative. As for if one of them is particular, then either it is the minor or the major. If it is the minor, its direct syllogism is not other than the third mood of the second. As for if it is from the fourth figure, its premises are also either universal or one of them is particular. If both are universal, its direct syllogism is not other than the fourth mood of the fourth. If one of them is particular, it can only be the major, and then its direct syllogism is not other than the fifth mood of the fourth. As for if the conclusion is particular negative, it is not from any but the first figure, but from the second, third, or fourth. If it is from the second figure, it cannot be reduced to the direct [syllogism] — consider that. As for if it is from the third figure, then either it is from the fourth, fifth, or sixth mood. If it is from the fourth, its direct syllogism is from the second mood of the second, and its conclusion then is universal negative, and the sought conclusion is included in it because it is particular negative. If it is from the fifth, its direct syllogism is not other than the fourth mood of the second. If it is from the sixth, its direct syllogism is not other than the first mood of the fourth. As for if it is from the fourth figure, then either from the [fourth] mood or the fifth. If it is from the fourth, its direct syllogism is not other than the first mood of the fourth, and the conclusion then is universal negative, and the sought conclusion is included in it. Similarly if it is from the fifth, but its conclusion is not the sought conclusion. All this is if what is added to the contradictory of the impossible conclusion is different from the true premise taken in the [proof by] contradiction. Otherwise, if what is added to it is something else, the situation may differ in the moods we mentioned — consider that.
Source pages p. 454

Commentary
This passage is a detailed technical enumeration of how to reduce a proof by contradiction (قياس الخلف) to a direct categorical syllogism (قياس مستقيم). The author systematically goes through each possible case based on the quantity and quality of the conclusion and the figure of the original syllogism. The reduction works by taking the contradictory of the original conclusion and pairing it with one of the true premises to produce a result that reveals the falsehood of the contradictory, thereby establishing the original conclusion. The author specifies exactly which mood of which figure in direct syllogism corresponds to each case. For example, when the original syllogism is in the second figure with a universal negative conclusion and both premises affirmative, no reduction is possible because taking the contradictory yields a particular affirmative, which with the true premise gives a particular affirmative, not the required particular negative. The author covers universal negative, particular affirmative, and particular negative conclusions, noting that some cases cannot be reduced (e.g., second figure with particular negative conclusion). The final remark clarifies that the reduction works only when the premise added to the contradictory is the same as the true premise used in the original proof by contradiction; otherwise, the mapping of moods changes.

Key Terms
اقتران سادس
اقتران أول
اقتران ثالث
اقتران رابع
اقتران خامس

Checkpoint
According to the passage, if the original proof by contradiction is in the first figure with a particular affirmative conclusion, what is the correct direct syllogism?
A) Sixth mood of the third figure
B) First mood of the second figure
C) Fourth mood of the fourth figure
D) Third mood of the second figure

Key Takeaway
For a particular affirmative conclusion, the reduction always yields a direct syllogism, often with a stronger conclusion that implies the sought one.

?

?

Reduction for Particular Negative Conclusions and the Final Condition
Author enumerates reduction cases for particular negative conclusion and adds a final condition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when a proof by contradiction with a particular negative conclusion can be reduced, and understand the condition that the added premise must be the same as the true premise used in the original proof.

Core Idea
Some cases are irreducible; when reduction is possible, the added premise must match the true premise.

The author concludes the enumeration with proofs by contradiction that have a particular negative conclusion, covering the second, third, and fourth figures. He notes that the second figure case is irreducible. He also introduces a crucial final condition: the reduction mappings hold only if the premise added to the contradictory is the same as the true premise used in the original proof. This lesson examines these last details.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conclusion type
The conclusion is particular negative (جزئية سالبة). Figures considered: second, third, fourth. The first figure is not used for this type.
The second figure case is immediately said to be irreducible.
2
Third figure cases
The author lists three moods: fourth, fifth, sixth. For the fourth mood, the direct syllogism is the second mood of the second figure, giving a universal negative conclusion that contains the particular negative. For the fifth mood, it is the fourth mood of the second figure. For the sixth mood, it is the first mood of the fourth figure.
Note that all these yield a universal negative conclusion that implies the original particular negative.
3
Fourth figure cases
For the fourth figure, the moods considered are the fourth and fifth. The fourth mood reduces to the first mood of the fourth figure (universal negative). The fifth mood similarly, but its conclusion is not the sought conclusion.
The author does not elaborate on why the fifth mood's conclusion differs; it may be due to the specific mood structure.
4
The final condition
The author states that all these mappings hold only if the premise added to the contradictory (in the reduction) is the same as the true premise originally used in the proof by contradiction. If it is different, the situation may change.
This is a crucial caveat that affects the applicability of the reduction.

الفن الخامس: في تحقيق قياس الخلف ورده إلى المستقيم
الشكل الثاني، وإن كانت نتيجته كليّة سالبة فإما أن تكون مقدماته موجبة من المختلطات من الشكل الثاني أو أن إحداهما سالبة، فإن كانت مقدماته موجبة لم يمكن رده إلى المستقيم؛ لأنك إذا أخذت نقيض المطلوب كان جزئياً موجباً، وعند اقترانه بالصادقة الموجبة ينتج من الاقتران الثالث من الأول جزئياً موجباً والمطلوب قد كان جزئياً سالباً، لضرورة أن المأخوذ في الاقتران الأول نقيضه، وقد يكون كلياً موجباً.

Direct Syllogism vs Proof by Contradiction: Four Key Differences
Author compares direct syllogism and proof by contradiction in four respects.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between a direct syllogism and a proof by contradiction in terms of aim, premises, subject treatment, and number of syllogisms.

Core Idea
Direct syllogism proves directly; proof by contradiction proves indirectly via an impossibility.

In logic, there are two main ways to demonstrate a proposition: directly or by contradiction. This passage from Daqaiq al-Haqaiq lays out four clear differences between a direct categorical syllogism (mustaqīm) and a proof by contradiction (khalf). Understanding these differences is essential for evaluating arguments in theology and jurisprudence, where both methods are used.

Guided Path
1
Identify the aim of each method
In the direct syllogism, we aim immediately to prove the desired conclusion by setting up accepted premises. In proof by contradiction, we first aim to demonstrate an impossibility (the contradictory of the desired conclusion or something similar), and only then derive the truth of the original conclusion.
The author says: 'في المستقيم يقصد فيه أولاً بيان المطلوب ... وفي الخلف إنما يقصد فيه أولاً بيان أمر محال'
2
Compare the premises used
In a direct syllogism, all premises agree with and support the sought conclusion directly. In proof by contradiction, one of the premises (or assumptions) is actually the contradictory of the sought conclusion—it is later shown to be false.
The author says: 'فإن المستقيم إنما يوجد فيه المقدمات الموافقة للمطلوب بالذات، وفي الخلف فإحداهما نقيض المطلوب'
3
Examine the treatment of the subject
In a direct syllogism, the subject of the conclusion is not taken as a premise first (i.e., it is not the starting point). In proof by contradiction, the opposite occurs: the contradictory of the subject (or the subject itself) may be assumed initially.
Author says: 'فإن النتيجة في المستقيم لا يكون موضوعه أولاً، وفي الخلف بضده'
4
Count the number of syllogisms required
A direct syllogism reaches its conclusion in a single syllogistic sequence. A proof by contradiction requires two syllogisms: one to derive an impossibility from the contradictory assumption, and another to infer the original conclusion from the falsehood of its contradictory.
Author says: 'فإن المطلوب في المستقيم يتم بقياس واحد، وفي الخلف إنما يتم بقياسين'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعلى هذا فالفرق بين المستقيم والخلف أن المستقيم يقصد فيه أولاً بيان المطلوب بنصب مقدمات مسلمة، إما مطلقاً وإما بالنسبة والخلف إنما يقصد فيه أولاً بيان أمر محال إما مطلقاً أو بالنسبة هو غير المطلوب، ثم يستنتج بعد ذلك كذب ما هو شبيه لبيان صدق نقيضه، وأيضاً فإن المستقيم إنما يوجد فيه المقدمات الموافقة للمطلوب بالذات، وفي الخلف فإحداهما نقيض المطلوب، وأيضاً فإن النتيجة في المستقيم لا يكون موضوعه أولاً، وفي الخلف بضده، وأيضاً فإن المطلوب في المستقيم يتم بقياس واحد ، وفي الخلف إنما يتم بقياسين. نعم، رد الخلف إلى المستقيم من جهة عكس القياس قد شابه قياس الخلف من
And accordingly, the difference between the direct [syllogism] and the [proof by] contradiction is that in the direct [syllogism] the aim is first to demonstrate the sought conclusion by setting up accepted premises, either absolutely or relatively, while in the [proof by] contradiction the aim is first to demonstrate an impossible matter, either absolutely or relatively, which is not the sought conclusion, then after that it infers the falsehood of what is similar in order to demonstrate the truth of its contradictory. Also, in the direct [syllogism] only the premises that agree with the sought conclusion in essence are present, while in the [proof by] contradiction one of them is the contradictory of the sought conclusion. Also, in the direct [syllogism] its subject is not [considered] first, while in the [proof by] contradiction it is the opposite. Also, the sought conclusion in the direct [syllogism] is completed with one syllogism, while in the [proof by] contradiction it is completed with two syllogisms. Indeed, the reduction of the proof by contradiction to the direct syllogism from the aspect of conversion of the syllogism has resembled the proof by contradiction from...
Source pages p. 455

Commentary
This passage enumerates four key differences between a direct categorical syllogism (مستقيم) and a proof by contradiction (خلف). First, the direct syllogism aims directly to prove the desired conclusion by adducing accepted premises, whereas proof by contradiction first aims to prove an impossibility (something contradictory to the desired conclusion or a related absurdity) and then derives the truth of the original conclusion by showing the falsehood of its contradictory. Second, in a direct syllogism all premises are in agreement with the conclusion and support it directly; in a proof by contradiction, one of the premises (or the assumption added) is the contradictory of the conclusion, which is later shown to be false. Third, the direct syllogism does not take the subject of the conclusion as a premise first; in proof by contradiction, the opposite occurs — the contradictory of the subject or predicate may be assumed. Fourth, a direct syllogism achieves its conclusion in a single syllogistic sequence, whereas a proof by contradiction requires two syllogisms: one to derive an impossibility from the contradictory assumption, and another to infer the truth of the original conclusion. The passage ends with a remark that reducing proof by contradiction to a direct syllogism via conversion of the syllogism resembles the proof by contradiction itself, but the text is cut off, leaving the exact point unclear.

Key Terms
مستقيم

محال

Reducing Particular Negative Syllogisms to the First Figure
Author details reduction of particular negative conclusions from second, third, and fourth figures.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the correct direct first-figure mood for any particular negative syllogism from the second, third, or fourth figure.

Core Idea
Every particular negative conclusion from non-first figures has a specific first-figure counterpart.

The author provides a systematic mapping for reducing syllogisms whose conclusion is 'Some S is not P' from the second, third, and fourth figures to the direct first figure. Understanding these mappings is essential for evaluating the validity of such syllogisms in Islamic logical works, especially when dealing with arguments in Kalam and Fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the figure and mood of the original syllogism
Determine whether the syllogism belongs to the second, third, or fourth figure, and note its mood (e.g., fourth mood of the third figure). The passage specifies which moods yield a particular negative conclusion.
The second figure cannot be reduced directly; the author marks it with 'واعتبره'.
2
Locate the corresponding direct syllogism
For each eligible mood, the author gives a direct syllogism (mustaqīm) from the first figure or another figure that produces the desired conclusion. For example, the fourth mood of the third figure reduces to the second mood of the second figure.
The direct syllogism's conclusion is often universal negative, containing the desired particular.
3
Verify that the desired conclusion is contained
The direct syllogism yields a universal negative conclusion (e.g., 'All S are not P'), which logically implies the particular negative ('Some S is not P'). Check that the desired conclusion is a subaltern of the direct one.
This is based on the logical rule that the universal implies the particular.
4
Consider exceptions and special cases
The author notes that for the fifth mood of the fourth figure, the direct syllogism's conclusion is not the desired one. Also, the reduction scheme assumes a specific condition about the reductio ad absurdum premise. Be aware of these nuances.
This step prevents overgeneralization.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما إن كانت النتيجة جزئية سالبة فلا يكون من غير الشكل الأول، بل من الثاني أو الثالث أو الرابع، فإن كان من الشكل الثاني فلا يمكن رده إلى المستقيم واعتبره. وأما إن كان من الشكل الثالث فإما أن يكون من الاقتران الرابع أو الخامس أو السادس، فإن كان من الرابع فقياسه المستقيم من الاقتران الثاني من الثاني، ونتيجته إذ ذاك كلية سالبة، والمطلوب يكون داخلاً فيها؛ لكونه جزئياً سالباً، وإن كان من الخامس فقياسه المستقيم لا يكون من غير الاقتران الرابع من الثاني، وإن كان من السادس فقياسه المستقيم لا يكون من غير الاقتران الأول من الرابع. وأما إن كان من الشكل الرابع فإما من الاقتران أو الخامس، فإن كان من الرابع فقياسه المستقيم لا يكون من غير الاقتران الأول من الرابع، والنتيجة إذ ذاك تكون كلية سالبة، والمطلوب يكون داخلاً فيها، وكذا إن كان من الخامس لكن نتيجته غير المطلوب. هذا كله أن كان ما أضيف إلى نقيض النتيجة المحالة هو غير المقدمة الصادقة المأخوذة في الخلف، وإلا أن كان المضاف إليه غيرها فقد تختلف الحال في ما ذكرناه من الاقترانات واعتبره
And if the conclusion is particular negative, then it is not from the first figure, but from the second, third, or fourth. If it is from the second figure, then it cannot be reduced to the direct [syllogism] — consider that. And if it is from the third figure, then it is either from the fourth, fifth, or sixth mood. If from the fourth, then its direct syllogism is from the second mood of the second [figure], and its conclusion then is universal negative, and the desired [conclusion] is included within it because it is particular negative. If from the fifth, then its direct syllogism is only from the fourth mood of the second [figure]. If from the sixth, then its direct syllogism is only from the first mood of the fourth [figure]. And if it is from the fourth figure, then it is either from the [fourth] mood or the fifth. If from the fourth, then its direct syllogism is only from the first mood of the fourth [figure], and the conclusion then is universal negative, and the desired [conclusion] is included within it. Likewise if from the fifth, but its conclusion is not the desired [conclusion]. All this [applies] if what is added to the contradictory of the impossible conclusion is other than the true premise taken in the reductio. But if what is added to it is other than it, then the situation may differ regarding what we mentioned of the moods — consider that.
Source pages p. 455

Commentary
This passage demonstrates how to reduce syllogisms with particular negative conclusions from non-first figures to direct first figure syllogisms. The author begins by stating that any syllogism whose conclusion is particular negative (like 'Some A is not B') cannot be from the first figure; it must belong to the second, third, or fourth figure. For the second figure, such a conclusion cannot be reduced directly — the author marks this with 'consider that' (واعتبره), indicating it's an exception. For the third figure, three moods (fourth, fifth, sixth) can produce a particular negative. Each is reduced to a specific mood in the first figure: the fourth mood of the third reduces to the second mood of the second figure, yielding a universal negative conclusion that contains the desired particular negative. The fifth reduces to the fourth mood of the second figure, and the sixth to the first mood of the fourth figure. For the fourth figure, two moods (fourth and fifth) are considered. The fourth mood of the fourth reduces to the first mood of the fourth figure, also yielding a universal negative containing the desired. The fifth mood's direct reduction gives a universal negative, but the conclusion is not the desired — meaning the reduction is not straightforward. The author then notes that this entire scheme assumes that the contradictory premise added in the reductio ad absurdum is not the same as the true premise taken for the reduction; otherwise, the moods vary.

Four Main Differences Between Direct Proof and Reductio
Author lists four differences between direct and reductio proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish direct proof from reductio ad absurdum on the basis of purpose, premises, conclusion orientation, and number of syllogisms.

Core Idea
Direct proof and reductio differ in four essential ways: purpose, premises, outcome focus, and complexity.

In this passage, the author systematically contrasts two fundamental methods of logical proof: direct syllogism (al-mustaqīm) and reductio ad absurdum (al-khalf). Understanding these differences is crucial for selecting the appropriate proof strategy in Islamic theology and jurisprudence, where both methods are employed to establish truths about God, the world, and legal rulings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the overarching purpose
Notice that the author states the direct proof aims first to demonstrate the desired conclusion using conceded premises, while reductio aims first to demonstrate something impossible and then infer the truth of the contradictory.
This is the most fundamental difference: one works forward, the other backward.
2
Examine the premises involved
In direct proof, all premises agree with the desired conclusion. In reductio, one premise is explicitly the contradictory of the desired conclusion.
This means reductio introduces an assumption that would normally be rejected.
3
Check the status of the conclusion's subject
The author says in direct proof the conclusion does not have its subject as a first intention; in reductio, the opposite is true (the conclusion is the contradictory of the original claim, so its subject is primary).
This reflects that reductio transforms the original question.
4
Count the number of syllogisms
Direct proof is completed with one syllogism; reductio requires two syllogisms (one to derive an impossibility, another to infer the denial of the assumption).
This is a practical difference in argument structure.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعلى هذا فالفرق بين المستقيم والخلف أن المستقيم يقصد فيه أولاً بيان المطلوب بنصب مقدمات مسلمة، إما مطلقاً وإما بالنسبة والخلف إنما يقصد فيه أولاً بيان أمر محال إما مطلقاً أو بالنسبة هو غير المطلوب، ثم يستنتج بعد ذلك كذب ما هو شبيه لبيان صدق نقيضه. وأيضاً فإن المستقيم إنما يوجد فيه المقدمات الموافقة للمطلوب بالذات، وفي الخلف فإحداهما نقيض المطلوب. وأيضاً فإن النتيجة في المستقيم لا يكون موضوعه أولاً، وفي الخلف بضده. وأيضاً فإن المطلوب في المستقيم يتم بقياس واحد، وفي الخلف إنما يتم بقياسين. نعم، رد الخلف إلى المستقيم من جهة عكس القياس قد شابه قياس الخلف من جهة أن كل واحد من قياس الخلف، وعكس القياس كان ذلك عكساً لقياس الخلف أو غيره، قد يوجد فيه نقيض مطلوب ما ويضاف إليه مقدمة مسلمة لإبطال مسلم لكن ما به الافتراق بينهما أن عكس القياس لا يكون إلا بعد قياس آخر، والخلف فلا يكون إلا مبتدأ. وأيضاً فإن المأخوذ في عكس القياس قد يكون ضد مطلوب ما، وقد يكون نقيضه، والمأخوذ فى الخلف ليس إلا النقيض. وأيضاً فإن المأخوذ في عكس القياس ليس إلا نقيض مطلوب هو نتيجة، ولا كذلك في الخلف.
And based on this, the difference between the direct and the reductio is that in the direct, the aim initially is to demonstrate the desired [conclusion] by setting up premises that are conceded, either absolutely or relatively. In the reductio, the aim initially is to demonstrate an impossible matter, either absolutely or relatively, which is other than the desired [conclusion]; then after that it deduces the falsehood of what resembles it in order to demonstrate the truth of its contradictory. Also, in the direct, there exist only premises that are in agreement with the desired conclusion per se; in the reductio, one of them is the contradictory of the desired. Also, in the direct, the conclusion does not have its subject as a first [intention]; in the reductio, the opposite. Also, the desired in the direct is completed with one syllogism; in the reductio, it is completed with two syllogisms. Indeed, reduction of the reductio to the direct by way of conversion of the syllogism has resembled the reductio syllogism in that each of the reductio syllogism and the conversion of the syllogism — whether that is a conversion of the reductio syllogism or otherwise — may contain the contradictory of some desired [proposition] and to it is added a conceded premise to refute a conceded [proposition]. But the distinction between them is that conversion of a syllogism occurs only after another syllogism, whereas the reductio occurs only as a starting [point]. Also, what is taken in conversion of a syllogism may be the contrary of some desired, or its contradictory; but what is taken in the reductio is only the contradictory. Also, what is taken in conversion of a syllogism is only the contradictory of a desired [conclusion] that is a result; not so in the reductio.
Source pages p. 455

Commentary
This passage systematically distinguishes between direct syllogism (al-mustaqīm) and argument by contradiction (al-khalf). The author presents four main differences and then an additional nuance regarding conversion (ʿaks al-qiyās). First, the purpose differs: direct proof aims at establishing the desired conclusion using conceded premises, whereas reductio initially aims to prove something impossible (that is not the desired), then from that impossibility deduces the truth of the opposite of the desired. Second, the premises in a direct syllogism all agree with the desired conclusion essentially; in reductio, one premise is the contradictory of the desired. Third, the conclusion in a direct syllogism does not have its subject as the primary subject of inquiry; in reductio, it does (i.e., the conclusion is the contradictory of the original claim). Fourth, direct proof is completed with one syllogism; reductio requires two (one to derive an impossibility, then another to infer the denial of the original). The author then addresses a subtle point: when reductio is reduced to a direct syllogism via conversion, it may resemble reduced syllogism in that both involve adding a contradictory premise to a conceded premise to derive a contradiction. However, they differ in that conversion presupposes another syllogism already, while reductio is original. Also, conversion may use a contrary or contradictory, but reductio uses only the contradictory. Finally, in conversion, the added premise is the contradictory of a result, whereas in reductio it is not necessarily so.

The Nuance of Conversion (ʿAks al-Qiyās) in Reductio
Author distinguishes conversion from reductio on three criteria.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to differentiate between conversion of a syllogism (ʿaks al-qiyās) and reductio ad absurdum in terms of origination, type of opposite premise, and the status of the added premise.

Core Idea
Conversion presupposes a prior syllogism; reductio is original. Conversion may use contrary or contradictory; reductio uses only contradictory.

After presenting the four main differences, the author delves into a finer point: the relationship between reductio and conversion of a syllogism. Both involve adding a contradictory premise to derive a contradiction, but they differ in crucial aspects. Understanding this nuance sharpens your ability to analyze complex arguments in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the similarity between reductio and conversion
The author states that reduction of reductio to a direct syllogism via conversion (ʿaks al-qiyās) resembles reductio because both contain the contradictory of a desired proposition alongside a conceded premise.
This similarity can cause confusion; the author clarifies the distinctions.
2
Identify the difference in origination
Conversion occurs only after another syllogism (it is a transformation of a prior argument), whereas reductio occurs as a starting point (it is original, not derived).
So conversion is a secondary operation; reductio is a primary proof strategy.
3
Note the type of opposite premise allowed
In conversion, the added premise may be either the contrary (ḍidd) or the contradictory (naqīḍ) of a desired proposition. In reductio, it is only the contradictory.
This is a key restriction: reductio requires full contradiction, not mere contrariety.
4
Check the status of the added premise in relation to a result
In conversion, the added premise is the contradictory of a desired conclusion that is a result (i.e., derived from a previous syllogism). In reductio, the added premise is not necessarily the contradictory of a result; it is the contradictory of the original desired claim.
This shows conversion is more constrained: it operates on conclusions, while reductio operates on the original claim.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعلى هذا فالفرق بين المستقيم والخلف أن المستقيم يقصد فيه أولاً بيان المطلوب بنصب مقدمات مسلمة، إما مطلقاً وإما بالنسبة والخلف إنما يقصد فيه أولاً بيان أمر محال إما مطلقاً أو بالنسبة هو غير المطلوب، ثم يستنتج بعد ذلك كذب ما هو شبيه لبيان صدق نقيضه. وأيضاً فإن المستقيم إنما يوجد فيه المقدمات الموافقة للمطلوب بالذات، وفي الخلف فإحداهما نقيض المطلوب. وأيضاً فإن النتيجة في المستقيم لا يكون موضوعه أولاً، وفي الخلف بضده. وأيضاً فإن المطلوب في المستقيم يتم بقياس واحد، وفي الخلف إنما يتم بقياسين. نعم، رد الخلف إلى المستقيم من جهة عكس القياس قد شابه قياس الخلف من جهة أن كل واحد من قياس الخلف، وعكس القياس كان ذلك عكساً لقياس الخلف أو غيره، قد يوجد فيه نقيض مطلوب ما ويضاف إليه مقدمة مسلمة لإبطال مسلم لكن ما به الافتراق بينهما أن عكس القياس لا يكون إلا بعد قياس آخر، والخلف فلا يكون إلا مبتدأ. وأيضاً فإن المأخوذ في عكس القياس قد يكون ضد مطلوب ما، وقد يكون نقيضه، والمأخوذ فى الخلف ليس إلا النقيض. وأيضاً فإن المأخوذ في عكس القياس ليس إلا نقيض مطلوب هو نتيجة، ولا كذلك في الخلف.
And based on this, the difference between the direct and the reductio is that in the direct, the aim initially is to demonstrate the desired [conclusion] by setting up premises that are conceded, either absolutely or relatively. In the reductio, the aim initially is to demonstrate an impossible matter, either absolutely or relatively, which is other than the desired [conclusion]; then after that it deduces the falsehood of what resembles it in order to demonstrate the truth of its contradictory. Also, in the direct, there exist only premises that are in agreement with the desired conclusion per se; in the reductio, one of them is the contradictory of the desired. Also, in the direct, the conclusion does not have its subject as a first [intention]; in the reductio, the opposite. Also, the desired in the direct is completed with one syllogism; in the reductio, it is completed with two syllogisms. Indeed, reduction of the reductio to the direct by way of conversion of the syllogism has resembled the reductio syllogism in that each of the reductio syllogism and the conversion of the syllogism — whether that is a conversion of the reductio syllogism or otherwise — may contain the contradictory of some desired [proposition] and to it is added a conceded premise to refute a conceded [proposition]. But the distinction between them is that conversion of a syllogism occurs only after another syllogism, whereas the reductio occurs only as a starting [point]. Also, what is taken in conversion of a syllogism may be the contrary of some desired, or its contradictory; but what is taken in the reductio is only the contradictory. Also, what is taken in conversion of a syllogism is only the contradictory of a desired [conclusion] that is a result; not so in the reductio.

Commentary
This passage systematically distinguishes between direct syllogism (al-mustaqīm) and argument by contradiction (al-khalf). The author presents four main differences and then an additional nuance regarding conversion (ʿaks al-qiyās). Compound Syllogisms from Opposed Premises: Concept and Purpose
Author defines compound syllogisms from opposed premises and their use in jadal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two types of opposition (essential and apparent) used in compound syllogisms for dialectical refutation and explain their role in jadal.

Core Idea
Refute an opponent by secretly replacing his premises with their opposites to derive the opposite conclusion.

In the sixth art of logic, we learn how to construct compound syllogisms that turn an opponent's own argument against him. The key is to use opposed premises, either by true contradiction or contrariety, or by apparent opposition through related terms. This technique is the backbone of dialectical refutation (jadal) and requires careful management of figures and moods.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original syllogism and its conclusion
Determine the first syllogism that the opponent concedes. For example, from the text: 'Every human is an animal' (major premise) and 'Every animal is mortal' (minor premise) leading to 'Every human is mortal'.
The author uses paired propositions but we can construct a full syllogism.
2
Recognize the type of opposition intended
The refuter plans to use an opposite of one or both premises. Opposition can be essential (tadadd or tanaqud) or apparent (by substituting a term with its concomitant or part). Example: 'Every human is an animal' opposed by 'No human is an animal' (tadadd) or 'Not every human is an animal' (tanaqud).
3
Understand the dialectical purpose
In jadal, the refuter elicits a second set of premises from the opponent that are contrary to the first set, then constructs a new syllogism that yields the opposite of the original conclusion, thereby refuting the opponent's position.

نقول: قد يؤلف قياس من مقدمات لإنتاج مطلوب ما، ثم يؤلف بالتخيل على وجه خفي، إما بتبديل لفظ بلفظ مرادف أو كلي بجزئيته، أو جزئي بكليته من مقابل تلك المقدمات أو بعضها، إما بالتضاد أو بالتناقض، قياس آخر، لإنتاج مقابل المطلوب المسلم، وذلك كثيراً ما يستعمل في الجدال لتبكيت المناظر، حيث يتسلم منه قول ثم يتسلم منه أصول أخرى مقابلة للأصول الأولى أو بعضها، ينتج مقابل ما سلمه أولاً، لكن منه ما المأخوذ فيه المقابل بالحقيقة، ومنه ما المأخوذ فيه المقابل بالظن، وقد يكون ذلك على سبيل الاقتران، وقد يكون على سبيل الاستثناء، فإن كان على سبيل الاقتران فالمأخوذ المقابل سواء كان مقابلاً بالذات - أي بالتضاد أو التناقض - كقولنا كل إنسان حيوان و لا شيء من الإنسان حيوان أو ليس كل إنسان حيواناً أو مقابلاً بالظن لا بالحقيقة، بأن يكون الحكم بإيجاب شيء لشيء وسلبه عن لازم ذلك الشيء أو بالعكس، كما يقال: كل إنسان حيوان و لا شيء من الناطق حيوان أو لا شيء من الإنسان حيوان وكل ناطق حيوان أو أن يكون الحكم بإيجاب شيء لشيء وسلبه من جزئي ذلك الشيء أو بالعكس، كما يقال: كل علم ظن و لا شيء من الظن ظن أو لا شيء من العلم ظن وكل طب ظن فالقياس الأول إما أن تكون مقدماته كلها موجبة أو أن إحداهما سالبة، فإن كانت موجبة فلا يمكن أخذ مقابلها لاستنتاج مقابل المطلوب المسلم أولاً؛ إذ المقابل لها كان بالتضاد أو التناقض ليس إلا سالباً، ولا قياس عن سالبتين ولا تبال من أي شكل كان.
نعم، إن أمكن ذلك فليس إلا بأخذ مقابل إحداهما، لكن إن كانت نتيجة النظم الأول موجبة فالنظم الثاني إما من الشكل الأول أو الثاني أو الثالث أو الرابع، فإن كان من الأول فلا يكون المقابل المأخوذ فيه جزئياً، فإنه لا يكون صغرى؛ لكونه سالباً، ولا كبرى لكونه جزئياً، فإذن لا بد أن يكون كلياً، ولا يتصور أن يكون الأكبر لكونه سالباً، وإن كان من النظم الثاني أمكن أن يكون جزئياً وكلياً، لكن إن كان جزئياً فلا يتصور أن يكون إلا صغرى وإن كان كلياً أمكن أن يكون صغرى وكبرى، وإن كان من الشكل الثالث أمكن أيضاً أن يكون جزئياً وكلياً، وعلى كلا التقديرين فلا يتصور أن يكون إلا كبرى، وإن كان من الشكل الرابع لم يمكن أن يكون إلا كلياً؛ إذ الجزئي السالب فيه لا يتصور أن يكون لا صغرى ولا كبرى، وإن كان كلياً أمكن أن يكون صغرى وكبرى لكن ما كان هذه الاقترانات منتجاً من للكلي السالب فالمطلوب فيه مقابل لما سلم أولاً بالتضاد وإلا فبالتناقض، وإن كانت نتيجة النظم الأول جزئية موجبة فلا بد أن يكون المقابل المأخوذ في النظم الثاني كلياً، والمقدمة المقترنة به أيضاً كلية وأن يكون اقترانهما اقتراناً ينتج الكلي السالب كالثاني من الأول والأول والثاني من الثاني والأول من الرابع، وإلا فإن كانت نتيجته جزئية فلا تقابل بين الجزئيتين لا بالتضاد ولا بالتناقض وأما إن كانت إحدى المقدمتين من النظم الأول سالبة فنتيجته أيضاً إما كلية أو جزئية، فإن كانت كليّة لم يجز أيضاً أخذ مقابل المقدمتين في النظم الثاني، وإلا فالنتيجة إن كانت ولا بُدّ غير مخالفة لما سلم من المطلوب أولاً في الكيف فلا تقابل، بل المأخوذ فيه لا يكون إلا مقابل إحداهما وهي سالبة لا موجبة؛ إذ مقابل الموجبة سالبة، ولا سبيل إلى اقترانه بالسالبة الأخرى؛ إذ لا قياس عن سالبتين، وإذا كان المأخوذ فيه إنما هو مقابل السالبة من مقدمتي النظم الأول فمقدمتاه موجبة، وليس يتصور إنتاج الموجب من الموجبتين من غير الشكل الأول والثالث والرابع على ما عرف، وعند ذلك فالمقابل إن كان كلياً أمكن جعله صغرى أو كبرى من هذه الأشكال الثلاثة، وإن كان جزئياً فهو لا يكون من الأول إلا صغرى، ومن الثالث قد يكون صغرى وكبري، ومن الرابع كبرى. وكل قياس أنتج من هذه الجزئيتين فمطلوبه مقابل للمطلوب المسلم أولاً بالتضاد وإلا فبالتناقض.
وأما إن كانت نتيجته جزئية فلا يمكن أيضاً في النظم الثاني أخذ مقابل
We say: A syllogism may be composed from premises to produce some required conclusion. Then another syllogism is imaginatively composed in a hidden manner, either by substituting a word with a synonym or a universal with its particular or a particular with its universal, from the opposite of those premises or some of them, either by opposition (tadadd) or by contradiction (tanaqud), to produce the opposite of the accepted conclusion. This is often used in dialectic (jadal) to refute the disputant, where a statement is taken from him, then other opposed principles are taken from him, opposite to the first principles or some of them, producing the opposite of what he first conceded. But some of these take the opposite in truth, and some take the opposite in estimation. And this may be in the mode of conjunction (iqtiran) or in the mode of exception (istithna'). If it is in the mode of conjunction, then the taken opposite, whether it is essential opposition – i.e., by tadadd or tanaqud – like our saying 'Every human is an animal' and 'No human is an animal' or 'Not every human is an animal', or is opposition in estimation not in truth, such as the judgment of affirming something of something and negating it from a necessary concomitant of that thing or vice versa, as is said: 'Every human is an animal' and 'No rational is an animal' or 'No human is an animal' and 'Every rational is an animal', or the judgment of affirming something of something and negating it from a particular of that thing or vice versa, as is said: 'Every knowledge is opinion' and 'No opinion is opinion' or 'No knowledge is opinion' and 'Every medicine is opinion'. So the first syllogism either has both premises affirmative or one of them negative. If they are affirmative, then it is not possible to take their opposite to deduce the opposite of the first conceded conclusion; because its opposite by tadadd or tanaqud is only negative, and there is no syllogism from two negatives, regardless of which figure. Yes, if possible, it is only by taking the opposite of one of them. But if the conclusion of the first arrangement is affirmative, then the second arrangement is either from the first, second, third, or fourth figure. If it is from the first, then the taken opposite cannot be particular; because it would not be a minor premise due to being negative, nor a major due to being particular. So it must be universal, and it is inconceivable that it be the major because it is negative. If it is from the second arrangement, it can be particular or universal, but if particular it can only be minor, and if universal it can be minor or major. If it is from the third figure, it can also be particular or universal, and in both cases it can only be major. If it is from the fourth figure, it can only be universal; for the particular negative in it cannot be minor nor major, and if universal it can be minor or major. But whatever in these conjunctions produces a universal negative, the required conclusion is the opposite of what was first conceded by tadadd, otherwise by tanaqud. If the conclusion of the first arrangement is particular affirmative, then the opposite taken in the second arrangement must be universal, and the premise conjoined with it also must be universal, and their conjunction must be a conjunction that produces a universal negative, like the second of the first, the first and second of the second, and the first of the fourth. Otherwise, if its conclusion is particular, there is no opposition between two particulars, neither by tadadd nor by tanaqud. And if one of the two premises of the first arrangement is negative, then its conclusion is either universal or particular. If it is universal, it is also not permissible to take the opposite of both premises in the second arrangement. Otherwise, if the conclusion, necessarily, is not different from what was conceded of the first required conclusion in quality, then there is no opposition. Rather the taken opposite can only be the opposite of one of them, and it is negative, not affirmative; because the opposite of an affirmative is negative, and there is no way to conjoin it with the other negative, since there is no syllogism from two negatives. And if the taken opposite is only the opposite of the negative premise from the two premises of the first arrangement, then its two premises are affirmative, and it is not conceivable to produce an affirmative from two affirmatives except from the first, third, and fourth figures, as known. In that case, if the opposite is universal, it can be made minor or major in these three figures; if it is particular, then from the first it is only minor, from the third it may be minor and major, and from the fourth it is major. And every syllogism that produces from these two particulars has its required conclusion opposite to the first conceded required conclusion by tanaqud, otherwise by tadadd. As for if its conclusion is particular, it is also not possible in the second arrangement to take an opposite...

Commentary
This passage defines the sixth art of logic: compound syllogisms composed from opposed premises (al-muqabilat). The author describes how, in dialectical debate, a first syllogism is constructed to prove a conclusion. Then, by substituting terms with their synonyms, or universals with particulars (or vice versa), and using opposites (either by tadadd – contrariety, or tanaqud – contradiction), a second syllogism is formed covertly to produce the opposite conclusion, thus refuting the opponent. This is practiced in jadal to force the opponent into inconsistency. The author distinguishes between true opposition (bi-l-haqiqa) and apparent opposition (bi-l-zann), giving examples: for true opposition, e.g., 'Every human is an animal' vs. 'No human is an animal'; for apparent opposition, e.g., 'Every human is an animal' vs. 'No rational is an animal' (since 'rational' is a necessary concomitant of human). The discussion then shifts to the conditions under which such compound syllogisms are valid. If the first syllogism has both premises affirmative, its opposite can only be negative, but two negatives cannot yield a conclusion; so only the opposite of one premise can be taken. The author then examines the resulting figures and moods: given an affirmative conclusion from the first arrangement, the second arrangement can use various figures (1-4) with constraints on the universality/particularity and negativity of the opposite premise. For instance, in the first figure the opposite must be universal; in the second it can be particular (as minor) or universal (as minor or major), etc. The aim is to produce a universal negative conclusion that opposes the original conclusion by tadadd (contrariety) or tanaqud (contradiction). Special cases are treated: if the first conclusion is particular affirmative, the opposite must be universal and the conjunction must yield a universal negative; otherwise, no opposition is possible between two particulars. If one premise of the first syllogism is negative, the second arrangement can only take the opposite of that negative premise (which becomes an affirmative), and the resulting syllogism must come from the first, third, or fourth figures. Throughout, the author emphasizes that not every combination works; the figures and moods must be chosen carefully to produce valid opposition. This passage is a technical exposition of advanced dialectical strategy, showing how to construct refutations by manipulating premises in structured ways.

Checkpoint
What is the primary purpose of constructing a syllogism from opposed premises in jadal?
A) To produce a likely conclusion from uncertain premises
B) To refute the disputant by producing the opposite of his conceded conclusion
C) To demonstrate the truth of a proposition by analogy
D) To combine two syllogisms into one without transformation

Key Takeaway
Compound syllogisms from opposed premises are a powerful dialectical tool, but they require precise handling of opposition types and logical structure.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Given the syllogism 'Every intoxicant is forbidden, every wine is intoxicant, therefore wine is forbidden', identify an essential opposite premise (by tadadd) and an apparent opposite premise (by substituting 'wine' with its particular 'red wine') that could be used to construct a refuting compound syllogism.
?
In a jadal scenario, imagine your opponent concedes 'Every miracle is from Allah'. How might you use a compound syllogism from opposed premises to refute him? Specify the type of opposition.
