The Syllogism
advanced
12 min
Conditions for Valid Compound Syllogisms: Figure and Mood Constraints
Author explains validity conditions for compound syllogisms when first syllogism has both affirmative premises.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct a valid compound syllogism from opposed premises by applying the figure and mood conditions when the first syllogism has both affirmative premises.

Core Idea
When the original syllogism is all-affirmative, the second syllogism can only use one opposed premise, and it must be universal and negative, fitting a valid mood.

Once you understand the concept of opposed premises, the next challenge is ensuring the compound syllogism is logically valid. The author details strict constraints: if the first syllogism has both premises affirmative, its opposite would be a negative, but two negatives yield no conclusion. So only one premise can be replaced. Moreover, the figure and mood of the second syllogism must produce a universal negative to achieve proper opposition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the premises and conclusion of the first syllogism
Suppose the first syllogism is in Barbara (AAA-1): all A are B, all B are C, therefore all A are C. The premises are both universal affirmatives.
2
Determine that only one premise can be opposed
Since both are affirmative, taking opposite of both yields two negatives, which cannot produce a conclusion. So we must choose to oppose only one premise, e.g., take 'No A is B' (E) against 'all A is B' (A).
3
Check the figure and mood conditions
The author analyzes each figure. For example, in the first figure, the opposite premise must be a universal negative and must be the major premise. In Celarent (EAE-1), with minor affirmative and major negative: all B are C (A), no A is B (E) yields no A is C. But here the original minor is all A is B, so we need to adjust the order. The key is that the second syllogism must produce a universal negative conclusion through a valid mood.
The passage details constraints for each figure; we focus on the first figure case.
4
Produce the opposite conclusion
If the conditions are satisfied, the second syllogism yields a universal negative conclusion (e.g., no A is C), which is the contradictory of the original conclusion (all A is C) by tadadd.

نقول: قد يؤلف قياس من مقدمات لإنتاج مطلوب ما، ثم يؤلف بالتخيل على وجه خفي، إما بتبديل لفظ بلفظ مرادف أو كلي بجزئيته، أو جزئي بكليته من مقابل تلك المقدمات أو بعضها، إما بالتضاد أو بالتناقض، قياس آخر، لإنتاج مقابل المطلوب المسلم، وذلك كثيراً ما يستعمل في الجدال لتبكيت المناظر، حيث يتسلم منه قول ثم يتسلم منه أصول أخرى مقابلة للأصول الأولى أو بعضها، ينتج مقابل ما سلمه أولاً، لكن منه ما المأخوذ فيه المقابل بالحقيقة، ومنه ما المأخوذ فيه المقابل بالظن، وقد يكون ذلك على سبيل الاقتران، وقد يكون على سبيل الاستثناء، فإن كان على سبيل الاقتران فالمأخوذ المقابل سواء كان مقابلاً بالذات - أي بالتضاد أو التناقض - كقولنا كل إنسان حيوان و لا شيء من الإنسان حيوان أو ليس كل إنسان حيواناً أو مقابلاً بالظن لا بالحقيقة، بأن يكون الحكم بإيجاب شيء لشيء وسلبه عن لازم ذلك الشيء أو بالعكس، كما يقال: كل إنسان حيوان و لا شيء من الناطق حيوان أو لا شيء من الإنسان حيوان وكل ناطق حيوان أو أن يكون الحكم بإيجاب شيء لشيء وسلبه من جزئي ذلك الشيء أو بالعكس، كما يقال: كل علم ظن و لا شيء من الظن ظن أو لا شيء من العلم ظن وكل طب ظن فالقياس الأول إما أن تكون مقدماته كلها موجبة أو أن إحداهما سالبة، فإن كانت موجبة فلا يمكن أخذ مقابلها لاستنتاج مقابل المطلوب المسلم أولاً؛ إذ المقابل لها كان بالتضاد أو التناقض ليس إلا سالباً، ولا قياس عن سالبتين ولا تبال من أي شكل كان.
نعم، إن أمكن ذلك فليس إلا بأخذ مقابل إحداهما، لكن إن كانت نتيجة النظم الأول موجبة فالنظم الثاني إما من الشكل الأول أو الثاني أو الثالث أو الرابع، فإن كان من الأول فلا يكون المقابل المأخوذ فيه جزئياً، فإنه لا يكون صغرى؛ لكونه سالباً، ولا كبرى لكونه جزئياً، فإذن لا بد أن يكون كلياً، ولا يتصور أن يكون الأكبر لكونه سالباً، وإن كان من النظم الثاني أمكن أن يكون جزئياً وكلياً، لكن إن كان جزئياً فلا يتصور أن يكون إلا صغرى وإن كان كلياً أمكن أن يكون صغرى وكبرى، وإن كان من الشكل الثالث أمكن أيضاً أن يكون جزئياً وكلياً، وعلى كلا التقديرين فلا يتصور أن يكون إلا كبرى، وإن كان من الشكل الرابع لم يمكن أن يكون إلا كلياً؛ إذ الجزئي السالب فيه لا يتصور أن يكون لا صغرى ولا كبرى، وإن كان كلياً أمكن أن يكون صغرى وكبرى لكن ما كان هذه الاقترانات منتجاً من للكلي السالب فالمطلوب فيه مقابل لما سلم أولاً بالتضاد وإلا فبالتناقض، وإن كانت نتيجة النظم الأول جزئية موجبة فلا بد أن يكون المقابل المأخوذ في النظم الثاني كلياً، والمقدمة المقترنة به أيضاً كلية وأن يكون اقترانهما اقتراناً ينتج الكلي السالب كالثاني من الأول والأول والثاني من الثاني والأول من الرابع، وإلا فإن كانت نتيجته جزئية فلا تقابل بين الجزئيتين لا بالتضاد ولا بالتناقض وأما إن كانت إحدى المقدمتين من النظم الأول سالبة فنتيجته أيضاً إما كلية أو جزئية، فإن كانت كليّة لم يجز أيضاً أخذ مقابل المقدمتين في النظم الثاني، وإلا فالنتيجة إن كانت ولا بُدّ غير مخالفة لما سلم من المطلوب أولاً في الكيف فلا تقابل، بل المأخوذ فيه لا يكون إلا مقابل إحداهما وهي سالبة لا موجبة؛ إذ مقابل الموجبة سالبة، ولا سبيل إلى اقترانه بالسالبة الأخرى؛ إذ لا قياس عن سالبتين، وإذا كان المأخوذ فيه إنما هو مقابل السالبة من مقدمتي النظم الأول فمقدمتاه موجبة، وليس يتصور إنتاج الموجب من الموجبتين من غير الشكل الأول والثالث والرابع على ما عرف، وعند ذلك فالمقابل إن كان كلياً أمكن جعله صغرى أو كبرى من هذه الأشكال الثلاثة، وإن كان جزئياً فهو لا يكون من الأول إلا صغرى، ومن الثالث قد يكون صغرى وكبري، ومن الرابع كبرى. وكل قياس أنتج من هذه الجزئيتين فمطلوبه مقابل للمطلوب المسلم أولاً بالتضاد وإلا فبالتناقض.
وأما إن كانت نتيجته جزئية فلا يمكن أيضاً في النظم الثاني أخذ مقابل
We say: A syllogism may be composed from premises to produce some required conclusion. Then another syllogism is imaginatively composed in a hidden manner, either by substituting a word with a synonym or a universal with its particular or a particular with its universal, from the opposite of those premises or some of them, either by opposition (tadadd) or by contradiction (tanaqud), to produce the opposite of the accepted conclusion. This is often used in dialectic (jadal) to refute the disputant, where a statement is taken from him, then other opposed principles are taken from him, opposite to the first principles or some of them, producing the opposite of what he first conceded. But some of these take the opposite in truth, and some take the opposite in estimation. And this may be in the mode of conjunction (iqtiran) or in the mode of exception (istithna'). If it is in the mode of conjunction, then the taken opposite, whether it is essential opposition – i.e., by tadadd or tanaqud – like our saying 'Every human is an animal' and 'No human is an animal' or 'Not every human is an animal', or is opposition in estimation not in truth, such as the judgment of affirming something of something and negating it from a necessary concomitant of that thing or vice versa, as is said: 'Every human is an animal' and 'No rational is an animal' or 'No human is an animal' and 'Every rational is an animal', or the judgment of affirming something of something and negating it from a particular of that thing or vice versa, as is said: 'Every knowledge is opinion' and 'No opinion is opinion' or 'No knowledge is opinion' and 'Every medicine is opinion'. So the first syllogism either has both premises affirmative or one of them negative. If they are affirmative, then it is not possible to take their opposite to deduce the opposite of the first conceded conclusion; because its opposite by tadadd or tanaqud is only negative, and there is no syllogism from two negatives, regardless of which figure. Yes, if possible, it is only by taking the opposite of one of them. But if the conclusion of the first arrangement is affirmative, then the second arrangement is either from the first, second, third, or fourth figure. If it is from the first, then the taken opposite cannot be particular; because it would not be a minor premise due to being negative, nor a major due to being particular. So it must be universal, and it is inconceivable that it be the major because it is negative. If it is from the second arrangement, it can be particular or universal, but if particular it can only be minor, and if universal it can be minor or major. If it is from the third figure, it can also be particular or universal, and in both cases it can only be major. If it is from the fourth figure, it can only be universal; for the particular negative in it cannot be minor nor major, and if universal it can be minor or major. But whatever in these conjunctions produces a universal negative, the required conclusion is the opposite of what was first conceded by tadadd, otherwise by tanaqud. If the conclusion of the first arrangement is particular affirmative, then the opposite taken in the second arrangement must be universal, and the premise conjoined with it also must be universal, and their conjunction must be a conjunction that produces a universal negative, like the second of the first, the first and second of the second, and the first of the fourth. Otherwise, if its conclusion is particular, there is no opposition between two particulars, neither by tadadd nor by tanaqud. And if one of the two premises of the first arrangement is negative, then its conclusion is either universal or particular. If it is universal, it is also not permissible to take the opposite of both premises in the second arrangement. Otherwise, if the conclusion, necessarily, is not different from what was conceded of the first required conclusion in quality, then there is no opposition. Rather the taken opposite can only be the opposite of one of them, and it is negative, not affirmative; because the opposite of an affirmative is negative, and there is no way to conjoin it with the other negative, since there is no syllogism from two negatives. And if the taken opposite is only the opposite of the negative premise from the two premises of the first arrangement, then its two premises are affirmative, and it is not conceivable to produce an affirmative from two affirmatives except from the first, third, and fourth figures, as known. In that case, if the opposite is universal, it can be made minor or major in these three figures; if it is particular, then from the first it is only minor, from the third it may be minor and major, and from the fourth it is major. And every syllogism that produces from these two particulars has its required conclusion opposite to the first conceded required conclusion by tanaqud, otherwise by tadadd. As for if its conclusion is particular, it is also not possible in the second arrangement to take an opposite...
Source pages p. 457

Commentary
This passage defines the sixth art of logic: compound syllogisms composed from opposed premises (al-muqabilat). The author describes how, in dialectical debate, a first syllogism is constructed to prove a conclusion. Then, by substituting terms with their synonyms, or universals with particulars (or vice versa), and using opposites (either by tadadd – contrariety, or tanaqud – contradiction), a second syllogism is formed covertly to produce the opposite conclusion, thus refuting the opponent. This is practiced in jadal to force the opponent into inconsistency. The author distinguishes between true opposition (bi-l-haqiqa) and apparent opposition (bi-l-zann), giving examples: for true opposition, e.g., 'Every human is an animal' vs. 'No human is an animal'; for apparent opposition, e.g., 'Every human is an animal' vs. 'No rational is an animal' (since 'rational' is a necessary concomitant of human). The discussion then shifts to the conditions under which such compound syllogisms are valid. If the first syllogism has both premises affirmative, its opposite can only be negative, but two negatives cannot yield a conclusion; so only the opposite of one premise can be taken. The author then examines the resulting figures and moods: given an affirmative conclusion from the first arrangement, the second arrangement can use various figures (1-4) with constraints on the universality/particularity and negativity of the opposite premise. For instance, in the first figure the opposite must be universal; in the second it can be particular (as minor) or universal (as minor or major), etc. The aim is to produce a universal negative conclusion that opposes the original conclusion by tadadd (contrariety) or tanaqud (contradiction). Special cases are treated: if the first conclusion is particular affirmative, the opposite must be universal and the conjunction must yield a universal negative; otherwise, no opposition is possible between two particulars. If one premise of the first syllogism is negative, the second arrangement can only take the opposite of that negative premise (which becomes an affirmative), and the resulting syllogism must come from the first, third, or fourth figures. Throughout, the author emphasizes that not every combination works; the figures and moods must be chosen carefully to produce valid opposition. This passage is a technical exposition of advanced dialectical strategy, showing how to construct refutations by manipulating premises in structured ways.

مقدمة موجبة
مقدمة سالبة
كلية سالبة

The Syllogism
advanced
12 min
Conditions for Constructing a Second Syllogism from Contradictory Premises
Author rules for taking contradictory premises in a second syllogism to produce an opposite conclusion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine whether a second syllogism can be constructed to oppose a previous conclusion by applying the rules regarding the quality and quantity of the first syllogism's conclusion and premises.

Core Idea
A second syllogism from contradictory premises requires a universal negative conclusion or a negative premise in the first syllogism.

In dialectical debates, after establishing a conclusion, one may need to derive the opposite conclusion to refute an opponent. The author sets forth precise conditions for when and how to construct a second syllogism from the contradictory of a premise in the first syllogism. This lesson guides you through those conditions using Islamic debate examples.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conclusion of the first syllogism
Determine whether the result is universal or particular, and affirmative or negative. For example, if the first syllogism aims to prove 'All intoxicants are forbidden' (universal affirmative), that is a different case than 'No intoxicant is permissible' (universal negative).
Focus on the quality (affirmative/negative) and quantity (universal/particular).
2
Check if the conclusion is universal negative
If the first result is a universal negative (e.g., 'No wine is permissible'), then a second syllogism is possible. You must take the opposite of one premise—either contradictory or contrary—to produce an opposite conclusion. The opposite premise must be universal and paired to yield a universal negative in certain figures.
The passage details which figures permit this: combinations like 'second from the first, first and second from the second, and first from the fourth'.
3
If the conclusion is particular affirmative
When the first result is a particular affirmative (e.g., 'Some wine is permissible'), the opposite taken must be universal, and the other premise also universal. Their pairing must produce a universal negative conclusion. Otherwise, no opposition exists between the two particular conclusions.
The passage warns that no syllogism yields a particular opposite from a particular.
4
If one premise of the first syllogism is negative
When a premise is negative, the opposite of only the negative premise can be taken—never the affirmative. The opposite of an affirmative is negative, and pairing two negatives yields no syllogism. The new premises must be affirmative, and the second syllogism must follow figures 1, 3, or 4.
The opposite taken can be universal (then it may be major or minor in these figures) or particular (only minor in first, etc.).
5
Apply the rules to an Islamic debate example
Test a case: First syllogism (Barbara): 'Every intoxicant is forbidden. Every nabidh is intoxicant. Therefore every nabidh is forbidden.' The conclusion is universal affirmative. According to the passage, can a second syllogism be constructed? The answer is no, because the conclusion is not universal negative, and no premise is negative. Thus, no second syllogism from contradictory premises is possible here.
This shows that not every syllogism allows a contradictory sequel.

The sixth branch: on syllogisms composed of contradictory premises. ... negative, and no major premise because it is particular; so it must be universal, and the major cannot be conceived as negative. And if it is from the second figure, it can be particular or universal, but if particular, it can only be minor, and if universal, it can be minor or major. Otherwise, if its result is particular, there is no opposition between two particulars, neither by contrariety nor contradiction. And then the second system is not conceivable except from the first pairing of the first. And consider an example of each of these by yourself.

Commentary
This passage is a detailed exposition of the conditions for constructing a second syllogism that uses contradictory premises to oppose a previously conceded conclusion. The author, likely following Aristotle, explains that when the first syllogism yields a universal negative conclusion, the second syllogism must take as a premise the opposite of one of the original premises, ensuring the new combination produces a contradictory or contrary opposite. The discussion proceeds through cases: when the result is a universal negative, particular affirmative, or when one premise is negative. For instance, if the result is a universal negative, the opposite taken must be universal and paired to yield a universal negative in certain figures. The author also notes that a second syllogism cannot be formed if the result is particular because there is no opposition between particulars. The example phrases like 'every lion is a lion' (though trivial) illustrate the logical patterns. The passage ends by urging the reader to test each case with examples, emphasizing the practical application of these rules.

Real and Imaginary Opposition in Syllogisms
Author distinguishes real and imaginary opposition in syllogisms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish real from imaginary opposition in syllogisms with contradictory premises and identify which syllogistic figures are possible for each type.

Core Idea
Real opposition uses synonyms; imaginary opposition restricts to the second figure.

The author raises a subtle question: can two contradictory premises within a single syllogism form a valid argument? He distinguishes two types of opposition—real (where terms are synonymous) and imaginary (where predicate is same but subject different)—and shows that each type constrains which syllogistic figures are possible. This distinction helps prevent fallacies when dealing with seemingly contradictory premises.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two premises and check if they are contradictory.
Look for a pair of premises that are opposites (one affirmative, one negative). Determine if they are intended as part of one syllogism.
Contradictory premises are required for this analysis.
2
Determine if the three terms are synonymous (real opposition) or if the predicate is repeated while subjects differ (imaginary opposition).
If all three terms are synonyms (e.g., layth, asad, ghudanfar all meaning lion), opposition is real. If the predicate is the same but subjects differ (e.g., 'human' and 'speaking' both with 'animal' as predicate), opposition is imaginary.
Real opposition requires synonymous terms; otherwise it's imaginary.
3
Apply the figure conditions for each type.
For real opposition, the syllogism can be in first, third, or fourth figure depending on term arrangement. For imaginary opposition, only the second figure is possible because the common predicate serves as the middle term.
The second figure has the predicate as middle term.
4
Test with concrete examples from the passage.
Real: 'Every lion is a lion' and 'Nothing of the ghudanfar is a lion' (first figure). Imaginary: 'Every human is an animal' and 'Nothing of the speaking is an animal' (second figure).
The examples illustrate the term arrangements for each figure.

And can opposition between the two premises of a single syllogism be conceived in a way that leads to error or not? We say: either it produces an affirmative or a negative. If it produces an affirmative, then no, since the two opposites must have one negative, and producing an affirmative from a negative is inconceivable. And if it produces a negative, then the opposition is either real or imaginary. If real, then it is only possible if the names of the three terms are synonymous; two are taken as the two terms of the desired conclusion and one as the middle term. If from the first figure, the terms are taken such that the predicate in the minor and the subject in the major are one, and the subject in the minor and the predicate in the major are different, as when one says: 'Every lion is a lion' [actually 'Every lion is a lion' but should be 'Every lion is a lion'? The example given: 'Every lion (layth) is a lion (asad)' and 'Nothing of the ghudanfar is a lion (asad)'], and if from the third figure, the terms are taken such that the subject is one and the predicate different, as when one says: 'Every lion is a lion' and 'Nothing of the lion is a ghudanfar'. And if from the fourth figure, the terms are taken such that the subject in the minor and the predicate in the major are one, and the predicate in the minor and the subject in the major are different, as when one says: 'Every lion is a lion' and 'Nothing of the ghudanfar is a lion'. But if the opposition is imaginary not real, as known, then the predicate in the two premises must be one and the subject different, as when one says: 'Every human is an animal' and 'Nothing of the speaking (faculty) is an animal'. Based on this, from the opposites in this sense, a syllogism from the first, third, or fourth figure cannot be prepared; it can only be prepared from the second figure with its different moods. Source pages p. 459

Commentary
This passage addresses a specific question: can two contradictory premises within a single syllogism (i.e., a pair of premises that are opposites) produce a valid syllogism, or does this lead to fallacy? The author distinguishes two types of opposition: real (haqīqī) and imaginary (maẓnūn). Real opposition occurs when the three terms are synonymous names (like 'layth' and 'asad' both meaning lion, and 'ghudanfar' also meaning lion) — in such cases, the terms are effectively the same, and the syllogism works in the first, third, and fourth figures. The examples given are trivial: 'Every lion is a lion' and 'Nothing of the ghudanfar is a lion' produce a negative conclusion because the terms are synonymous. Imaginary opposition, on the other hand, occurs when the terms are not synonymous but the predicate is the same and the subject different, as in 'Every human is an animal' and 'Nothing of the speaking is an animal'. Here the predicate 'animal' is repeated, but the subjects 'human' and 'speaking' are different. In this case, only the second figure can form a valid syllogism, because the middle term is the predicate common to both premises. The passage is cut off while discussing real opposition without synonymy, but the main point is clear: real opposition with synonymy allows multiple figures; imaginary opposition forces the second figure.

The Syllogism

Distinguishing Real and Imaginary Opposition in Syllogisms
Author examines types of opposition in premises

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify opposition between syllogistic premises as real (haqiqi) or imaginary (zanni) and identify the syllogistic figures that can accommodate each type.

Core Idea
Real opposition requires synonymous terms; imaginary opposition only works in the second figure.

In this passage, the author investigates whether two premises of a single syllogism can be opposite in a way that misleads. He distinguishes real opposition (where terms are synonymous) from imaginary opposition (where only the predicate is shared). This distinction is essential for verifying the validity of any two-premise argument in Islamic logic, especially in theological reasoning where terms may be used loosely.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conclusion type
Determine if the syllogism aims for an affirmative or negative conclusion. Only negative conclusions can involve opposite premises, because one premise must be negative for opposition to occur.
The author says: 'if it produces an affirmative, then no; for opposites must have a negative.'
2
Check for synonymy
If the three terms are synonymous (e.g., layth, asad, ghudanfar all meaning lion), the opposition is real. If the predicate is identical but subjects differ, the opposition is imaginary.
Real opposition is called 'haqiqi', imaginary is 'bi al-zann'.
3
Assign to correct figure
Real opposition can appear in first, third, or fourth figures with specific arrangements of synonymous terms. Imaginary opposition only fits the second figure.
For the first figure: predicate in minor = subject in major; for third: subject unified; for fourth: subject in minor = predicate in major.
4
Test with an Islamic example
Suppose we have 'Every prayer (salat) is worship ('ibada)' and 'No fasting (sawm) is worship'. Predicate same, subjects differ: this is imaginary opposition. It only works in the second figure (conclusion: No fasting is prayer).
If placed in the first figure, no valid conclusion follows.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
دقائق الحقائق الجزء الثاني تتمة المقالة الرابعة في تعريف صور الحجج المقدمتين في النظم الأول لإنتاج مقابل مطلوبها؛ إذ لا اختلاف في الكيف، ولا أخذ مقابل الموجبة منها أيضاً لما عرف، بل المأخوذ إنما هو مقابل السالبة منهما؛ لأن يكون الاقتران الكائن منه ومن المقدمة الأخرى منتجاً للكلي الموجب؛ لأن يقع التقابل بين المطلوبين المُسلّمين؛ إذ لا تقابل بين الجزأين، وعند ذلك فليس يتصور النظم الثاني من غير الاقتران الأول من الأول واعتبر مثال كل واحد من هذه بنفسك. وهل يتصور التقابل بين مقدمتي الاقتران الواحد على وجه يغلط أم لا؟ فنقول: إما أن يكون منتجاً للموجب أو السالب فإن كان منتجاً للموجب فلا؛ إذ المتقابلتان لا بد وأن تكون إحداهما سالبة، وليس يتصور إنتاج الموجب من السالب وإن كان منتجاً للسالب فإما أن يكون التقابل حقيقياً أو مظنوناً، فإن كان حقيقياً فليس يمكن إلا أن تكون أسماء الحدود الثلاثة مترادفة، فيؤخذ منها اثنان على أنهما حدا المطلوب وواحد على أنه حد وسط، فإن كان من الشكل الأول فتؤخذ الحدود على أن المحمول في الصغرى والموضوع في الكبرى واحد، والموضوع في الصغرى والمحمول في الكبرى مختلف، وذلك كما يقال: (كل ليث أسد) و (لا شيء من الغضنفر أسد) وإن كان من الثالث فتؤخذ الحدود على أن الموضوع فيها متحد والمحمول مختلف، وهو كما يقال: (كل ليث أسد) و(لا شيء من الليث غضنفر) وإن كان من الرابع فتؤخذ الحدود على أن الموضوع في الصغرى والمحمول في الكبرى واحد والمحمول في الصغرى والموضوع في الكبرى مختلف، وذلك كما يقال: (كل ليث أسد) و (لا شيء من الغضنفر ليث). أما إن كان التقابل بالظن لا بالحقيقة كما عرف فالمحمول في المقدمتين لا بُدّ وأن يكون واحداً، والموضوع مختلف، وهو كما يقال: (كل إنسان حيوان) و(لا شيء من الناطق حيوان) فعلى هذا ليس يتهيأ من المتقابلين بهذا المعنى قياس من الشكل الأول ولا الثالث ولا الرابع، وإنما يتهيأ ذلك من الشكل الثاني على اختلاف قرائنه. وأما إن كان التقابل حقيقياً ولم يتخيل فيه بأخذ الألفاظ مرادفة، بل كان الموضوع في المقدمتين واحداً، والمحمول واحداً، ولا اختلاف في غير الكيفية، كقولنا: (كل طب علم) و (لا شيء من الطب علم) فإنه وإن لزم منه أنه ليس كل علم علما) فليس الغلط فيه خافيا، ونحن وإن كنا نجوز لزوم النتيجة الصادقة عن المقدمات الكاذبة، فليس يلزمنا تجويز لزوم الصادق عن المتقابلتين؛ إذ اللازم عنهما أن الشيء ليس هو هو) وهو خلف. وأما أنه كيف يعرض للنظار الوقوع في المقاييس من المقدمات المتقابلة بحيث ينتج أن الشيء ليس هو هو من غير شعور، فذلك أنه قد يتفق أن يكون عنده أصل حق موضوع وقياسات فاسدة تنتج نتائج فاسدة يلزم عنها وعن الأصل الموضوع أنه ليس هو هو ومثاله وليكن الموضوع إن بعض الأعداد فرد) والقياس الفاسد (كل عدد منقسم بمتساويين) و (لا شيء من المنقسم بمتساويين فرد) فـ (لا شيء من العدد فرد) فإذا قلت (بعض العدد فرد) و (لا شيء من العدد فرد) أنتج بالنظر إلى الشكل الثاني (بعض العدد ليس بعدد) وبالنظر إلى الشكل الثالث (بعض الفرد ليس بفرد) وربما كان الموضوع باطلاً والقياس صحيح منتج لمقابله، وربما كان عنده قياسان إما كاذبان أو أحدهما نتيجتاهما متقابلتان كما سبق، وربما اتفق وقوع الغلط بإيهام أن المركب ليس هو أحد جزأيه - أي ليس هو هو بمجرده - ثم يجعل المركب حداً أوسط بين الجزء المسلوب وبين ما المركب ثابت له، وهو كما يقال: (زيد حيوان ناطق) و (لا شيء مما هو حيوان ناطق بناطق) ويكون ذلك في ما بينه نفسه، على أنه ليس هو هو) فقط فينتج أن زيداً ليس بناطق وقد كان بعينه ناطقاً، وليس ذلك كله، فما يقع التصور ابتدأ قبل الوقوع في بعض هذه الزلات، أو التخيّل ببعض هذه التخيلات.
Da'iq al-Haqa'iq, part two, continuation of the fourth article on defining the forms of two-premise arguments in the first order to produce the opposite of what is sought; for there is no difference in quality, and the opposite of the affirmative is not taken from it as known, but what is taken is only the opposite of the negative from them; so that the conjunction resulting from it and the other premise produces the universal affirmative; for opposition occurs between the two accepted conclusions, since there is no opposition between the two parts. At that point, the second order is not conceivable without the first conjunction from the first. And you yourself consider an example of each of these. Is opposition conceivable between the two premises of a single conjunction in a way that leads to error or not? We say: either it produces an affirmative or a negative. If it produces an affirmative, then no; for the two opposites must be one of them negative, and it is not conceivable to produce an affirmative from a negative. If it produces a negative, then either the opposition is real or imaginary. If it is real, then it is only possible if the names of the three terms are synonymous; two are taken as the two terms of the conclusion and one as the middle term. If it is of the first figure, then the terms are taken such that the predicate in the minor and the subject in the major are one, and the subject in the minor and the predicate in the major are different. That is as when it is said: 'Every lion (layth) is a lion (asad),' and 'Nothing from a ghudanfar is a lion (asad).' If it is of the third, then the terms are taken such that the subject in them is unified and the predicate different, as when it is said: 'Every lion (layth) is a lion (asad),' and 'Nothing from a lion (layth) is a ghudanfar.' If it is of the fourth, then the terms are taken such that the subject in the minor and the predicate in the major are one, and the predicate in the minor and the subject in the major are different, as when it is said: 'Every lion (layth) is a lion (asad),' and 'Nothing from a ghudanfar is a lion (layth).' As for when the opposition is by opinion, not in reality as known, then the predicate in the two premises must be one, and the subject different. That is as when it is said: 'Every human is an animal,' and 'Nothing from the rational is an animal.' According to this, from the opposites in this sense, a syllogism in the first, third, or fourth figure is not prepared; rather it is prepared from the second figure according to the variation of its contexts. As for when the opposition is real and not imagined by taking terms as synonyms, but rather the subject in the two premises is one, and the predicate one, with no difference except in quality, as when we say: 'Every medicine is knowledge,' and 'Nothing from medicine is knowledge.' Even though it follows from it that 'Not every knowledge is knowledge,' the error in it is not hidden. And even though we allow the occurrence of a true conclusion from false premises, we are not required to allow the occurrence of truth from two opposites; for what is necessary from them is that a thing is not itself, which is absurd. As for how it happens that reasoners fall into syllogisms from opposite premises such that it produces that a thing is not itself without awareness, that is because it may happen that a true principle is set before him and false syllogisms produce false results, and from them and the set principle it follows that it is not itself. An example: let the principle be 'Some numbers are odd,' and the false syllogism: 'Every number is divisible by equals,' and 'Nothing divisible by equals is odd' gives 'No number is odd.' Then if you say 'Some number is odd' and 'No number is odd,' it produces by the second figure 'Some number is not a number,' and by the third figure 'Some odd is not odd.' And sometimes the principle is false and the syllogism is true producing its opposite. And sometimes he has two syllogisms either both false or one of them, their conclusions are opposite as previously. And sometimes error occurs by imagining that the composite is not one of its two parts - that is, it is not itself by itself - then the composite is made a middle term between the part negated and that which the composite is affirmed of. That is as when it is said: 'Zayd is a speaking animal,' and 'Nothing that is a speaking animal is speaking.' And that happens in what is between itself, on the basis that it is not itself, only without awareness? It produces that Zayd is not speaking, while he is precisely speaking. And not all of that: what occurs is the conception initially before falling into some of these slips, or imagination through some of these imaginings.
Source pages p. 459

Commentary
This passage is an analytical discussion of syllogisms that involve contradictory or opposite premises (المقدمات المتقابلة). The author begins by referencing the 'first order' of two-premise arguments that aim to produce the opposite of the desired conclusion. He clarifies that only the opposite of the negative premise is taken, not the affirmative, to achieve a universal affirmative conclusion. The core question is whether two premises of a single syllogism can be opposite in a way that leads to error. The answer distinguishes between syllogisms producing affirmative vs. negative conclusions. If the conclusion is affirmative, no error arises because opposites require a negative premise. If the conclusion is negative, opposition can be real (حقيقي) or imaginary (مظنون). Real opposition occurs when the three terms are synonymous; the author illustrates this with examples in the first, third, and fourth figures using synonymous terms for lion (ليث, أسد, غضنفر). Imaginary opposition occurs when the predicate is the same but subjects differ; this only fits the second figure, as shown with the example 'Every human is an animal' and 'Nothing rational is an animal.' Then the author considers a case of real opposition without synonymy: the subject and predicate are identical, differing only in quality (affirmative vs. negative), e.g., 'Every medicine is knowledge' and 'No medicine is knowledge.' This leads to the absurd conclusion that a thing is not itself (الشيء ليس هو هو), which is logically impossible (خلف). The author notes that while true conclusions can follow from false premises, they cannot follow from contradictory premises. The rest of the passage explains how reasoners inadvertently fall into such absurdity. One scenario combines a true principle (e.g., 'Some numbers are odd') with a false syllogism (e.g., 'Every number is divisible by equals' and 'Nothing divisible by equals is odd') to derive 'No number is odd,' then combining 'Some number is odd' with 'No number is odd' yields, via the second figure, 'Some number is not a number' or via the third figure 'Some odd is not odd.' Another scenario involves two false syllogisms or one false and one true that produce opposite conclusions. Finally, the author describes a sophistical trick: mistaking a composite (e.g., 'speaking animal') as not being one of its parts, then using the composite as a middle term to conclude the absurd negation of that part (e.g., from 'Zayd is a speaking animal' and 'Nothing that is a speaking animal is speaking' to 'Zayd is not speaking'). Throughout, the author emphasizes that such errors arise from confused conception or imagination before reasoning.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, imaginary opposition (al-taqabul bi al-zann) can only produce a valid syllogism in which figure?
A) First figure
B) Second figure
C) Third figure
D) Fourth figure

Key Takeaway
Real opposition demands synonymous terms across figures, while imaginary opposition restricts to the second figure only.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify whether the following pair of premises exhibits real or imaginary opposition: 'Every obligatory act is commanded' and 'No voluntary act is commanded.' Explain which figure, if any, can yield a valid conclusion.
?
Construct a valid first-figure syllogism using synonymous terms for 'knowledge' (e.g., 'ilm, ma'rifa, fahm) with one affirmative and one negative premise, and check that the opposition is real.

The Syllogism
advanced
10 min
The Absurd Conclusion: When Syllogisms Produce Self-Contradiction
Author explains absurdity from opposite premises

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when a syllogism composed of opposite premises leads to the absurd conclusion that a thing is not itself (khalf), and explain why this is logically impossible.

Core Idea
Contradictory premises force the conclusion that a thing is not itself, which is absolute logical impossibility (khalf).

Even if true conclusions can follow from false premises, the author insists that no true conclusion can follow from directly contradictory premises. Such premises inevitably lead to the absurdity that a thing is not itself. This lesson examines that logical law and the ways reasoners inadvertently fall into this error, especially in theological and legal reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the contradictory pair
Look for premises with identical subject and predicate but differing only in quality (affirmative vs negative), e.g., 'Every medicine is knowledge' and 'No medicine is knowledge'.
This is a case of real opposition without synonymy: subject and predicate are the same, but one premise affirms and the other denies.
2
Derive the necessary conclusion
From these premises, the only possible conclusion is 'some medicine is not medicine' or 'some knowledge is not knowledge'. This means a thing is not itself.
The author calls this 'khalf' (absurdity), an impossibility.
3
Recognize the logical law
The author emphasizes that while true conclusions can arise from false premises, they cannot arise from contradictory premises. The result is always absurd.
This is a foundational principle: ex contradictione quodlibet does not apply here because the contradiction is explicit in the premises.
4
Examine error scenarios
Reasoners may combine a true principle with a false syllogism to unknowingly derive a self-contradiction. For example: from 'Some numbers are odd' and a false syllogism yielding 'No number is odd', we get 'Some number is not a number'.
Another scenario: using a composite term as middle term to negate one of its parts, e.g., 'Zayd is a speaking animal' and 'No speaking animal is speaking' leads to 'Zayd is not speaking'.

Da'iq al-Haqa'iq, part two, continuation of the fourth article on defining the forms of two-premise arguments in the first order to produce the opposite of what is sought; for there is no difference in quality, and the opposite of the affirmative is not taken from it as known, but what is taken is only the opposite of the negative from them; so that the conjunction resulting from it and the other premise produces the universal affirmative; for opposition occurs between the two accepted conclusions, since there is no opposition between the two parts. At that point, the second order is not conceivable without the first conjunction from the first. And you yourself consider an example of each of these. Is opposition conceivable between the two premises of a single conjunction in a way that leads to error or not? We say: either it produces an affirmative or a negative. If it produces an affirmative, then no; for the two opposites must be one of them negative, and it is not conceivable to produce an affirmative from a negative. If it produces a negative, then either the opposition is real or imaginary. If it is real, then it is only possible if the names of the three terms are synonymous; two are taken as the two terms of the conclusion and one as the middle term. If it is of the first figure, then the terms are taken such that the predicate in the minor and the subject in the major are one, and the subject in the minor and the predicate in the major are different. That is as when it is said: 'Every lion (layth) is a lion (asad),' and 'Nothing from a ghudanfar is a lion (asad).' If it is of the third, then the terms are taken such that the subject in them is unified and the predicate different, as when it is said: 'Every lion (layth) is a lion (asad),' and 'Nothing from a lion (layth) is a ghudanfar.' If it is of the fourth, then the terms are taken such that the subject in the minor and the predicate in the major are one, and the predicate in the minor and the subject in the major are different, as when it is said: 'Every lion (layth) is a lion (asad),' and 'Nothing from a ghudanfar is a lion (layth).' As for when the opposition is by opinion, not in reality as known, then the predicate in the two premises must be one, and the subject different. That is as when it is said: 'Every human is an animal,' and 'Nothing from the rational is an animal.' According to this, from the opposites in this sense, a syllogism in the first, third, or fourth figure is not prepared; rather it is prepared from the second figure according to the variation of its contexts. As for when the opposition is real and not imagined by taking terms as synonyms, but rather the subject in the two premises is one, and the predicate one, with no difference except in quality, as when we say: 'Every medicine is knowledge,' and 'Nothing from medicine is knowledge.' Even though it follows from it that 'Not every knowledge is knowledge,' the error in it is not hidden. And even though we allow the occurrence of a true conclusion from false premises, we are not required to allow the occurrence of truth from two opposites; for what is necessary from them is that a thing is not itself, which is absurd. As for how it happens that reasoners fall into syllogisms from opposite premises such that it produces that a thing is not itself without awareness, that is because it may happen that a true principle is set before him and false syllogisms produce false results, and from them and the set principle it follows that it is not itself. An example: let the principle be 'Some numbers are odd,' and the false syllogism: 'Every number is divisible by equals,' and 'Nothing divisible by equals is odd' gives 'No number is odd.' Then if you say 'Some number is odd' and 'No number is odd,' it produces by the second figure 'Some number is not a number,' and by the third figure 'Some odd is not odd.' And sometimes the principle is false and the syllogism is true producing its opposite. And sometimes he has two syllogisms either both false or one of them, their conclusions are opposite as previously. And sometimes error occurs by imagining that the composite is not one of its two parts - that is, it is not itself by itself - then the composite is made a middle term between the part negated and that which the composite is affirmed of. That is as when it is said: 'Zayd is a speaking animal,' and 'Nothing that is a speaking animal is speaking.' And that happens in what is between itself, on the basis that it is not itself, only without awareness? It produces that Zayd is not speaking, while he is precisely speaking. And not all of that: what occurs is the conception initially before falling into some of these slips, or imagination through some of these imaginings.

Commentary
This passage is an analytical discussion of syllogisms that involve contradictory or opposite premises (المقدمات المتقابلة). The author begins by referencing the 'first order' of two-premise arguments that aim to produce the opposite of the desired conclusion. He clarifies that only the opposite of the negative premise is taken, not the affirmative, to achieve a universal affirmative conclusion. The core question is whether two premises of a single syllogism can be opposite in a way that leads to error. The answer distinguishes between syllogisms producing affirmative vs. negative conclusions. If the conclusion is affirmative, no error arises because opposites require a negative premise. If the conclusion is negative, opposition can be real (حقيقي) or imaginary (مظنون). Real opposition occurs when the three terms are synonymous; the author illustrates this with examples in the first, third, and fourth figures using synonymous terms for lion (ليث, أسد, غضنفر). Imaginary opposition occurs when the predicate is the same but subjects differ; this only fits the second figure, as shown with the example 'Every human is an animal' and 'Nothing rational is an animal.' Then the author considers a case of real opposition without synonymy: the subject and predicate are identical, differing only in quality (affirmative vs. negative), e.g., 'Every medicine is knowledge' and 'No medicine is knowledge.' This leads to the absurd conclusion that a thing is not itself (الشيء ليس هو هو), which is logically impossible (خلف). The author notes that while true conclusions can follow from false premises, they cannot follow from contradictory premises. The rest of the passage explains how reasoners inadvertently fall into such absurdity. One scenario combines a true principle (e.g., 'Some numbers are odd') with a false syllogism (e.g., 'Every number is divisible by equals' and 'Nothing divisible by equals is odd') to derive 'No number is odd,' then combining 'Some number is odd' with 'No number is odd' yields, via the second figure, 'Some number is not a number' or via the third figure 'Some odd is not odd.' Another scenario involves two false syllogisms or one false and one true that produce opposite conclusions. Finally, the author describes a sophistical trick: mistaking a composite (e.g., 'speaking animal') as not being one of its parts, then using the composite as a middle term to conclude the absurd negation of that part (e.g., from 'Zayd is a speaking animal' and 'Nothing that is a speaking animal is speaking' to 'Zayd is not speaking'). Throughout, the author emphasizes that such errors arise from confused conception or imagination before reasoning.

Key Terms
خلف
النتيجة المحالة
الشيء ليس هو هو

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why can't a true conclusion follow from two opposite premises (e.g., 'Every prayer is worship' and 'No prayer is worship')?
A) Because one premise must be false.
B) Because the necessary conclusion would be that a thing is not itself, which is absurd.
C) Because only false premises can produce a true conclusion.
D) Because the syllogism would have four terms.

Key Takeaway
Contradictory premises (identical subject and predicate, differing only in quality) necessarily produce an absurd conclusion that a thing is not itself, and these premises cannot yield a true conclusion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Consider the following pair: 'Every marriage contract requires a guardian' and 'No marriage contract requires a guardian.' What absurd conclusion follows? Explain why this is a case of contradictory premises.

What is Muqawama? Definition and Two Types
Author defines muqawama and introduces its two types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the definition of muqawama and differentiate between its two types: 'inad and munaqada.

Core Idea
Muqawama is a counter-syllogism that targets the major premise, not just any objection.

This lesson introduces muqawama, a key concept in Islamic logic for refuting an opponent's argument. The author defines it precisely and distinguishes two methods: 'inad (obstinacy) and munaqada (contradiction). Understanding this foundation is crucial for deploying counter-arguments systematically.

Guided Path
1
Identify the defined term
Find the term being defined: muqawama (المقاومة). The author states its definition as a syllogism composed to refute a major premise in another syllogism.
2
Extract genus and differentia
The genus is 'syllogism' (قياس), and the differentia is 'composed to refute a major premise in another syllogism' (مؤلف لإبطال مقدمة كبرى في قياس آخر). This distinguishes muqawama from other syllogisms.
3
Learn the two types
The author divides muqawama into two types: 'inad (by obstinacy) and munaqada (by contradiction). Note the condition for 'inad: the resisted premise must be universal.
'Inad is not just any counterexample; it requires a more general premise with opposite quality.
4
Summarize the definition
Combine the above to state the full definition: muqawama is a syllogism aimed at refuting a major premise, either by taking a more general opposite premise ('inad) or by taking a part of the subject with an opposite ruling (munaqada).

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما المقاومة، فعبارة عن قياس مؤلف لإبطال مقدمة كبرى في قياس آخر، إما على سبيل العناد أو المناقضة، فإن كان على سبيل العناد فلا يتصور إلا أن تكون المقدمة المقصود مقاومتها كلية إما موجبة وإما سالبة ولا تبال من أي شكل كان قياسها، وذلك لأن تؤخذ مقدمة أخرى هي أشد عموماً منها مخالفة لها في الكيف ويضاف إليها، فإن قياسهما ينتج ضد المقدمة الكبرى للقياس الأول، مثال ذلك عند كونها موجبة والقياس الأول من الشكل الأول ما لو كان أن السواد والبياض (ضدان و كل) ضدين فالعلم بهما (واحد فقلت كل ضدين متقابلان) و(لا شيء مما هما متقابلان فالعلم بهما (واحد فإنه ينتج من الاقتران الأول من الشكل الأول (لا) شيء من الأضداد العلم بهما واحد وليس يتهيأ ما مثل هذا القياس المقاوم عند كون المقدمة المقاومة موجبة من الشكل الثاني؛ إذ الحد الأوسط لا يكون مجهولاً في المقدمتين دون عكس السالبة، وهو أن يقال: (لا شيء مما به العلم بمتقابلين لكنه بعيد عن البيان ؛ إذ إذعان الذهن له دون إذعانه للأول، ولا من الثالث؛ لضرورة أن نتيجته لا تكون إلا جزئية، ولا من الشكل الرابع؛ إذ الحد الأوسط كما علم لا بد وأن يكون فيه موضوعاً في الصغرى ومحمولاً في الكبرى، والموضوع في الصغرى هو بعينه الحد الأصغر، فلا يتصور أن يكون أوسط، ولأنه إذا كانت الكبرى فيه سالبة والصغرى موجبة فالنتيجة فيه لا تكون إلا جزئية، ومثال ذلك عند كونها سالبة ما لو كان الحكم في الكبرى من القياس الأول (لا شيء من الأضداد العلم بها (واحد فقلت الأضداد متقابلة) و(كل متقابلين فالعلم بهما واحد ولكن ما مثل هذا أيضاً ليس ينتج من غير الاقتران الأول من الشكل الأول؛ لضرورة أن نتيجته لا تكون إلا كلية موجبة. وأما إن كان على سبيل المناقضة، فالمقدمة المقاومة إما كليّة موجبة أو كليّة سالبة، أو جزئية موجبة أو جزئية سالبة، فإن كانت كليّة موجبة فالمقاومة إنما تتحقق بأخذ جزء من موضوعها الحكم فيه مخالف في الكيفية لحكمها، مثال ذلك ما لو
As for muqawama (resistance), it is a syllogism composed to refute a major premise in another syllogism, either by way of obstinacy ('inad) or contradiction (munaqada). If it is by way of obstinacy, then it is only conceivable that the premise intended to be resisted is universal, either affirmative or negative, regardless of the figure of its syllogism. That is because another premise is taken that is more general than it, differing in quality, and is added to it. Then their syllogism produces the opposite of the major premise of the first syllogism. An example of this when it is affirmative and the first syllogism is of the first figure is if blackness and whiteness are opposites, and every pair of opposites, knowledge of them is one. Then you say: 'Every pair of opposites are correlated' and 'Nothing that is correlated is such that knowledge of them is one', for it produces from the first mood of the first figure: 'No opposites are such that knowledge of them is one.' Such a resistant syllogism is not possible when the resisted premise is affirmative from the second figure, for the middle term cannot be unknown in both premises without conversion of the negative, which would be to say: 'Nothing from which knowledge of them is of correlated pairs' but that is far from clarity, for the mind's assent to it is not like its assent to the first. Nor from the third, because its conclusion is necessarily only particular. Nor from the fourth, because the middle term must be subject in the minor and predicate in the major, and the subject in the minor is itself the minor term, so it cannot be middle. Also, when the major is negative and the minor affirmative, the conclusion is only particular. An example when the resisted premise is negative: if the ruling in the major premise of the first syllogism is 'No opposites are such that knowledge of them is one', then you say 'Opposites are correlated' and 'Every pair of correlated things are such that knowledge of them is one'. But this also does not produce except from the first mood of the first figure, because its conclusion is necessarily only universal affirmative. As for if it is by way of contradiction, the resisted premise is either universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative, or particular negative. If it is universal affirmative, then resistance is realized by taking a part of its subject whose ruling differs in quality from its ruling. An example of that is as if...

Commentary
This passage defines muqawama (resistance) as a syllogism constructed to refute the major premise of another syllogism. The author distinguishes two types: 'inad (obstinacy) and munaqada (contradiction). In 'inad, the resisted premise must be universal (affirmative or negative). The method is to take another premise that is more general and opposite in quality, then combine them to produce the opposite of the original major premise. The author gives an example from the first figure: if the original syllogism had the major premise 'Every pair of opposites are such that knowledge of them is one' (affirmative), the resistant syllogism uses 'Every pair of opposites are correlated' and 'Nothing correlated is such that knowledge of them is one' to conclude 'No opposites are such that knowledge of them is one'. The author then explains why such resistance is not possible in the second, third, or fourth figures for an affirmative premise, citing restrictions about the middle term and conclusion quality. For a negative resisted premise, a similar example is given, again only successful in the first figure's first mood. The author then transitions to the second type, munaqada, where the resisted premise can be universal or particular. For a universal affirmative, resistance is done by taking a part of its subject with an opposite ruling; the example is cut off. The passage is detailed in showing the conditions under which muqawama works, emphasizing the importance of figure and mood.

Key Terms
المقاومة
العناد
المناقضة

Key Takeaway
Muqawama is a targeted counter-syllogism that invalidates the major premise of an opponent's argument.

How 'Inad Works: The First Figure Condition
Author provides an example of 'inad with a universal affirmative premise and explains why only first figure first mood works.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct a valid muqawama by 'inad and explain why it only works in the first figure's first mood for universal premises.

Core Idea
'Inad requires a more general premise opposite in quality, and only the first figure first mood yields the needed universal conclusion.

This lesson dives into the mechanics of 'inad. The author provides a detailed example and then explains why other figures and moods fail. Understanding this sharpens your ability to deploy muqawama correctly.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original syllogism's major premise
The resisted premise is 'Every pair of opposites is such that knowledge of them is one' (affirmative universal). This is the target.
2
Construct the resistant premise
Take a more general premise opposite in quality: 'No correlated things are such that knowledge of them is one' (negative universal). Add a minor premise 'All opposites are correlated' (affirmative universal).
The resistant premise is more general because 'correlated' is broader than 'opposites'.
3
Combine in first figure first mood
The syllogism becomes: No correlated things are such that knowledge of them is one (major), All opposites are correlated (minor). Conclusion: No opposites are such that knowledge of them is one. This is a valid first-figure syllogism (Celarent).
The author calls this 'first mood' of the first figure.
4
Check why other figures fail
For an affirmative resisted premise, the second figure fails because the middle term would be unknown in both premises without conversion. The third figure yields only particular conclusions. The fourth figure cannot have the middle term as subject in minor and predicate in major simultaneously.
These restrictions ensure the resistant syllogism produces a universal conclusion opposite to the original.

Muqawama and the First Type: al-'Inad
Defines muqawama and explains the first type (obstinacy) with examples from first figure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct a valid muqawama of the 'inad type for a universal premise and justify why it only works in the first mood of the first figure.

Core Idea
Muqawama refutes a premise by using a more general premise of opposite quality, but only in the first figure.

In Islamic logic, after constructing a syllogism, one must also know how to refute its premises—this is muqawama. The first type, 'inad, requires a specific technique: take a more general premise opposite in quality, combine it with an appropriate second premise, and produce the opposite conclusion. This lesson unpacks the definition, the method, and the strict figure and mood constraints, using examples from the science of opposites.

Guided Path
1
Identify the targeted premise
The premise to be refuted must be universal (either affirmative or negative). Find it in the original syllogism.
2
Formulate the counter-premise
Take another universal premise that is more general than the targeted premise and opposite in quality (e.g., if targeted is affirmative, counter is negative).
3
Add a linking premise
Add a premise that connects the subject of the counter-premise to the subject of the targeted premise (e.g., 'All opposites are mutually exclusive').
4
Construct the counter-syllogism
Combine the counter-premise and the linking premise in a categorical syllogism. The conclusion will be the opposite of the targeted premise.
This only yields a valid refutation in the first mood of the first figure.
5
Analyze why other figures fail
The passage explains: second figure requires conversion that reduces clarity; third figure gives a particular conclusion (not opposite of universal); fourth figure fails because middle term coincides with minor term.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما المقاومة، فعبارة عن قياس مؤلف لإبطال مقدمة كبرى في قياس آخر، إما على سبيل العناد أو المناقضة، فإن كان على سبيل العناد فلا يتصور إلا أن تكون المقدمة المقصود مقاومتها كلية إما موجبة وإما سالبة ولا تبال من أي شكل كان قياسها، وذلك لأن تؤخذ مقدمة أخرى هي أشد عموماً منها مخالفة لها في الكيف ويضاف إليها، فإن قياسهما ينتج ضد المقدمة الكبرى للقياس الأول، مثال ذلك عند كونها موجبة والقياس الأول من الشكل الأول ما لو كان أن السواد والبياض (ضدان و كل) ضدين فالعلم بهما (واحد فقلت كل ضدين متقابلان) و(لا شيء مما هما متقابلان فالعلم بهما (واحد فإنه ينتج من الاقتران الأول من الشكل الأول (لا) شيء من الأضداد العلم بهما واحد وليس يتهيأ ما مثل هذا القياس المقاوم عند كون المقدمة المقاومة موجبة من الشكل الثاني؛ إذ الحد الأوسط لا يكون مجهولاً في المقدمتين دون عكس السالبة، وهو أن يقال: (لا شيء مما به العلم بمتقابلين لكنه بعيد عن البيان ؛ إذ إذعان الذهن له دون إذعانه للأول، ولا من الثالث؛ لضرورة أن نتيجته لا تكون إلا جزئية، ولا من الشكل الرابع؛ إذ الحد الأوسط كما علم لا بد وأن يكون فيه موضوعاً في الصغرى ومحمولاً في الكبرى، والموضوع في الصغرى هو بعينه الحد الأصغر، فلا يتصور أن يكون أوسط، ولأنه إذا كانت الكبرى فيه سالبة والصغرى موجبة فالنتيجة فيه لا تكون إلا جزئية، ومثال ذلك عند كونها سالبة ما لو كان الحكم في الكبرى من القياس الأول (لا شيء من الأضداد العلم بها (واحد فقلت الأضداد متقابلة) و(كل متقابلين فالعلم بهما واحد ولكن ما مثل هذا أيضاً ليس ينتج من غير الاقتران الأول من الشكل الأول؛ لضرورة أن نتيجته لا تكون إلا كلية موجبة.
As for al-muqawama (counter-argument), it is a composed syllogism to refute a major premise in another syllogism, either by way of obstinacy or by way of contradiction. If it is by way of obstinacy, then it is only conceivable that the premise intended to be resisted is universal, either affirmative or negative, regardless of which figure its syllogism is from. That is because another premise is taken which is more general than it, differing from it in quality, and is added to it; then their syllogism produces the opposite of the major premise of the first syllogism. An example of that when it is affirmative and the first syllogism is from the first figure: if it were that blackness and whiteness [are two opposites] and every two opposites, knowledge of them is one, so you say 'Every two opposites are mutually exclusive' and 'Nothing which is mutually exclusive has knowledge of them as one' – then it produces from the first mood of the first figure 'No opposition has knowledge of them as one'. And such a counter-syllogism is not possible when the resisted premise is affirmative from the second figure, because the middle term would not be unknown in both premises without conversion of the negative; that is by saying 'Nothing which has knowledge of it is mutually exclusive' but that is far from clarity because the mind's assent to it is less than its assent to the first. Nor from the third, because necessarily its conclusion would be only particular. Nor from the fourth, because the middle term, as is known, must be subject in the minor and predicate in the major, and the subject in the minor is exactly the minor term, so it cannot be middle; and because if the major is negative and the minor affirmative, the conclusion cannot but be particular. And an example of that when it is negative: if the judgment in the major of the first syllogism were 'Nothing of the opposites has knowledge of them as one', then you say 'Opposites are mutually exclusive' and 'Every mutually exclusive pair has knowledge of them as one' – but such an example also does not produce except from the first mood of the first figure; because necessarily its conclusion would be only universal affirmative.
Source pages p. 463

Commentary
This passage defines muqawama as a syllogism composed to refute a major premise of another syllogism, and distinguishes two types: 'inad (obstinacy) and munaqada (contradiction). For the first type, the premise to be refuted must be universal (affirmative or negative). The method is to take a premise more general than the original and opposite in quality, then combine them to produce the opposite conclusion. The author provides an example with opposites and knowledge, showing that the counter-syllogism only works in the first mood of the first figure. The author then systematically explains why other figures fail: in the second, the middle term would not be unknown; in the third, the conclusion would be particular; in the fourth, the middle term would coincide with the minor term. The same applies for a negative premise. This passage is a precise technical analysis of the constraints on constructing a muqawama of the 'inad type.

Key Terms
المقاومة
العناد
الشكل الأول
الاقتران الأول

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Because the conclusion would be particular.
B) Because the middle term would not be unknown in both premises without converting the negative.
C) Because the minor premise must be particular.
D) Because the fourth mood is not valid.

Muqawama of the 'inad type requires the counter-premise to be more general and opposite in quality, and only yields a valid refutation in the first mood of the first figure.

Muqawama by Contradiction for Universal Affirmative Premises
Explains muqawama for universal affirmative premise

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct a muqawama by contradiction against a universal affirmative premise using the correct syllogistic figure and mood.

Core Idea
Contradicting a universal affirmative requires taking a part of its subject with opposite quality.

In a dialectical debate, when an opponent asserts a universal affirmative claim, you can resist it by producing a counter-syllogism that derives a contradictory particular conclusion. This lesson shows you the exact method for universal affirmative premises and why only certain figures work.

Guided Path
1
Identify the universal affirmative premise
The premise to be resisted is of the form 'Every S is P'. Recognize its quantity and quality.
2
Take a part of the subject with opposite quality
Select a subset M of S such that 'Some S is M' (particular affirmative) and 'No M is P' (universal negative). This sets up the counter-argument.
The opposite quality refers to the judgment's polarity: here the counter-premise 'No M is P' is negative, opposite to the affirmative original.
3
Construct the counter-syllogism
Combine the two premises: major 'No M is P' (E) and minor 'Some S is M' (I). From these, the conclusion in the first figure fourth mood (Ferio) is 'Some S is not P' (O), which contradicts the original 'Every S is P' (A).
The fourth mood of the first figure is also known as Ferio (EIO).
4
Verify why other figures fail
The author explains that the second figure would require conversion of the negative premise, and the third figure would require conversion of both premises, making the argument less direct or invalid without those conversions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما إن كان على سبيل المناقضة، فالمقدمة المقاومة إما كليّة موجبة أو كليّة سالبة، أو جزئية موجبة أو جزئية سالبة، فإن كانت كليّة موجبة فالمقاومة إنما تتحقق بأخذ جزء من موضوعها الحكم فيه مخالف في الكيفية لحكمها، مثال ذلك ما لو كان الحكم في القياس السابق على المتقابلات فقيل وكل متقابلين فالعلم بهما (واحد) فقيل (بعض المتقابلات أضداد) و (لا شيء من الأضداد العلم بها واحد) فإنه ينتج من الاقتران الرابع من الأول بعض المتقابلات العلم بها واحد وليس يتهيأ هذا المقام من الثاني دون عكس السالبة، ومن الثالث دون عكس الموجبة والسالبة معاً، وإن كانت كلية سالبة فطريق المقاومة أن نفكك كما علمناك في الموجبة فنقول (بعض المتقابلات أضداد) و(كل) ضدين فالعلم بهما واحد فإنه ينتج من ثالث الأول (بعض المتقابلات العلم بها واحد وقد كان لا شيء من المتقابلات العلم بها واحد) وما مثل هذا المقاوم ليس يتهيأ من الشكل الثاني؛ إذ النتيجة فيه موجبة، ولا من الرابع؛ إذ لا يتم منه إلا بعكس الصغرى والكبرى، وإذ ذاك فيعود إلى جزئيتين، بل إن يتهيأ ذلك فليس إلا من الشكل الثالث بعكس الصغرى، كما عرف أما إن كانت جزئية موجبة أو سالبة، فليس يتحقق المقاومة؛ إذ القياس المقاوم لا بُدّ أن تكون صغراه جزئية، وإذ ذاك فنتيجته جزئية، ولا مقاومة بين جزئيتين. واعلم أنه مهما كانت المقدمة المأخوذة للمقاومة غير أوّليّة فلا تساعد، إلا أن تكون محمودة. وعلى الجملة مهما أخذت نتيجة القياس التالي وقُرنت بصغرى الأول فإنه ينتج مقابل النتيجة الأولى. اللهم إلا أن تكون نتيجة القياس الثاني وصغرى الأول جزئية، أو على نمط غير منتج، وأما المثال فحاصله راجع إلى القضاء برد معلوم إلى معلوم في حكم، بناء على جامع بينهما، ولا تبال كان المردود والمردود إليه كليتين أو جزئيتين، أو أن إحداهما كلي والآخر جزئي أو كان ما فيه الرد من الحكم نفياً أو إثباتاً، أو كان الجامع سالباً أو إيجاباً، وهو كقولنا: العالم) موجود فكان قديماً كالباري) وقد جرت عادة المتكلمين به أن يُسمّوا المردود وهو ما مثل العالم هاهنا فرعاً، والمردود إليه وهو ما مثل الباري أصلاً، وما فيه الرد كالقديم حكماً، وما فيه الاشتراك كالموجود علة، ويخالف الاستقراء من جهة أن الحكم فيه قد يكون على أحد جزئي كلي بما...
As for if it is by way of contradiction, the resisted premise is either universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative, or particular negative. If it is universal affirmative, then the counter-argument is realized by taking a part of its subject whose judgment is opposite in quality to its judgment. An example of that is if the judgment in the previous syllogism about mutually exclusive things was 'Every mutually exclusive pair has knowledge of them as one', then it is said 'Some mutually exclusive things are opposites' and 'Nothing of the opposites has knowledge as one' – then it produces from the fourth mood of the first figure 'Some mutually exclusive things have knowledge as one'. And this position is not possible from the second figure without conversion of the negative, and from the third without conversion of both affirmative and negative together. And if it is universal negative, the method of counter-argument is to break [it down] as we taught you for the affirmative: we say 'Some mutually exclusive things are opposites' and 'Every pair of opposites has knowledge as one' – then it produces from the third mood of the first figure 'Some mutually exclusive things have knowledge as one', whereas before it was 'Nothing of the mutually exclusive things has knowledge as one'. And such a counter-argument is not possible from the second figure, because the conclusion there is affirmative; nor from the fourth, because it is only completed by conversion of the minor and major, and then it would revert to two particulars. Rather, if it is possible, it is only from the third figure with conversion of the minor, as is known. But if it is particular affirmative or particular negative, then the counter-argument is not realized; because the counter-syllogism must have a particular minor, and then its conclusion is particular, and there is no counter-argument between two particulars. And know that whenever the premise taken for the counter-argument is not primary (axiomatic), it does not help, unless it is commended. In summary, whenever the conclusion of the second syllogism is taken and paired with the minor premise of the first, it produces the opposite of the first conclusion. Except if the conclusion of the second syllogism and the minor of the first are particular, or in a non-productive pattern. As for the example, its summary returns to the judgment of referring a known thing to a known thing in a ruling, based on a commonality between them, regardless of whether the referred and the referred-to are universal or particular, or one is universal and the other particular, or the ruling in the referral is negative or affirmative, or the commonality is negative or affirmative. It is like our saying: 'The world is existent, so it is eternal like the Creator.' Theologians customarily call the referred (like the world here) a branch, the referred-to (like the Creator) a root, the ruling (like eternity) a judgment, and the commonality (like existence) a cause. And it differs from induction in that the judgment in it may be on one particular of a universal by...
Source pages p. 463

Commentary
This passage covers the second type of muqawama (by contradiction). It categorizes the resisted premise into four types. For universal affirmative, the counter-argument uses a particular premise with opposite quality, producing a particular conclusion from the fourth mood of the first figure. For universal negative, a similar breakdown uses the third mood. The author explains why other figures fail. For particular premises, muqawama is impossible because both the counter-syllogism's minor and conclusion are particular, and two particulars cannot contradict each other. The passage then adds general rules: the counter-premise must be axiomatic or commended; pairing the second syllogism's conclusion with the first's minor yields the opposite of the first conclusion unless particulars are involved. Finally, the author gives an example of analogical reasoning (qiyas 'adiy) used by theologians, comparing muqawama to the method of referring a branch to a root via a common attribute ('illa), and noting its difference from induction. This last part introduces a different logical tool, but the overall passage is focused on muqawama.

كليّة موجبة
الاقتران الرابع من الأول

Muqawama by Contradiction for Universal Negative and Particular Premises
Explains muqawama for universal negative and particular premises

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct a muqawama by contradiction against a universal negative premise and explain why particular premises cannot be resisted.

Core Idea
A universal negative is contradicted by a universal affirmative major and a particular affirmative minor; particular premises cannot be resisted because both sides yield particulars.

After learning to resist universal affirmatives, we now turn to universal negatives. The method mirrors the previous one but uses a different mood. Additionally, the author explains a key limitation: if the premise is particular, muqawama by contradiction is impossible because the counter-syllogism would also produce a particular conclusion, and two particulars cannot contradict each other.

Guided Path
1
Identify the universal negative premise
The premise to be resisted is 'No S is P' (E).
2
Take a part of the subject with opposite quality
Select a subset M of S such that 'Some S is M' (I) and 'Every M is P' (A). This sets up the counter-argument.
Opposite quality: the original is negative, so the counter-premise 'Every M is P' is affirmative.
3
Construct the counter-syllogism
Combine the two premises: major 'Every M is P' (A) and minor 'Some S is M' (I). In the first figure third mood (Datisi), the conclusion is 'Some S is P' (I), which contradicts 'No S is P' (E).
The third mood of the first figure is also known as Datisi (AII).
4
Understand why particular premises cannot be resisted
If the original premise is particular (affirmative or negative), a counter-syllogism must have a particular minor, leading to a particular conclusion. Two particular propositions (I and O) are not direct contradictories; they can both be true together, so no genuine refutation occurs.
The passage states: 'ولا مقاومة بين جزئيتين'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
