General Rules of Muqawama and the Theological Analogy
General rules and theological analogy

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the general conditions for muqawama and identify the analogical reasoning used in theological debates.

Core Idea
Muqawama is valid only when the counter-premise is primary or commended; theological analogies share structural elements with muqawama.

Beyond the specific methods for each premise type, the author lays down general rules: the premise used in muqawama must be axiomatic (awwali) or at least commended (mahmuda). In a final note, he draws an analogy between muqawama and the method of the theologians, who refer a branch case to a root case via a common cause. This connects logical refutation to juridical and theological reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the condition for the counter-premise
The passage states that the premise taken for muqawama must be 'awwali' (self-evident) or at least 'mahmuda' (commended by consensus or authority). If it is neither, the muqawama fails.
This ensures that the counter-argument is based on strong epistemic grounds.
2
Apply the rule for pairing conclusions
If you take the conclusion of a second syllogism and pair it with the minor premise of the first syllogism, you get the opposite of the first conclusion. This is a general strategy, except when both are particular or the pattern is non-productive.
3
Recognize the theological analogy
The author gives an example: 'The world is existent, so it is eternal like the Creator.' This is a typical analogy (tamthil) used in kalam. The referred item (world) is called far' (branch), the referred-to (Creator) is asl (root), the judgment (eternity) is hukm, and the commonality (existence) is 'illa (cause).
This differs from induction: in induction, the judgment may apply to a particular of a universal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما إن كان على سبيل المناقضة، فالمقدمة المقاومة إما كليّة موجبة أو كليّة سالبة، أو جزئية موجبة أو جزئية سالبة، فإن كانت كليّة موجبة فالمقاومة إنما تتحقق بأخذ جزء من موضوعها الحكم فيه مخالف في الكيفية لحكمها، مثال ذلك ما لو كان الحكم في القياس السابق على المتقابلات فقيل وكل متقابلين فالعلم بهما (واحد) فقيل (بعض المتقابلات أضداد) و (لا شيء من الأضداد العلم بها واحد) فإنه ينتج من الاقتران الرابع من الأول بعض المتقابلات العلم بها واحد وليس يتهيأ هذا المقام من الثاني دون عكس السالبة، ومن الثالث دون عكس الموجبة والسالبة معاً، وإن كانت كلية سالبة فطريق المقاومة أن نفكك كما علمناك في الموجبة فنقول (بعض المتقابلات أضداد) و(كل) ضدين فالعلم بهما واحد فإنه ينتج من ثالث الأول (بعض المتقابلات العلم بها واحد وقد كان لا شيء من المتقابلات العلم بها واحد) وما مثل هذا المقاوم ليس يتهيأ من الشكل الثاني؛ إذ النتيجة فيه موجبة، ولا من الرابع؛ إذ لا يتم منه إلا بعكس الصغرى والكبرى، وإذ ذاك فيعود إلى جزئيتين، بل إن يتهيأ ذلك فليس إلا من الشكل الثالث بعكس الصغرى، كما عرف أما إن كانت جزئية موجبة أو سالبة، فليس يتحقق المقاومة؛ إذ القياس المقاوم لا بُدّ أن تكون صغراه جزئية، وإذ ذاك فنتيجته جزئية، ولا مقاومة بين جزئيتين. واعلم أنه مهما كانت المقدمة المأخوذة للمقاومة غير أوّليّة فلا تساعد، إلا أن تكون محمودة. وعلى الجملة مهما أخذت نتيجة القياس التالي وقُرنت بصغرى الأول فإنه ينتج مقابل النتيجة الأولى. اللهم إلا أن تكون نتيجة القياس الثاني وصغرى الأول جزئية، أو على نمط غير منتج، وأما المثال فحاصله راجع إلى القضاء برد معلوم إلى معلوم في حكم، بناء على جامع بينهما، ولا تبال كان المردود والمردود إليه كليتين أو جزئيتين، أو أن إحداهما كلي والآخر جزئي أو كان ما فيه الرد من الحكم نفياً أو إثباتاً، أو كان الجامع سالباً أو إيجاباً، وهو كقولنا: العالم) موجود فكان قديماً كالباري) وقد جرت عادة المتكلمين به أن يُسمّوا المردود وهو ما مثل العالم هاهنا فرعاً، والمردود إليه وهو ما مثل الباري أصلاً، وما فيه الرد كالقديم حكماً، وما فيه الاشتراك كالموجود علة، ويخالف الاستقراء من جهة أن الحكم فيه قد يكون على أحد جزئي كلي بما...
As for if it is by way of contradiction, the resisted premise is either universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative, or particular negative. If it is universal affirmative, then the counter-argument is realized by taking a part of its subject whose judgment is opposite in quality to its judgment. An example of that is if the judgment in the previous syllogism about mutually exclusive things was 'Every mutually exclusive pair has knowledge of them as one', then it is said 'Some mutually exclusive things are opposites' and 'Nothing of the opposites has knowledge as one' – then it produces from the fourth mood of the first figure 'Some mutually exclusive things have knowledge as one'. And this position is not possible from the second figure without conversion of the negative, and from the third without conversion of both affirmative and negative together. And if it is universal negative, the method of counter-argument is to break [it down] as we taught you for the affirmative: we say 'Some mutually exclusive things are opposites' and 'Every pair of opposites has knowledge as one' – then it produces from the third mood of the first figure 'Some mutually exclusive things have knowledge as one', whereas before it was 'Nothing of the mutually exclusive things has knowledge as one'. And such a counter-argument is not possible from the second figure, because the conclusion there is affirmative; nor from the fourth, because it is only completed by conversion of the minor and major, and then it would revert to two particulars. Rather, if it is possible, it is only from the third figure with conversion of the minor, as is known. But if it is particular affirmative or particular negative, then the counter-argument is not realized; because the counter-syllogism must have a particular minor, and then its conclusion is particular, and there is no counter-argument between two particulars. And know that whenever the premise taken for the counter-argument is not primary (axiomatic), it does not help, unless it is commended. In summary, whenever the conclusion of the second syllogism is taken and paired with the minor premise of the first, it produces the opposite of the first conclusion. Except if the conclusion of the second syllogism and the minor of the first are particular, or in a non-productive pattern. As for the example, its summary returns to the judgment of referring a known thing to a known thing in a ruling, based on a commonality between them, regardless of whether the referred and the referred-to are universal or particular, or one is universal and the other particular, or the ruling in the referral is negative or affirmative, or the commonality is negative or affirmative. It is like our saying: 'The world is existent, so it is eternal like the Creator.' Theologians customarily call the referred (like the world here) a branch, the referred-to (like the Creator) a root, the ruling (like eternity) a judgment, and the commonality (like existence) a cause. And it differs from induction in that the judgment in it may be on one particular of a universal by...
Source pages p. 463

Commentary
This passage covers the second type of muqawama (by contradiction). It categorizes the resisted premise into four types. For universal affirmative, the counter-argument uses a particular premise with opposite quality, producing a particular conclusion from the fourth mood of the first figure. For universal negative, a similar breakdown uses the third mood. The author explains why other figures fail. For particular premises, muqawama is impossible because both the counter-syllogism's minor and conclusion are particular, and two particulars cannot contradict each other. The passage then adds general rules: the counter-premise must be axiomatic or commended; pairing the second syllogism's conclusion with the first's minor yields the opposite of the first conclusion unless particulars are involved. Finally, the author gives an example of analogical reasoning (qiyas 'adiy) used by theologians, comparing muqawama to the method of referring a branch to a root via a common attribute ('illa), and noting its difference from induction. This last part introduces a different logical tool, but the overall passage is focused on muqawama.

Muqawama requires the counter-premise to be axiomatic or commended, and its structure parallels theological analogies that refer a branch to a root via a common cause.

Constructing a Counter-Syllogism to Resist a Universal Negative
Constructing a counter-syllogism in the first figure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct a valid first-figure counter-syllogism (muqāwama) that yields the contradictory of a universal negative statement.

Core Idea
To resist a universal negative, use a particular affirmative minor and a universal affirmative major in Darii.

In logical debate, when faced with a universal negative conclusion, you can construct a counter-syllogism (muqāwama) that proves its contradictory. The author demonstrates this using the example of opposites. By analyzing the structure, you'll learn a systematic method to produce a valid counter-argument.

Guided Path
1
Identify the target universal negative
Locate the conclusion you want to resist. In the passage, it's 'No opposites have knowledge as one' (لا شيء من المتقابلات العلم بها واحد).
This is a universal negative (E) proposition.
2
Determine the contradictory conclusion
The contradictory of a universal negative is a particular affirmative: 'Some opposites have knowledge as one' (بعض المتقابلات العلم بها واحد). That is your target conclusion.
Contradictories: E vs I.
3
Select premises that entail the target conclusion
You need a middle term that links the subject and predicate. The author uses 'contraries' (أضداد) as middle. Premises: 'Some opposites are contraries' (particular affirmative) and 'Every pair of contraries has a single knowledge' (universal affirmative).
The minor premise must be particular affirmative to yield a particular conclusion.
4
Check the syllogistic figure and validity
This is first figure mood Darii (AII): major universal affirmative, minor particular affirmative, conclusion particular affirmative. Valid. The middle term is the predicate of the minor and subject of the major.
First figure requires the middle to be subject of major and predicate of minor.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The seventh art: Investigating what remains of the types of proofs. The ruling in the previous syllogism was about opposites, and it was said: 'Every pair of opposites, knowledge of them is one,' so it was said: 'Some opposites are contraries,' and 'No contraries have knowledge of them as one.' For it yields from the fourth combination of the first figure: 'Some opposites have knowledge of them as one.' And this position is not possible from the second figure without converting the negative, nor from the third without converting both the affirmative and the negative together. And if it is a universal negative, the method of resistance is that we analyze as we taught you in the affirmative, so we say: 'Some opposites are contraries,' and 'Every pair of contraries, knowledge of them is one,' for it yields from the third of the first: 'Some opposites have knowledge of them as one,' whereas it was: 'No opposites have knowledge of them as one.' And a resistance like this is not possible from the second figure, because the conclusion in it is affirmative, nor from the fourth, because it is only completed by converting the minor and major, and then it reverts to two particulars. Rather, if that is possible, it is only from the third figure by converting the minor, as known. But if it is particular affirmative or negative, resistance is not realized, because the resisting syllogism must have its minor premise particular, and then its conclusion is particular, and there is no resistance between two particulars. Know that whenever the premise taken for resistance is not self-evident, it does not help unless it is praiseworthy. In general, whenever the conclusion of the following syllogism is taken and paired with the minor of the first, it produces the opposite of the first conclusion. Except if the conclusion of the second syllogism and the minor of the first are particular, or in a non-productive pattern.
Source pages p. 464

Commentary
This passage discusses how to construct a counter-syllogism (muqawama) when a previous syllogism used opposite premises. The author begins by stating the topic: the seventh art concerning remaining types of proofs. He refers to a previous syllogism about opposites (mutāqābilāt). The claim was that knowledge of any two opposites is one, but it was specified that some opposites are contraries (aḍdād), and no contraries have knowledge of them as one. The author shows that from the fourth combination of the first figure (i.e., with premises that are particular affirmative and universal negative?) the conclusion 'some opposites have knowledge of them as one' is produced. He then explains that the same result cannot be obtained from the second figure without converting the negative premise, nor from the third without converting both. If the premise is a universal negative, the method of resistance is to analyze it like the affirmative case: we say 'some opposites are contraries' and 'every pair of contraries has knowledge of them as one', which yields a conclusion in the third figure. This resistance is not possible in the second figure because its conclusion is always affirmative, nor in the fourth because it requires conversion of both premises, reducing to two particulars. The only feasible figure for such resistance is the third, after converting the minor premise. If the premise is particular (affirmative or negative), resistance is impossible because the resisting syllogism must have a particular minor, leading to a particular conclusion, and there is no genuine opposition between two particulars. The author adds that the premise used for resistance must be self-evident (awwalī) or at least praiseworthy (maḥmūda). Finally, he summarizes: whenever you take the conclusion of a second syllogism and combine it with the minor premise of the first, it produces the contradictory of the first conclusion, unless both are particular or the pattern is non-productive. This passage is a detailed technical discussion of syllogistic figures and their behavior with opposites.

المقاومة
الأضداد
الاقتران

To resist a universal negative, construct a first-figure Darii syllogism with a particular affirmative minor and a universal affirmative major to yield the contradictory particular affirmative.

Conditions for Successful Resistance and the General Rule
Conditions for resistance and general rule.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when resistance is impossible and apply the general rule for constructing a counter-syllogism.

Core Idea
Resistance fails when the target is particular; always pair a second conclusion with the first's minor.

The author concludes with crucial conditions: resistance is impossible when dealing with particular propositions, and the premise used must be self-evident. He then gives a powerful general rule: combining the conclusion of a second syllogism with the minor of the first yields the opposite of the first conclusion. Let's unpack these conditions.

Guided Path
1
Determine if the target proposition is particular
If the statement you want to resist is particular (affirmative or negative), resistance is impossible. The passage says: 'أما إن كانت جزئية موجبة أو سالبة، فليس يتحقق المقاومة'.
Resistance aims to produce a universal opposite, but a particular conclusion cannot contradict another particular.
2
Check the epistemic status of the resisting premise
The premise used for resistance must be self-evident (أوّليّة) or at least praiseworthy (محمودة). Not any premise will do.
Self-evident means immediately known to be true; praiseworthy means widely accepted.
3
Apply the general rule for constructing a counter-syllogism
The general rule: Take the conclusion of a second syllogism and combine it with the minor premise of the first syllogism. This will produce the opposite of the first conclusion.
This assumes the second syllogism's conclusion and the first's minor are universal; otherwise, it may fail.
4
Identify the exception to the general rule
The rule fails if the conclusion of the second syllogism and the minor of the first are both particular, or if the pattern is non-productive.
The passage says: 'اللهم إلا أن تكون نتيجة القياس الثاني وصغرى الأول جزئية، أو على نمط غير منتج.'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The Dalil as a Probabilistic Syllogism
Defines 'dalil' as a syllogism with an acceptable major premise used in dialectic and rhetoric.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a dalil argument and distinguish it from demonstrative proof.

Core Idea
A dalil is a syllogism with acceptable premises; it persuades but does not prove.

In Islamic logic, not every argument aims at certainty. Some are designed to produce opinion (ẓann) for persuasive purposes in dialectic and rhetoric. This lesson defines 'dalil' (also called 'al-ra'y'), a two-premise argument whose major premise is an acceptable proposition that listeners naturally accept. Understanding this distinction is crucial for evaluating the strength of arguments in different Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum
The passage defines 'al-dalil' (also called 'al-ra'y'). Note that the term 'dalil' can have broader meanings in other contexts, but here it is specifically restricted.
2
Extract the genus
The dalil is 'composed of two premises' – it is a type of syllogism (qiyas). This places it within the broader category of arguments that combine premises.
3
Identify the differentia
The major premise must be an acceptable proposition (qadiyya mahmuda) – one that minds do not reject instinctively and listeners lean toward. This distinguishes the dalil from demonstrative syllogisms whose premises are certain.
4
Understand the epistemic status
The author explicitly states that the dalil 'has no entry into certainty' and yields only opinion (ẓann). It is a probabilistic argument, not a demonstration.
This is critical: in Islamic logic, certainty is reserved for demonstrations (burhan).
5
Examine the example
The example given is 'Every envious person is despised, and every benefactor is loved.' These are acceptable propositions (people generally agree with them) and could serve as major premises in dialectical arguments.
The example illustrates the type of premise used, not a complete syllogism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الدليل ويسمى أيضاً بـ (الرأي) فعبارة عما أُلِف من مقدمتين كبراهما قضية محمودة، يميل إليها السامعون، ولا تردها الأذهان ببديهتها، بهـا يـروج ما يراد ترويجه في الجدليات والخطابيات، كقولهم كل حاسد (ممقوت وكل منعم (محبوب) لأن الدليل [ليس هو نفس الحسد والمحبة أو الحسد والأنعام، بل الدليل هو نفس القول، من جهة أنه متبع مقبول، ولا مدخل له في اليقين أيضاً، فإن جملة هو من جنس هذه المقدمات إنما هو بحسب الظن، والمظنون لا يفيد غير الظن.
As for the 'dalil', also called 'al-ra'y' (opinion), it is an expression of what is composed of two premises, the major of which is an acceptable proposition, towards which the listeners lean, and which minds do not reject by their intuition. By it, what is intended to be promoted is promoted in dialectical and rhetorical discussions, like their saying: 'Every envious person is despised, and every benefactor is loved.' Because the dalil is not itself envy and love, or envy and benevolence; rather the dalil is the statement itself, in that it is followed and accepted. And it has no entry into certainty either, for the whole of it is of the type of these premises only according to opinion, and the opinable yields nothing but opinion.
Source pages p. 467

Commentary
This passage defines 'al-dalil' (also called 'al-ra'y' or opinion) as a type of argument composed of two premises: a major premise that is an acceptable proposition (qadiyya mahmuda) – one that people naturally incline to and do not reject instinctively. This type of argument is used in dialectical (jadalīyāt) and rhetorical (khiṭābīyāt) contexts to promote a particular view. The author gives an example: 'Every envious person is despised, and every benefactor is loved.' The author clarifies that the dalil is not the content itself (envy or love) but the statement (qawl) in its capacity as being followed and accepted. Importantly, the author states that the dalil does not produce certainty (yaqīn); it only yields opinion (ẓann). Thus, it is a probabilistic argument suitable for persuasion, not demonstration.

Key Terms
الدليل
الرأي
قضية محمودة
الجدليات والخطابيات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

C) It is a type of immediate inference.
D) It is only used in poetic discourse.

Key Takeaway
A dalil is a syllogism with an acceptable major premise that yields only opinion, not certainty, and is used in dialectical and rhetorical contexts.

Definition of Damir (Enthymeme)
Defines damir as a syllogism with the major premise suppressed.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a damir (enthymeme) and explain why its major premise is suppressed.

Core Idea
An enthymeme hides a false premise to avoid detection.

In Islamic scholarly debates, arguments often rely on unstated premises. The logicians classified a rhetorical syllogism called 'damir' where the major premise is deliberately omitted – precisely because stating it would reveal its falsehood. Understanding this device helps you detect weak reasoning in theological and juristic arguments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term and its translation
Note that 'damir' is related to the Arabic word for conscience or innermost thought, but here it means 'enthymeme' – a syllogism with a hidden premise.
The term emphasizes concealment.
2
Locate the suppressed premise
The passage states that the major premise (المقدمة الكبرى) is the one that is folded away (طويت).
Not the minor premise.
3
Understand the motive for suppression
The motive is fear (مخافة) of discovering its falsity if stated explicitly.
The arguer knows the premise is false or weak.
4
Compare with a full categorical syllogism
Reconstruct the full syllogism: if the major premise were stated, the audience could reject it. The damir conceals that weak link.
Example from prior text: likely a theological argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الضمير، فعبارة عما طويت فيه المقدمة الكبرى؛ مخافة الاطلاع على كذبها عند التصريح بها، كما سبق مثاله.
As for the 'damir', it is an expression of that in which the major premise is folded, out of fear of discovering its falsity when it is stated explicitly, as its example preceded.
Source pages p. 467

Commentary
This passage defines 'al-damir' (enthymeme) as a syllogism where the major premise is suppressed (طويت, literally 'folded') to avoid revealing its falsehood if stated explicitly. The author mentions that an example has already been given previously. This type of argument is used when the major premise is known to be false or weak, and the arguer wishes to avoid direct scrutiny of it.

Key Terms
الضمير

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) Because it is too complicated for the audience.
C) Out of fear of discovering its falsity if stated explicitly.
D) Because the minor premise is sufficient.

Key Takeaway
The damir is a rhetorical device where the major premise is omitted to hide its weakness.

The Syllogism

The 'Alama as a Suppressed Syllogism
Defines ‘alama as a suppressed syllogism

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two components that are suppressed in a ‘alama (sign) argument and reconstruct the underlying syllogism.

Core Idea
A ‘alama is a syllogism with its major premise and middle term hidden.

In this lesson, we examine the definition of ‘alama (sign) as presented in the text. The ‘alama is not a simple indicator; it is a truncated syllogism. Understanding which parts are missing is key to evaluating its strength.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition of ‘alama
Read the opening sentence: ‘As for the ‘alama, it is an expression of that in which the major premise and the middle term are folded.’ The term ‘folded’ (tuwaya) means they are suppressed, not explicitly stated.
2
Distinguish the suppressed elements
Note that two components are always missing: the major premise (al-muqaddima al-kubra) and the middle term (al-ḥadd al-awsaṭ). The minor premise and conclusion are usually present.
3
Reconstruct an example
Take the example ‘This wood is burnt, so fire has ignited in it.’ The missing major premise is something like ‘Everything burnt has fire ignited.’ The middle term is ‘burnt’ which connects the wood to fire.
4
Determine the type of sign
The sign can be necessary (ḍarūriyya) or acceptable (maḥmūda). Check if the conclusion follows necessarily from the premise.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما العلامة، فعبارة عما طويت فيه المقدمة الكبرى والحد الأوسط، فيه علامة إما ضرورية أو محمودة متبعة، لكن منه ما لوضوح فيه بالمقدمة الكبرى كأن هيئة الحد الأوسط فيه على نسق الشكل الأول، كقولنا: (هذا الخشب محترق فقد اشتعلت فيه النار وقد يخص ما مثل هذا النوع كثيراً بالدليل، ومنه ما لو صرح فيه بالمقدمة الكبرى كان الحد الأوسط فيه أعمّ من الطرفين ومحمولاً عليها بالإيجاب كقولنا: (هذه المرأة مصفار فهي حبلى ومنه ما لو صرح فيه بالمقدمة الكبرى كان الحد الأوسط فيه موضوعاً للطرفين وهو جزئي، كقولنا: (الحجاج كان شجاعاً فالشجعان (ظلمة لكن ما من مثل الشكل الأول ومستمر غير منتقض وما من الشكل الثاني والثالث فغير مستمر، فإنه ليس إذا كان واحد من الظلمة شجاعاً فالظلمة كلهم شجعان ولا أن يكون كل مصفار حبلى، وليس العلامة كما ظن ما يدل بها على الوجود فقط، حتى لا تكون المقدمات ولا في أي شكل كان إلا موجبة، بل العلامة كما تكون للوجود قد تكون للعدم وربما كانت العدم عدم
As for the 'alama' (sign), it is an expression of that in which the major premise and the middle term are folded. In it is a sign, either necessary or acceptable and followed. But there is from it that which due to clarity, the major premise [is omitted] and the configuration of the middle term is according to the first figure, like our saying: 'This wood is burnt, then fire has ignited in it.' And this type is often particularly called 'dalil'. And from it there is that which if the major premise were stated explicitly, the middle term would be more general than the two extremes and predicated on them positively, like our saying: 'This woman is yellowish, then she is pregnant.' And from it there is that which if the major premise were stated explicitly, the middle term would be subject for the two extremes and it is particular, like our saying: 'Al-Hajjaj was brave, then brave people are oppressors.' But that which is like the first figure is continuous and not invalid, while that which is from the second and third figures is not continuous. For if one of the oppressors is brave, it does not follow that all brave people are oppressors, nor that every yellowish woman is pregnant. And the sign is not, as was thought, that which indicates existence only, so that the premises in whatever figure they are would only be affirmative. Rather, the sign, just as it may be for existence, may be for non-existence, and perhaps it was non-existence of non-existence.
Source pages p. 467

Commentary
This passage defines 'al-ʿalāma' (sign) as a syllogism where both the major premise and the middle term are suppressed (folded). The sign itself can be either necessary (ḍarūriyya) or acceptable (maḥmūda). The author then classifies three types based on what would happen if the major premise were made explicit:

1. **First type**: The middle term is configured as in the first figure, e.g., 'This wood is burnt, so fire has ignited in it.' This type is often particularly called 'dalil' (opinion).

2. **Second type**: The middle term would be more general than both extremes and predicated positively, e.g., 'This woman is yellowish, so she is pregnant.' (Yellowishness is a general sign of pregnancy.)

3. **Third type**: The middle term would be the subject for both extremes and is particular, e.g., 'Al-Hajjaj was brave, so brave people are oppressors.' (This inverts the relationship: from a particular instance to a general rule.)

The author then evaluates validity: Only the first figure yields a continuous (consistent) argument that is not invalid. The second and third figures are not continuous. For example, from 'One oppressor is brave' it does not follow that all brave are oppressors, nor from 'One yellowish woman is pregnant' that all yellowish women are pregnant. Finally, the author corrects a misconception: a sign is not only used to indicate existence (positive statements); it can also indicate non-existence (negatives) or double negation.

Key Terms
العلامة
الحد الأوسط
الشكل الأول
مستمر غير منتقض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which two components are suppressed in a ‘alama argument?
A) Minor premise and conclusion
B) Major premise and middle term
C) Minor premise and middle term
D) Major premise and conclusion

Key Takeaway
A ‘alama is a truncated syllogism; its validity depends on the form of the missing parts.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Take the argument ‘This liquid is intoxicating, therefore it is forbidden.’ Reconstruct the suppressed major premise and identify the middle term.

Three Types of ‘Alama and Their Underlying Figures
Classifies signs into three types based on the underlying syllogistic figure

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify a given ‘alama argument into one of three types based on the relationship of the middle term to the extremes.

Core Idea
Three types of signs arise from the position of the middle term: first-figure, second-figure, or third-figure pattern.

The text presents three examples of signs, each corresponding to a different syllogistic figure if the major premise were made explicit. Understanding this classification helps predict the sign's validity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the first type
The text says the first type has the middle term configured as in the first figure. Example: ‘This wood is burnt, so fire has ignited.’ The middle term ‘burnt’ is subject in the suppressed major and predicate in the minor.
This type is often called ‘dalil’.
2
Identify the second type
The second type: if the major were stated, the middle term would be more general than both extremes and predicated positively. Example: ‘This woman is yellowish, so she is pregnant.’ Here the middle term ‘yellowish’ would be predicate in both premises, indicating second figure.
3
Identify the third type
Third type: if the major were stated, the middle term would be subject for both extremes and is particular. Example: ‘Al-Hajjaj was brave, so brave people are oppressors.’ The middle term ‘brave’ is subject in both premises, indicating third figure.
4
Summarize the classification
Thus, signs can be categorized by whether the underlying syllogism (if spelled out) would be first, second, or third figure.

Validity of Signs: First Figure Prevails
Discusses validity of signs and corrects misconception about sign indicating only existence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate a given ‘alama argument for validity by determining if its underlying syllogism is in the first figure.

Core Idea
Only signs with the first-figure structure yield necessary and continuous conclusions; second and third figures are invalid.

The author rates the validity of signs based on their figure. Only the first figure produces a sign that is ‘continuous and not invalid.’ In contrast, signs from the second and third figures are unreliable. He also corrects the misconception that signs only indicate existence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the criterion for valid signs
The text states: ‘that which is like the first figure is continuous and not invalid.’ The term ‘continuous’ (mustamirr) likely means the inference holds consistently; ‘not invalid’ (ghayr muntaqiḍ) means it is not subject to counterexamples.
2
Examine the counterexamples from second and third figures
The author gives two refutations: from ‘one oppressor is brave’ it does not follow that all brave are oppressors, and from ‘a yellowish woman is pregnant’ it does not follow that all yellowish women are pregnant. These are hasty generalizations.
3
Understand why these are invalid
In the second figure, the middle term is the predicate in both premises, which yields only negative conclusions in valid forms. The examples use affirmative conclusions, making them invalid. Similarly, the third figure requires particular conclusions, not universal.
4
Analyze the correction of the existence-only misconception
The author clarifies that a sign can also indicate non-existence (al-‘adam) or even double negation. So signs are not limited to positive existence claims.

Understanding the 'Alama (Sign Argument) and Its Types
Definition of 'alama and its three subtypes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three types of 'alama based on logical figure and evaluate the reliability of each type.

Core Idea
Only the first-figure 'alama yields certainty; others are unreliable.

In this passage, the author defines the 'alama, a type of argument where both the major premise and the middle term are hidden. He distinguishes three subtypes depending on what the logical form would look like if the major premise were made explicit. This lesson will help you recognize these subtypes and understand why only the first-figure type is continuous and non-contradictory.

Guided Path
1
Identify the hidden elements
In an 'alama, both the major premise and the middle term are implicit. Identify what is hidden in the given example.
The 'alama is an enthymeme; reconstruct the full syllogism to see the structure.
2
Recognize the three subtypes
The author presents three subtypes based on the position of the middle term when the major premise is made explicit: (1) first-figure pattern, (2) second-figure pattern (middle term more general), (3) third-figure pattern (middle term as subject, particular).
Note the examples: burnt wood (first), yellowish woman (second), al-Hajjaj's bravery (third).
3
Assess reliability
Only the first subtype mirrors the first figure and is continuous and non-contradictory. The other subtypes are not reliable because the conclusion does not necessarily follow.
For instance, not all yellowish women are pregnant, and not all brave people are tyrants.
4
Apply the existence/non-existence distinction
The author corrects the misconception that 'alama only indicates existence. It can also indicate non-existence, and premises may be negative.
Example: 'No smoke, so no fire' is a valid 'alama for non-existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما العلامة، فعبارة عما طويت فيه المقدمة الكبرى والحد الأوسط، فيه علامة إما ضرورية أو محمودة متبعة، لكن منه ما لوضوح فيه بالمقدمة الكبرى كأن هيئة الحد الأوسط فيه على نسق الشكل الأول، كقولنا: (هذا الخشب محترق فقد اشتعلت فيه النار) وقد يخص ما مثل هذا النوع كثيراً بالدليل، ومنه ما لو صرح فيه بالمقدمة الكبرى كان الحد الأوسط فيه أعمّ من الطرفين ومحمولاً عليها بالإيجاب كقولنا: (هذه المرأة مصفار فهي حبلى) ومنه ما لو صرح فيه بالمقدمة الكبرى كان الحد الأوسط فيه موضوعاً للطرفين وهو جزئي، كقولنا: (الحجاج كان شجاعاً فالشجعان ظلمة) لكن ما من مثل الشكل الأول ومستمر غير منتقض وما من الشكل الثاني والثالث فغير مستمر، فإنه ليس إذا كان واحد من الظلمة شجاعاً فالظلمة كلهم شجعان ولا أن يكون كل مصفار حبلى، وليس العلامة كما ظن ما يدل بها على الوجود فقط، حتى لا تكون المقدمات ولا في أي شكل كان إلا موجبة، بل العلامة كما تكون للوجود قد تكون للعدم وربما كانت العدم عدم علامة الوجود، وإذ ذاك فقد تكون المقدمات موجبة وسالبة.
As for the 'alama, it is an expression of what the major premise and the middle term are folded in it. In it is a sign, either necessary or praiseworthy and followed. But among it is that which, due to clarity in the major premise, is as if the form of the middle term is in the pattern of the first figure, like our saying: 'This wood is burnt, so fire has caught in it.' And often this type is specifically called dalil. And among it is that which, if the major premise were stated explicitly, the middle term would be more general than the two extremes and predicated on them positively, like our saying: 'This woman is yellowish, so she is pregnant.' And among it is that which, if the major premise were stated explicitly, the middle term would be subject for both extremes and it is particular, like our saying: 'Al-Hajjaj was brave, so the brave are tyrants.' But what is like the first figure is continuous and not contradictory, while what is like the second and third figures is not continuous. For it is not the case that if one of the tyrants is brave, then all tyrants are brave, nor that every yellowish woman is pregnant. And the 'alama is not, as thought, what indicates existence only, so that the premises are not, in any figure, except affirmative. Rather, the 'alama, just as it indicates existence, may indicate non-existence, and perhaps it indicates the non-existence of something which is a sign of existence. And then the premises may be affirmative and negative.
Source pages p. 467

Commentary
This passage defines the 'alama, a type of argument where both the major premise and the middle term are concealed. The 'alama contains a sign that may be necessary or praiseworthy. The author then distinguishes three subtypes based on the logical relationship if the major premise were explicit. The first subtype mirrors the first figure (e.g., burnt wood indicates fire); the author notes this type is often called dalil. The second subtype has the middle term more general and predicated on both extremes (e.g., yellowness as a sign of pregnancy). The third has the middle term as subject for both, particular (e.g., bravery of al-Hajjaj as a sign that all brave are tyrants). The author warns that only the first subtype yields continuous, non-contradictory results; the others are unreliable (e.g., not all yellowish women are pregnant). Finally, the author corrects a misconception that 'alama only indicates existence; it can also indicate non-existence, and premises may be negative.

Key Terms
العلامة (al-'alama): An argument with hidden major premise and middle term, containing a sign (necessary or praiseworthy) that leads to a conclusion; can be of three subtypes based on logical form. [Note: In other works, 'alama may simply mean a sign or symptom; here it is a technical logical argument.]
الحد الأوسط (al-hadd al-awsat): The middle term in a syllogism, which is hidden in the 'alama. [Note: Standard term in Aristotelian logic; stable.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which type of 'alama is continuous and non-contradictory?
A) The type that mirrors the first figure
B) The type with the middle term more general than both extremes
C) The type with the middle term as subject for both extremes
D) All types are equally continuous

Key Takeaway
The 'alama is a powerful but potentially deceptive argument; only those that mirror the first figure are certain, and the 'alama can indicate both existence and non-existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Classify each of the following 'alama into one of the three subtypes and state whether it is reliable: (a) 'This person is fasting, so he is Muslim.' (b) 'The sun has risen, so it is daytime.' (c) 'He is a scholar, so all scholars are knowledgeable.'
?
Construct a valid 'alama of the first-figure type on a topic from fiqh, e.g., using the sign of intoxication to conclude prohibition.
